\section{TECT Master Context: Math 0-7 Summary}
\subsection{Topological Energy Condensate Theory}

\subsubsection{Research Goal}

The goal of Topological Energy Condensate Theory (TECT) is to derive gravity, gauge fields, and Standard Model fermions from a single underlying energy condensate.

The fundamental hypothesis is that the universe is a condensed energy medium whose equilibrium configuration forms a body-centered cubic (BCC) energy structure.

Particles and gauge fields emerge as collective excitations and topological defects of this condensate.

\[
\text{Energy condensate}
\rightarrow
\text{BCC structure}
\rightarrow
\text{Elastic continuum}
\rightarrow
\text{Gauge fields and fermions}
\]

The ultimate target is the derivation of the Standard Model gauge group

\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)
\]

together with gravity as emergent phenomena.

\subsection{Fundamental Condensate Structure}

The condensate is assumed to form a BCC reciprocal star.

The BCC reciprocal shell is

\[
S_{BCC}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{(\pm1,\pm1,0),(\pm1,0,\pm1),(0,\pm1,\pm1)\}
\]

This shell contains

\[
|S_{BCC}|=12
\]

wavevectors.

The quartic interaction closure coefficient derived from the BCC amplitude expansion is

\[
C_4 = 540
\]

which plays a central role in the BCC structure selection theorem.

\subsection{Emergent Elastic Continuum}

At long wavelengths the condensate behaves as an elastic medium.

The displacement field

\[
u_i(x)
\]

produces strain

\[
\epsilon_{ij} = \frac12(\partial_i u_j + \partial_j u_i)
\]

and elastic free energy

\[
F = \int d^3x
\left(
C_{11}\epsilon_{ii}^2
+
C_{12}\epsilon_{ii}\epsilon_{jj}
+
2C_{44}\epsilon_{ij}^2
\right)
\]

The low-energy sector is dominated by transverse modes.

These modes possess an internal rotation symmetry

\[
SO(2)
\]

which becomes the origin of the emergent electromagnetic gauge symmetry.

\subsection{Emergent Electromagnetism}

The transverse polarization rotation

\[
SO(2)
\]

is promoted to a local symmetry

\[
SO(2) \rightarrow U(1)
\]

producing a gauge connection

\[
A_\mu
\]

with curvature

\[
F_{\mu\nu}
\]

The effective infrared action becomes Maxwell theory

\[
S = -\frac14\int F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}
\]

Lorentz invariance emerges from the isotropy of the infrared elastic sector.

\subsection{Internal Geometry}

Topological defects of the condensate possess an internal moduli space

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2
\]

which can be written as the coset

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2 \simeq
\frac{SU(3)}{SU(2)\times U(1)}
\]

This structure provides the geometric origin of the color gauge group.

\subsection{Topological Charge}

Defects are classified by

\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2) = \mathbb{Z}
\]

Let

\[
B \in \mathbb{Z}
\]

be the defect topological charge.

This quantity is interpreted as baryon number.

\subsection{Wess-Zumino-Witten Phase}

The defect sector carries a WZW phase

\[
\exp(i\pi B)
\]

which implies

\[
\exp(i\pi B) = (-1)^B
\]

Thus defects with odd charge exhibit fermionic statistics.

\subsection{Fermion Zero Modes}

Fermions arise as defect-bound zero modes of a twisted Dirac operator.

The internal space is

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2
\]

which admits spin$^c$ structures.

The twisted chiral Dirac operator is

\[
D_+^{(L,E)}
\]

The Atiyah-Singer index theorem gives

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}
\]

For the primitive twisting

\[
(q,m)=(1,1)
\]

one obtains

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1
\]

which implies

\[
n_L-n_R=1
\]

This establishes topological chirality protection.

\subsection{Mirror Sector}

Index theory does not eliminate mirror pairs.

One may still have

\[
(n_L,n_R)=(I+r,r)
\]

Mirror removal requires a dynamical lifting operator

\[
M =
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger \\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\]

If

\[
\mu \neq 0
\]

all mirror pairs acquire nonzero energy and the spectrum becomes

\[
n_L = I, \quad n_R=0
\]

\subsection{Dirac Operator Emergence}

Consider the linearized condensate equation

\[
i\partial_t\Psi = \mathcal{L}(-i\nabla)\Psi
\]

Assume an isolated valley crossing at momentum

\[
k_*
\]

The projected Hamiltonian near the valley is

\[
h_+(p)=\sum_i p_i V_i
\]

Since this is a $2\times2$ Hermitian matrix,

\[
h_+(p)
=
a_0(p)\mathbf1_2
+
\sum_{a=1}^3 a_a(p)\sigma^a
\]

If the traceless coefficient matrix is nondegenerate, the Hamiltonian reduces to

\[
h_+(p)\sim p_i\sigma^i
\]

which is a Weyl fermion.

\subsection{Dirac Doubling}

If an opposite valley exists at

\[
-k_*
\]

satisfying

\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p)
\]

then the doubled Hamiltonian becomes

\[
H =
\begin{pmatrix}
p_i\sigma^i & 0 \\
0 & -p_i\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}
\]

This is the massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

\subsection{BCC Shell Valley Structure}

Choose one reciprocal pair

\[
\pm G_*
\]

from the BCC shell.

Decompose momentum as

\[
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2
\]

The projected Hamiltonian takes the form

\[
h_+(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf1
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1\sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2\sigma^2
\]

If

\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0
\]

this is Weyl.

Opposite valley doubling gives Dirac.

\subsection{Current Proven Results}

The following results are rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item BCC condensate selection theorem
\item Elastic continuum emergence
\item U(1) gauge emergence
\item Maxwell dynamics
\item Lorentz symmetry emergence
\item CP$^2$ internal geometry
\item SU(3) Yang-Mills emergence
\item Chirality index theorem
\item Mirror lifting theorem
\item Weyl valley theorem
\item Dirac emergence from valley doubling
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Open Problems}

Remaining frontier problems are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Derive the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE
\item Prove the existence of BCC shell valleys
\item Compute velocity coefficients
\[
v_\parallel, v_1, v_2
\]
and verify nondegeneracy
\item Derive the SU(2) electroweak sector
\item Verify anomaly cancellation
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Current Research Frontier}

The fermion emergence chain currently reads

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2
\rightarrow
\text{Chiral Index}
\rightarrow
\text{Mirror Lifting}
\rightarrow
\text{Weyl Valleys}
\rightarrow
\text{Dirac Fermion}
\]

The remaining goal is the full recovery of

\[
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1)
\]

from the condensate geometry.

\section{Emergent \(SU(2)\) Gauge Structure from the \( \mathbb{CP}^2 \) Internal Geometry}

\subsection{Statement of the result}

We show that the internal geometry
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2 \simeq \frac{SU(3)}{U(2)}
\]
naturally carries a rank-two complex bundle whose local orthonormal frame redundancy is
\(U(2)\).
Consequently, the corresponding bundle connection decomposes canonically into
\[
\mathfrak{u}(2) \simeq \mathfrak{su}(2)\oplus \mathfrak{u}(1),
\]
which yields an emergent \(SU(2)\) gauge connection together with an additional \(U(1)\)
connection.

Thus the internal \( \mathbb{CP}^2 \) geometry does not merely encode an \(SU(3)\) tangent-algebra
structure; it also contains a distinguished local \(U(2)\) frame bundle, and therefore an
electroweak-type precursor
\[
U(2)\cong \frac{SU(2)\times U(1)}{\mathbb Z_2}.
\]

\subsection{Normalized order parameter and projective reduction}

Let
\[
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z = 1.
\]
The physical internal state is invariant under the local phase identification
\[
z(x)\sim e^{i\alpha(x)} z(x).
\]
Hence the physical configuration space is the complex projective space
\[
[z(x)] \in \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

Equivalently, define the rank-one Hermitian projector
\[
P = z z^\dagger,
\qquad
P^2=P,
\qquad
P^\dagger=P,
\qquad
\mathrm{Tr}\,P = 1.
\]
Then \(P\) is phase-invariant and therefore represents a point of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).

\subsection{The tautological line and the orthogonal rank-two complement}

At each point \([z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2\), define the tautological complex line
\[
L_{[z]} := \mathrm{span}_{\mathbb C}\{z\}\subset \mathbb C^3.
\]
Its Hermitian orthogonal complement is
\[
Q_{[z]} := L_{[z]}^\perp
=
\left\{
v\in \mathbb C^3 \mid z^\dagger v = 0
\right\}.
\]
Since \(\dim_{\mathbb C}\mathbb C^3=3\) and \(\dim_{\mathbb C}L=1\), one has
\[
\dim_{\mathbb C} Q = 2.
\]
Thus \(Q\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) is a rank-two complex vector bundle.

The corresponding orthogonal projector onto \(Q\) is
\[
\Pi := \mathbf 1_3 - P = \mathbf 1_3 - z z^\dagger.
\]
Indeed,
\[
\Pi^2 = \Pi,
\qquad
\Pi^\dagger = \Pi,
\qquad
\Pi z = 0.
\]

\subsection{Local orthonormal frames and the \(U(2)\) redundancy}

Choose locally an orthonormal frame of \(Q\),
\[
e(x)=\bigl(e_1(x),e_2(x)\bigr),
\]
satisfying
\[
z^\dagger e_a = 0,
\qquad
e_a^\dagger e_b = \delta_{ab},
\qquad
a,b=1,2.
\]
Then
\[
\Pi = \sum_{a=1}^2 e_a e_a^\dagger.
\]

Any other local orthonormal frame \(\tilde e\) of the same bundle \(Q\) is related to \(e\) by
\[
\tilde e = e\,U,
\qquad
U(x)\in U(2).
\]
Indeed,
\[
\tilde e^\dagger \tilde e
=
U^\dagger e^\dagger e U
=
U^\dagger U
=
\mathbf 1_2.
\]
Since
\[
\sum_{a=1}^2 \tilde e_a \tilde e_a^\dagger
=
e U U^\dagger e^\dagger
=
e e^\dagger
=
\Pi,
\]
the projector \(\Pi\), and hence the physical point of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), is unchanged.

Therefore the local frame choice on \(Q\) has an exact \(U(2)\) redundancy.
This is the precise geometric origin of the emergent gauge structure.

\subsection{Emergent \(U(2)\) connection}

Define the local bundle connection by
\[
\mathcal A_\mu := - i\, e^\dagger \partial_\mu e.
\]
Because \(e^\dagger e = \mathbf 1_2\), differentiation gives
\[
(\partial_\mu e^\dagger)e + e^\dagger (\partial_\mu e)=0,
\]
hence
\[
\mathcal A_\mu^\dagger
=
(+i\, \partial_\mu e^\dagger e)
=
(-i\, e^\dagger \partial_\mu e)
=
\mathcal A_\mu.
\]
So \(\mathcal A_\mu\) is Hermitian and takes values in \(\mathfrak{u}(2)\).

Under a local frame change
\[
e \mapsto eU(x),
\qquad
U(x)\in U(2),
\]
we obtain
\[
\mathcal A_\mu
\mapsto
\mathcal A_\mu'
=
- i (eU)^\dagger \partial_\mu(eU)
=
U^\dagger \mathcal A_\mu U - i U^\dagger \partial_\mu U.
\]
Thus \(\mathcal A_\mu\) transforms exactly as a \(U(2)\) gauge connection.

\subsection{Canonical decomposition into \(SU(2)\) and \(U(1)\)}

Every \(\mathfrak{u}(2)\)-valued Hermitian matrix admits the decomposition
\[
\mathcal A_\mu
=
W_\mu + \frac{1}{2} B_\mu\, \mathbf 1_2,
\]
where
\[
W_\mu \in \mathfrak{su}(2),
\qquad
\mathrm{Tr}\,W_\mu = 0,
\]
and
\[
B_\mu := \mathrm{Tr}\,\mathcal A_\mu.
\]
Expanding the traceless part in Pauli matrices,
\[
W_\mu = \frac{1}{2} W_\mu^a \sigma^a,
\qquad
a=1,2,3.
\]

Now let
\[
U(x)=e^{i\beta(x)/2} S(x),
\qquad
S(x)\in SU(2).
\]
Then the transformation law splits into

\[
W_\mu \mapsto S^\dagger W_\mu S - i S^\dagger \partial_\mu S,
\]
and
\[
B_\mu \mapsto B_\mu + \partial_\mu \beta.
\]
Hence \(W_\mu\) is an \(SU(2)\) gauge connection and \(B_\mu\) is a \(U(1)\) gauge connection.

Therefore the local frame redundancy of the rank-two bundle \(Q\) yields the gauge structure
\[
U(2)\cong \frac{SU(2)\times U(1)}{\mathbb Z_2}.
\]

\subsection{Coset derivation from \(SU(3)/U(2)\)}

The same result may be derived from the coset structure directly.
Choose a local \(SU(3)\) matrix
\[
g(x)=\bigl(z(x), e_1(x), e_2(x)\bigr)\in SU(3),
\]
whose first column is \(z\) and whose remaining columns form an orthonormal basis of \(Q\).
Then the Maurer--Cartan form is
\[
\Omega_\mu := g^\dagger \partial_\mu g \in \mathfrak{su}(3).
\]
Block-decompose it as
\[
\Omega_\mu
=
\begin{pmatrix}
i a_\mu & - \beta_\mu^\dagger \\
\beta_\mu & i \mathcal A_\mu
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with \(a_\mu\in \mathbb R\), \(\beta_\mu\in \mathbb C^2\), and \(\mathcal A_\mu\in \mathfrak{u}(2)\),
subject to
\[
a_\mu + \mathrm{Tr}\,\mathcal A_\mu = 0
\]
because \(\Omega_\mu\in \mathfrak{su}(3)\) is traceless.

The right action of the stabilizer
\[
h(x)=
\begin{pmatrix}
e^{i\alpha(x)} & 0 \\
0 & U(x)
\end{pmatrix}
\in U(1)\times U(2)
\]
acts as
\[
g(x)\mapsto g(x) h(x),
\]
and induces
\[
\mathcal A_\mu \mapsto U^\dagger \mathcal A_\mu U - i U^\dagger \partial_\mu U,
\]
while
\[
\beta_\mu \mapsto U^\dagger \beta_\mu\, e^{i\alpha}.
\]
Thus the composite connection is precisely the stabilizer connection of the coset
\(SU(3)/U(2)\).

\subsection{Covariant derivative on \(Q\)-doublets}

Let \(\chi(x)\in \mathbb C^2\) be a local section of the rank-two bundle \(Q\), expressed in the frame \(e\).
Under frame changes,
\[
\chi \mapsto U^{-1}\chi.
\]
Then the covariant derivative
\[
D_\mu \chi := \partial_\mu \chi - i \mathcal A_\mu \chi
\]
transforms covariantly:
\[
D_\mu \chi \mapsto U^{-1} D_\mu \chi.
\]

After the decomposition
\[
\mathcal A_\mu = \frac{1}{2} W_\mu^a \sigma^a + \frac{1}{2} B_\mu \mathbf 1_2,
\]
we obtain
\[
D_\mu \chi
=
\left(
\partial_\mu
- \frac{i}{2} W_\mu^a \sigma^a
- \frac{i}{2} B_\mu
\right)\chi.
\]
This is exactly the standard form of a doublet-valued covariant derivative under
\(SU(2)\times U(1)\).

\subsection{Projector form of the sigma model and bundle geometry}

The \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) nonlinear sigma model may be written as
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathbb{CP}^2}
=
\frac{\rho}{2}\,\mathrm{Tr}(\partial_\mu P\, \partial^\mu P).
\]
Since
\[
P = z z^\dagger,
\qquad
\Pi = \mathbf 1 - P,
\]
one finds
\[
\partial_\mu P
=
(\Pi\,\partial_\mu z) z^\dagger
+
z (\partial_\mu z^\dagger \Pi).
\]
Thus only derivatives orthogonal to the tautological line contribute.
Equivalently, the dynamics naturally lives on the quotient bundle data
\[
L \oplus Q = \underline{\mathbb C}^3.
\]
This is the geometric reason that the stabilizer connection \( \mathcal A_\mu \in \mathfrak{u}(2)\)
appears canonically in the low-energy description.

\subsection{Curvature and field strengths}

The \(U(2)\) curvature is
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu \mathcal A_\nu
-
\partial_\nu \mathcal A_\mu
-
i[\mathcal A_\mu,\mathcal A_\nu].
\]
Decomposing again,
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}
=
F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}
+
\frac{1}{2} G_{\mu\nu}\,\mathbf 1_2,
\]
where
\[
F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}
=
\frac{1}{2} F_{\mu\nu}^a \sigma^a,
\qquad
F_{\mu\nu}^a
=
\partial_\mu W_\nu^a - \partial_\nu W_\mu^a
+ \epsilon^{abc} W_\mu^b W_\nu^c,
\]
and
\[
G_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu B_\nu - \partial_\nu B_\mu.
\]
Therefore the nonabelian \(SU(2)\) curvature and the abelian \(U(1)\) curvature both arise
directly from the same geometric \(U(2)\) connection.

\subsection{Infrared gauge dynamics}

Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the low-energy theory is local,
\item the \(Q\)-frame redundancy is exact,
\item a derivative expansion is valid,
\item no explicit \(U(2)\)-breaking operator survives in the infrared.
\end{enumerate}

Then the leading gauge-field self-action must be built from the curvatures:
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{gauge}}
=
-\frac{1}{4 g_2^2} F_{\mu\nu}^a F^{a\,\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4 g_1^2} G_{\mu\nu} G^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots.
\]
Mass terms of the form
\[
W_\mu^a W^{a\mu},
\qquad
B_\mu B^\mu
\]
are forbidden by exact local frame redundancy.

Thus the \( \mathbb{CP}^2 \) internal geometry implies, at the structural infrared level,
an emergent \(SU(2)\times U(1)\) gauge sector.

\subsection{What is proven and what remains open}

What is established here is the following theorem:

\paragraph{Geometric \(SU(2)\) emergence theorem.}
If the TECT internal low-energy sector is described by a normalized
\(z\in \mathbb C^3\) modulo phase, so that the physical internal manifold is
\(\mathbb{CP}^2\), then the orthogonal rank-two bundle
\[
Q = (\mathbb C z)^\perp
\]
has local orthonormal frame redundancy \(U(2)\).
Its bundle connection decomposes canonically into
\[
\mathfrak{u}(2)=\mathfrak{su}(2)\oplus \mathfrak{u}(1),
\]
thereby producing an emergent \(SU(2)\) gauge connection \(W_\mu\) and an emergent
\(U(1)\) gauge connection \(B_\mu\).

What is not yet established is the full Standard Model identification, namely:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact hypercharge embedding,
\item chiral fermion representation assignments,
\item electroweak symmetry breaking,
\item anomaly cancellation,
\item the Weinberg-angle prediction.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the present derivation closes the geometric origin of an electroweak-type
\(U(2)\) gauge bundle, but not yet the complete Standard Model electroweak sector.

\section{Final Summary}

Starting from the TECT internal order parameter
\[
z\in \mathbb C^3, \qquad z^\dagger z =1,
\]
with the physical phase identification \(z\sim e^{i\alpha}z\), the low-energy internal manifold is
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2 \simeq SU(3)/U(2).
\]

At each point \([z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2\), the orthogonal complement
\[
Q_{[z]} = \{v\in \mathbb C^3 \mid z^\dagger v = 0\}
\]
is a complex rank-two vector space. Hence \(Q\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) is a rank-two Hermitian bundle.

A local orthonormal frame \(e=(e_1,e_2)\) of \(Q\) is defined only up to
\[
e \mapsto e\,U(x), \qquad U(x)\in U(2),
\]
so the local frame redundancy is exactly \(U(2)\).

The associated bundle connection
\[
\mathcal A_\mu = -i e^\dagger \partial_\mu e
\]
transforms as a \(U(2)\) gauge field and admits the canonical decomposition
\[
\mathcal A_\mu
=
\frac{1}{2}W_\mu^a \sigma^a
+
\frac{1}{2}B_\mu \mathbf 1_2,
\]
which yields an emergent \(SU(2)\) connection \(W_\mu\) and an emergent \(U(1)\) connection \(B_\mu\).

Thus the \( \mathbb{CP}^2 \) internal geometry contains, in a mathematically precise sense,
an electroweak-type gauge precursor
\[
U(2)\cong \frac{SU(2)\times U(1)}{\mathbb Z_2}.
\]

Under locality, exact frame redundancy, and derivative expansion, the leading infrared gauge dynamics is
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{gauge}}
=
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2}F_{\mu\nu}^aF^{a\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4g_1^2}G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\mathbb{CP}^2 \text{ internal geometry}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
Q \text{ rank-two bundle}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(2) \text{ frame redundancy}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SU(2)\times U(1) \text{ gauge precursor}.
}
\]

This closes the geometric emergence of the \(SU(2)\) connection at the bundle level,
but the full Standard Model electroweak reconstruction remains an open problem.

\section{Induced Yang--Mills Dynamics for the Composite \(U(2)\) Connection}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The internal manifold is \( \mathbb{CP}^2 \), and the orthogonal complement
\[
Q = (\mathbb C z)^\perp
\]
is a rank-two Hermitian bundle.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A2: A local orthonormal frame \(e=(e_1,e_2)\) of \(Q\) defines a composite connection
\[
\mathcal A_\mu = -i e^\dagger \partial_\mu e \in \mathfrak u(2),
\]
transforming as a \(U(2)\) gauge connection.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A3: The infrared effective action is local and admits a derivative expansion.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption}}
\]

\item A4: There exists a dynamical field \(\chi\) transforming in the fundamental of the local
\(U(2)\) frame bundle.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption}}
\]

\item A5: Integrating out \(\chi\) produces a gauge-invariant effective action for
\(\mathcal A_\mu\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once A4 is assumed}}
\]

\item A6: The induced kinetic coefficient is nonzero and positive.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in explicit matter models, not yet from full TECT}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We distinguish three logically different statements:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Geometric statement:} \( \mathcal A_\mu \) is a \(U(2)\) connection.
\item \textbf{Kinematic EFT statement:} if a local gauge-invariant effective action exists,
its leading self-interaction is built from the curvature.
\item \textbf{Dynamical statement:} a nonzero Yang--Mills kinetic term is actually induced
by integrating out charged low-energy degrees of freedom.
\end{enumerate}

Only the first is unconditional.  
The second is conditional on locality and exact gauge redundancy.  
The third is conditional on the existence of dynamical \(Q\)-charged modes.

\subsection{Composite \(U(2)\) connection and curvature}

Let \(e=(e_1,e_2)\) be a local orthonormal frame of the rank-two bundle \(Q\).
Then
\[
\mathcal A_\mu := -i e^\dagger \partial_\mu e
\]
is Hermitian and transforms under a local frame change \(e\mapsto eU(x)\),
\(U(x)\in U(2)\), as
\[
\mathcal A_\mu \mapsto U^\dagger \mathcal A_\mu U - i U^\dagger \partial_\mu U.
\]
Its curvature is
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu \mathcal A_\nu
-
\partial_\nu \mathcal A_\mu
-
i[\mathcal A_\mu,\mathcal A_\nu].
\]
Under the same gauge transformation,
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mapsto U^\dagger \mathcal F_{\mu\nu} U.
\]

Decompose
\[
\mathcal A_\mu
=
W_\mu + \frac12 B_\mu \mathbf 1_2,
\qquad
W_\mu=\frac12 W_\mu^a \sigma^a\in \mathfrak{su}(2),
\]
with
\[
B_\mu = \mathrm{Tr}\,\mathcal A_\mu.
\]
Then
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}
=
F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}
+
\frac12 G_{\mu\nu}\mathbf 1_2,
\]
where
\[
F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}
=
\partial_\mu W_\nu-\partial_\nu W_\mu-i[W_\mu,W_\nu],
\]
and
\[
G_{\mu\nu}=\partial_\mu B_\nu-\partial_\nu B_\mu.
\]

\subsection{What gauge invariance alone implies}

Assume only that the infrared effective action for \(\mathcal A_\mu\) is local and invariant
under the exact local \(U(2)\) frame redundancy. Then:

\paragraph{Claim 1.}
A mass term
\[
\int d^4x\, \mathrm{Tr}(\mathcal A_\mu \mathcal A^\mu)
\]
is not gauge invariant, hence is forbidden.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Under
\[
\mathcal A_\mu \mapsto U^\dagger \mathcal A_\mu U - i U^\dagger \partial_\mu U,
\]
the quantity \(\mathrm{Tr}(\mathcal A_\mu \mathcal A^\mu)\) picks up inhomogeneous derivative
terms involving \(U^\dagger \partial_\mu U\), and therefore is not invariant.
Thus an exact local \(U(2)\) redundancy forbids such a term.
\(\square\)

\paragraph{Claim 2.}
The leading local gauge-invariant self-interaction of \(\mathcal A_\mu\) begins with
curvature invariants.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Gauge invariants without derivatives built solely from \(\mathcal A_\mu\) do not exist because
\(\mathcal A_\mu\) is not tensorial. The first tensorial object is \(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\),
which transforms covariantly. Therefore the first local gauge-invariant terms are built from
\(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\), e.g.
\[
\mathrm{Tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}),
\qquad
(\mathrm{Tr}\,\mathcal F_{\mu\nu})(\mathrm{Tr}\,\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}).
\]
\(\square\)

Thus, \emph{if} a local self-action exists, then to leading order it must take the form
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{gauge}}
=
-\frac{c_2}{2}\,\mathrm{Tr}(F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}F^{(W)\mu\nu})
-\frac{c_1}{4}\,G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots
\]
for some coefficients \(c_1,c_2\).

This conclusion is kinematic and does not yet show that \(c_1,c_2\neq 0\).

\subsection{Conditional dynamical theorem from integrating out \(Q\)-doublets}

Assume now the existence of a dynamical field \(\chi\) transforming in the fundamental
representation of the local \(U(2)\) frame bundle:
\[
\chi(x)\mapsto U^{-1}(x)\chi(x).
\]
Its covariant derivative is
\[
D_\mu \chi = \partial_\mu \chi - i \mathcal A_\mu \chi.
\]

We present two explicit model classes.

\subsubsection{Model A: massive complex scalar doublet}

Let \(\chi\) be a complex scalar doublet with Euclidean action
\[
S_\chi^{(E)}
=
\int d^4x\,
\left[
(D_\mu \chi)^\dagger (D_\mu \chi)
+
M^2 \chi^\dagger \chi
\right].
\]
Integrating out \(\chi\) gives
\[
Z[\mathcal A]
=
\int \mathcal D\chi\,\mathcal D\chi^\dagger\, e^{-S_\chi^{(E)}}
=
\Bigl[\det(-D^2+M^2)\Bigr]^{-1},
\]
hence
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}^{(E)}[\mathcal A]
=
-\log Z[\mathcal A]
=
\mathrm{Tr}\log(-D^2+M^2).
\]

Using the proper-time representation,
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}^{(E)}
=
-\int_0^\infty \frac{ds}{s}\, e^{-sM^2}\,
\mathrm{Tr}\,e^{-s(-D^2)}.
\]
For a Laplace-type operator on flat four-dimensional spacetime, the heat-kernel expansion is
\[
\mathrm{Tr}\,e^{-s(-D^2)}
\sim
\frac{1}{(4\pi s)^2}
\int d^4x\,
\mathrm{tr}
\left[
a_0 + s a_1 + s^2 a_2 + \cdots
\right].
\]
For a pure gauge connection background,
\[
a_2 = \frac{1}{12}\,\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}.
\]
Therefore,
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}^{(E)}
\supset
-\frac{1}{12(4\pi)^2}
\left(
\int_0^\infty \frac{ds}{s}e^{-sM^2}
\right)
\int d^4x\, \mathrm{tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}).
\]
After renormalization, this yields a local term of the form
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}^{(E)}
\supset
\kappa_s \int d^4x\, \mathrm{tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}),
\]
with a nonzero coefficient \(\kappa_s\) depending on the matter content and renormalization scale.

Thus a massive scalar \(Q\)-doublet induces a Yang--Mills kinetic term.

\subsubsection{Model B: massive Dirac doublet}

Let \(\psi\) be a Dirac fermion in the fundamental of \(U(2)\), with Euclidean action
\[
S_\psi^{(E)}
=
\int d^4x\,
\bar\psi (\gamma^\mu D_\mu + M)\psi.
\]
Then
\[
Z[\mathcal A]
=
\det(\gamma^\mu D_\mu + M),
\qquad
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}^{(E)}
=
-\log \det(\gamma^\mu D_\mu + M).
\]
Squaring the Dirac operator,
\[
(\gamma^\mu D_\mu + M)(-\gamma^\nu D_\nu + M)
=
-D^2 + M^2 + \frac12 \sigma^{\mu\nu}\mathcal F_{\mu\nu},
\]
so the heat-kernel again generates
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}^{(E)}
\supset
\kappa_f \int d^4x\, \mathrm{tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}),
\]
with nonzero \(\kappa_f\).

Hence massive \(Q\)-charged fermions also induce a Yang--Mills kinetic term.

\subsection{Decomposition into \(SU(2)\) and \(U(1)\)}

Since
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}
=
F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}+\frac12 G_{\mu\nu}\mathbf 1_2,
\]
we have
\[
\mathrm{tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu})
=
\mathrm{tr}(F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}F^{(W)\mu\nu})
+
\frac12 G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}.
\]
Indeed, the mixed term vanishes because
\[
\mathrm{tr}(F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)})=0.
\]
Using
\[
F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}=\frac12 F_{\mu\nu}^a \sigma^a,
\qquad
\mathrm{tr}(\sigma^a\sigma^b)=2\delta^{ab},
\]
we find
\[
\mathrm{tr}(F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}F^{(W)\mu\nu})
=
\frac12 F_{\mu\nu}^a F^{a\mu\nu}.
\]
Therefore the induced term splits as
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}
\supset
\int d^4x
\left[
\frac{\kappa}{2}F_{\mu\nu}^aF^{a\mu\nu}
+
\frac{\kappa}{2}G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}
\right]
\]
up to normalization conventions and possible representation-dependent coefficients.
After canonically normalizing fields, this becomes
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{gauge}}
=
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2}F_{\mu\nu}^aF^{a\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4g_1^2}G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots.
\]

\subsection{Precise logical status of the theorem}

We may now state the main result carefully.

\paragraph{Conditional induced-gauge theorem.}
Suppose that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT internal sector contains the rank-two bundle \(Q\to\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item the corresponding local orthonormal frame redundancy \(U(2)\) is exact,
\item there exist dynamical gapped fields transforming in the fundamental of \(Q\),
\item these fields can be integrated out in a local derivative expansion.
\end{enumerate}
Then the infrared effective action for the composite connection \(\mathcal A_\mu\)
contains a gauge-invariant Yang--Mills term
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[\mathcal A]
\supset
\int d^4x
\left[
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2}F_{\mu\nu}^aF^{a\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4g_1^2}G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}
\right].
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By assumptions (1)--(2), \(\mathcal A_\mu\) is a legitimate \(U(2)\) gauge connection.
By assumption (3), there exist matter operators with covariant derivative \(D_\mu\).
By assumption (4), integrating them out yields a local gauge-invariant effective action.
Gauge invariance forbids mass terms for \(\mathcal A_\mu\), while locality and derivative expansion
imply that the leading self-interaction is built from \(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\).
The explicit heat-kernel expansion of the scalar or Dirac determinant produces a nonzero
\(\mathrm{tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu})\) term.
Decomposition into traceless and trace pieces yields the \(SU(2)\) and \(U(1)\) kinetic terms.
\(\square\)

\subsection{What this does \emph{not} prove}

This theorem does not yet prove:

\begin{enumerate}
\item that the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE necessarily contains such a \(Q\)-charged field,
\item that the induced coefficients \(g_2,g_1\) match the Standard Model,
\item that the \(U(1)\) factor is precisely hypercharge,
\item that the \(SU(2)\) factor acts chirally only on left-handed fermions,
\item that anomaly cancellation holds.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the derivation closes the \emph{structural mechanism} for induced \(SU(2)\times U(1)\)
gauge dynamics, but not yet the complete electroweak sector.

\section{Final Summary}

The composite connection
\[
\mathcal A_\mu = -i e^\dagger \partial_\mu e \in \mathfrak u(2)
\]
arises rigorously from the local orthonormal frame redundancy of the rank-two bundle
\[
Q=(\mathbb C z)^\perp \to \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

From gauge invariance alone, one can prove:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a local mass term for \(\mathcal A_\mu\) is forbidden,
\item the leading local self-interaction must be built from the curvature
\[
\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu \mathcal A_\nu
-
\partial_\nu \mathcal A_\mu
-
i[\mathcal A_\mu,\mathcal A_\nu].
\]
\end{enumerate}

Therefore any local infrared gauge-invariant effective action must begin as
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
-\frac{c_2}{2}\,\mathrm{Tr}(F_{\mu\nu}^{(W)}F^{(W)\mu\nu})
-\frac{c_1}{4}\,G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}
+\cdots.
\]

However, geometry alone does \emph{not} prove that \(c_1,c_2\neq 0\).

A nonzero Yang--Mills kinetic term is obtained conditionally if there exist dynamical fields
\(\chi\) or \(\psi\) transforming in the fundamental representation of the \(Q\)-bundle and
if these fields may be integrated out in a local derivative expansion.
In that case the one-loop determinant generates
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[\mathcal A]
\supset
\kappa \int d^4x\, \mathrm{tr}(\mathcal F_{\mu\nu}\mathcal F^{\mu\nu}),
\]
which decomposes into
\[
-\frac{1}{4g_2^2}F_{\mu\nu}^aF^{a\mu\nu}
-\frac{1}{4g_1^2}G_{\mu\nu}G^{\mu\nu}.
\]

Hence the mathematically honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Geometric }U(2)\text{ emergence is proved;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Yang--Mills dynamics is conditionally induced once }Q\text{-charged matter exists.}
}
\]

What remains open is the full microscopic TECT derivation of the required charged modes,
their chirality structure, hypercharge embedding, and anomaly cancellation.

%%%%%%%

\section{Do \(Q\)-Charged Matter Modes Exist? A Precise Bundle-Theoretic Audit}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The internal order parameter is a normalized complex vector
\[
z\in \mathbb C^3,\qquad z^\dagger z=1,
\]
modulo local phase, so that the physical internal manifold is
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A2: The orthogonal complement
\[
Q=(\mathbb C z)^\perp
\]
is a rank-two Hermitian vector bundle over \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), with local orthonormal frame
redundancy \(U(2)\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A3: Any local section of \(Q\) transforms in the fundamental representation of the local
\(U(2)\) frame bundle.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in current framework}}
\]

\item A4: Physical defect-bound, valley, or collective modes of TECT are realized as sections of
\(Q\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption}}
\]

\item A5: In adiabatic or restricted defect constructions, the fluctuation space around a defect
contains a natural \(Q\)-valued sector.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in restricted constructions}}
\]

\item A6: The relevant zero modes exist as kernels of a linearized operator or twisted Dirac-type
operator.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in model operators, not yet from full TECT PDE}}
\]

\item A7: These modes are exactly the Standard Model chiral electroweak doublets.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We aim to separate the following logically distinct statements:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Bundle-theoretic statement:}
sections of \(Q\) transform as \(U(2)\) fundamentals.

\item \textbf{Restricted physical statement:}
for certain defect or collective-coordinate constructions, a natural fluctuation sector is
\(Q\)-valued.

\item \textbf{Full microscopic TECT statement:}
the actual low-energy matter modes of the condensate necessarily include such \(Q\)-valued
fields.

\item \textbf{Standard Model identification:}
those \(Q\)-valued modes coincide with chiral electroweak doublets.
\end{enumerate}

Only the first is fully unconditional at present.

\subsection{Rank-two bundle \(Q\) and local sections}

Let
\[
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,\qquad z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad z\sim e^{i\alpha}z.
\]
The physical internal configuration is the projective class \([z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2\).

Define the tautological complex line
\[
L_{[z]}=\mathbb C z
\]
and the orthogonal complement
\[
Q_{[z]} = (\mathbb Cz)^\perp = \{v\in \mathbb C^3\mid z^\dagger v=0\}.
\]
Then \(Q\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) is a rank-two complex Hermitian bundle.

Choose a local orthonormal frame
\[
e=(e_1,e_2),
\qquad
z^\dagger e_a=0,
\qquad
e_a^\dagger e_b=\delta_{ab}.
\]
Any local section \(s(x)\in Q_{[z(x)]}\) can be expanded uniquely as
\[
s(x)=e(x)\,\chi(x),
\qquad
\chi(x)\in \mathbb C^2.
\]
Under a local frame change
\[
e(x)\mapsto e(x)U(x),
\qquad
U(x)\in U(2),
\]
the same geometric section \(s\) must remain invariant:
\[
s=e\chi=(eU)\chi'.
\]
Therefore
\[
\chi' = U^{-1}\chi.
\]
Hence the coefficient field \(\chi\) transforms in the fundamental representation of the local
\(U(2)\) frame bundle.

This proves the following unconditional theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Bundle-fundamental theorem).}
Let \(Q\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) be the rank-two orthogonal complement bundle of the tautological line.
Then any local section of \(Q\), expressed in a local orthonormal frame, transforms in the
fundamental representation of the local \(U(2)\) frame redundancy.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The proof is exactly the computation above:
\[
s=e\chi=(eU)\chi'
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\chi'=U^{-1}\chi.
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{Covariant derivative and minimal coupling}

Since
\[
\mathcal A_\mu = -i e^\dagger \partial_\mu e \in \mathfrak u(2),
\]
the covariant derivative acting on the coefficient field \(\chi\) is
\[
D_\mu \chi = \partial_\mu \chi - i \mathcal A_\mu \chi.
\]
Indeed, differentiating the section \(s=e\chi\),
\[
\partial_\mu s = (\partial_\mu e)\chi + e\,\partial_\mu \chi.
\]
Projecting back to \(Q\) with \(e^\dagger\), we obtain
\[
e^\dagger \partial_\mu s
=
e^\dagger (\partial_\mu e)\chi + \partial_\mu \chi
=
i\mathcal A_\mu \chi + \partial_\mu \chi.
\]
Thus the covariant derivative is forced by the bundle geometry:
\[
D_\mu \chi = \partial_\mu \chi - i\mathcal A_\mu \chi.
\]

This establishes a second unconditional fact:
if a physical field is realized as a section of \(Q\), then it is necessarily minimally coupled
to the composite \(U(2)\) connection.

\paragraph{Corollary 1.}
Any physical excitation identified with a local section of \(Q\) is automatically a \(U(2)\)
doublet and couples to the composite connection \(\mathcal A_\mu\) through the covariant
derivative \(D_\mu=\partial_\mu-i\mathcal A_\mu\).

\subsection{Restricted route I: tangent fluctuations of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)}

The tangent space of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) at \([z]\) may be described as
\[
T_{[z]}\mathbb{CP}^2
\cong
\mathrm{Hom}(L_{[z]},Q_{[z]}).
\]
Since \(L_{[z]}\) is one-dimensional,
\[
\mathrm{Hom}(L,Q)\cong L^*\otimes Q.
\]
Thus infinitesimal deformations \(\delta z\) orthogonal to the phase direction obey
\[
z^\dagger \delta z = 0,
\]
so that
\[
\delta z \in Q_{[z]}.
\]
In a local gauge where the line \(L\) is trivialized, tangent fluctuations may therefore be
represented locally by \(Q\)-valued fields.

This gives a precise but limited statement:

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Local tangent-mode theorem).}
Infinitesimal internal fluctuations of the projective order parameter, modulo phase, are locally
\(Q\)-valued. Hence their local coordinate fields transform as \(U(2)\) fundamentals.

\paragraph{Proof.}
A variation \(\delta z\) preserving the normalization and modding out the phase direction satisfies
\[
z^\dagger \delta z=0,
\]
which means \(\delta z\in Q\). Expressing \(\delta z=e\chi\), Theorem 1 gives
\[
\chi\mapsto U^{-1}\chi.
\]
\(\square\)

However, this theorem describes \emph{bosonic tangent fluctuations} of the internal order
parameter. It does not yet identify them with fermions, defect zero modes, or Standard Model
matter.

\subsection{Restricted route II: defect moduli and normal fluctuations}

Suppose there exists a defect background
\[
z_0(x;\,a)
\]
depending on collective coordinates \(a^I\).
Linearizing around it,
\[
z(x)=z_0(x;a)+\delta z(x).
\]
If the defect preserves the normalization and projective constraints to linear order, then
admissible fluctuations satisfy
\[
z_0^\dagger \delta z = 0
\]
modulo gauge/phase directions.
Hence the normal fluctuation sector lies in
\[
\delta z(x)\in Q_{z_0(x)}.
\]
Then the coefficients of \(\delta z=e\chi\) again transform as a local \(U(2)\) doublet.

This yields a restricted structural statement.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Restricted defect-doublet theorem).}
If a defect background in the TECT internal sector admits linearized normal fluctuations that are
orthogonal to the tautological line \(L=\mathbb Cz_0\), then those fluctuation coefficients form
a local \(U(2)\) doublet.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The orthogonality condition implies \(\delta z\in Q\). Expand in a local orthonormal frame
\[
\delta z=e\chi.
\]
Then Theorem 1 implies
\[
\chi\mapsto U^{-1}\chi.
\]
\(\square\)

Again, this is a \emph{conditional} theorem: it applies once such a defect background exists and
once the normal fluctuation sector is indeed \(Q\)-valued.

\subsection{Restricted route III: twisted Dirac operators and zero modes}

A more ambitious route uses a Dirac-type operator on the internal defect geometry.
Let
\[
D_Q
\]
be a twisted Dirac operator whose twisting bundle is built from \(Q\) or from an associated
bundle derived from the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) geometry.
Then any zero mode
\[
D_Q \psi = 0
\]
takes values in the corresponding twisted spinor bundle.

If the twisting factor contains \(Q\), then locally
\[
\psi = e\,\eta
\]
for some coefficient field \(\eta\in \mathbb C^2\), and under frame changes
\[
\eta \mapsto U^{-1}\eta.
\]
Therefore the zero mode again transforms as a \(U(2)\) fundamental in its bundle index.

This gives the following model theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Model twisted-zero-mode theorem).}
Let \(D_Q\) be a Dirac-type operator twisted by a bundle containing the factor \(Q\). Then any
local zero mode carries a \(U(2)\) fundamental index inherited from \(Q\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
The twisted spinor bundle has the local product structure
\[
S\otimes Q
\]
or a direct summand containing \(Q\). Choosing a local frame \(e\) of \(Q\), any section admits
the decomposition
\[
\psi = \sum_{a=1}^2 e_a \otimes \eta^a.
\]
Under \(e\mapsto eU\), the coefficient vector transforms as
\[
\eta\mapsto U^{-1}\eta.
\]
Hence the \(Q\)-bundle index is in the fundamental representation of \(U(2)\).
\(\square\)

This is mathematically correct, but it still does \emph{not} prove that the actual TECT defect
operator is of this form.

\subsection{What would be sufficient for a full TECT theorem}

We now isolate a sufficient criterion for the existence of physical \(Q\)-charged matter.

\paragraph{Proposition 5 (Sufficient criterion).}
Suppose the microscopic TECT linearized operator around a background \(z_0\) decomposes as
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}
=
\mathcal L_{\parallel}\oplus \mathcal L_{\perp}
\]
with
\[
\mathcal L_{\perp} : \Gamma(Q)\to \Gamma(Q),
\]
and suppose \(\mathcal L_{\perp}\) possesses normalizable low-energy eigenmodes
\[
\mathcal L_{\perp}\chi_n = \lambda_n \chi_n,
\qquad |\lambda_n|\ll \Lambda.
\]
Then the corresponding low-energy excitations are physical \(Q\)-valued modes, hence local
\(U(2)\) doublets.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By assumption, the relevant low-energy modes are sections of \(Q\). By Theorem 1 their local
coefficient fields transform in the fundamental representation of the local \(U(2)\) frame
bundle. \(\square\)

This proposition is important because it shows exactly what remains to be proven microscopically:
one must derive a \(Q\)-sector in the linearized condensate operator and show that it carries
normalizable low-energy modes.

\subsection{What is actually established at present}

We may summarize the precise logical status as follows.

\paragraph{Unconditional facts.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(Q\to\mathbb{CP}^2\) is a rank-two Hermitian bundle.
\item Local sections of \(Q\) transform as \(U(2)\) fundamentals.
\item If a physical mode is \(Q\)-valued, it couples minimally to the composite \(U(2)\)
connection.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Conditionally established facts.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Tangent fluctuations of the internal projective order parameter are locally \(Q\)-valued.
\item Defect normal modes are \(Q\)-doublets if the admissible fluctuation sector is orthogonal
to the tautological line.
\item Twisted Dirac zero modes carry a \(U(2)\) fundamental bundle index if the twisting bundle
contains \(Q\).
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not yet established.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item The full microscopic TECT PDE necessarily produces such \(Q\)-valued matter modes.
\item Those modes are fermionic electroweak doublets rather than bosonic internal fluctuations.
\item The action is chiral in exactly the Standard Model way.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

The central question is whether the TECT internal geometry produces physical matter fields
transforming in the fundamental representation of the emergent local \(U(2)\) bundle structure.

The precise bundle-theoretic result is:

\[
Q=(\mathbb C z)^\perp \to \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
is a rank-two Hermitian bundle, and every local section
\[
s=e\,\chi
\]
of \(Q\), written in a local orthonormal frame \(e\), transforms as
\[
\chi \mapsto U^{-1}\chi,
\qquad U(x)\in U(2).
\]
Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Any physical excitation realized as a section of }Q
\text{ is automatically a local }U(2)\text{ doublet.}
}
\]

Moreover, such a mode couples minimally to the composite connection
\[
\mathcal A_\mu=-ie^\dagger\partial_\mu e
\]
through
\[
D_\mu\chi=\partial_\mu\chi-i\mathcal A_\mu\chi.
\]

This is fully rigorous.

What is only conditionally true is the physical realization of such sections:
tangent fluctuations, defect normal modes, and twisted Dirac zero modes can all produce
\(Q\)-valued sectors, but only under additional assumptions about the microscopic operator or
defect background.

Thus the honest status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The }U(2)\text{-doublet transformation law is proved.}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{The existence of actual TECT }Q\text{-charged matter modes is not yet proved microscopically.}
}
\]

A sufficient criterion for the full theorem would be to derive, from the linearized TECT
condensate dynamics, an invariant sector
\[
\mathcal L_\perp : \Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q)
\]
with normalizable low-energy eigenmodes.
Only then would the existence of physical \(U(2)\) doublet matter be fully established.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Block Decomposition of the Linearized Internal Fluctuation Operator}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The physical internal field is a projective complex vector
\[
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,\qquad z^\dagger z=1,\qquad z\sim e^{i\alpha(x)}z,
\]
so the target space is \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A2: At each point \(z\), the ambient space decomposes as
\[
\mathbb C^3 = L_z \oplus Q_z,
\qquad
L_z=\mathbb Cz,\qquad Q_z=(\mathbb Cz)^\perp.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A3: Infinitesimal physical fluctuations modulo normalization and phase are represented by
\(Q\)-valued perturbations.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in current framework}}
\]

\item A4: The linearized operator preserves the \(Q\)-sector:
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption in general}}
\]

\item A5: For projective, \(U(1)\)-redundant sigma-model/projector-type dynamics, the linearized
operator restricted to physical fluctuations closes on \(Q\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in current framework}}
\]

\item A6: The full microscopic TECT condensate PDE has the same block structure.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We want to determine when the linearized fluctuation operator around a background
\[
z_0(x)\in \mathbb C^3,\qquad z_0^\dagger z_0 = 1,
\]
admits a decomposition into longitudinal and transverse/projective sectors,
schematically
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}
=
\mathcal L_{\parallel}\oplus \mathcal L_{\perp},
\]
with
\[
\mathcal L_{\perp}:\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\to\Gamma(Q_{z_0}).
\]

We distinguish carefully between:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the kinematic decomposition of the ambient space \(\mathbb C^3=L\oplus Q\),
\item the representation of physical tangent fluctuations by \(Q\)-valued fields,
\item the dynamical closure of the linearized PDE on that \(Q\)-sector.
\end{enumerate}

Only the first two are unconditional at present.

\subsection{Ambient-space decomposition and projectors}

Let \(z_0(x)\) be a background section with
\[
z_0^\dagger z_0 = 1.
\]
Define the rank-one projector
\[
P := z_0 z_0^\dagger,
\qquad
P^2=P,
\qquad
P^\dagger=P,
\qquad
\mathrm{Tr}\,P=1,
\]
and the orthogonal complement projector
\[
\Pi := \mathbf 1_3 - P.
\]
Then
\[
\Pi^2=\Pi,
\qquad
\Pi^\dagger=\Pi,
\qquad
\Pi z_0 = 0.
\]
Hence
\[
L_{z_0} = \mathrm{Im}(P)=\mathbb C z_0,
\qquad
Q_{z_0} = \mathrm{Im}(\Pi)= (\mathbb C z_0)^\perp.
\]
Any ambient fluctuation \(\delta z\in \mathbb C^3\) splits uniquely as
\[
\delta z = P\delta z + \Pi \delta z.
\]
Since \(P\delta z = z_0(z_0^\dagger \delta z)\), the longitudinal component lies along \(z_0\),
while \(\Pi\delta z\in Q_{z_0}\).

\subsection{Physical infinitesimal fluctuations of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)}

Because the physical field is projective, not all \(\delta z\) correspond to physical directions.
We must quotient out:

\begin{enumerate}
\item normalization changes,
\item phase changes \( \delta z \sim i\alpha\, z_0 \).
\end{enumerate}

Linearizing the normalization constraint
\[
(z_0+\delta z)^\dagger (z_0+\delta z)=1
\]
gives
\[
z_0^\dagger \delta z + \delta z^\dagger z_0 = 0,
\]
equivalently
\[
2\,\mathrm{Re}(z_0^\dagger \delta z)=0.
\]
Thus \(z_0^\dagger \delta z\) is purely imaginary. But an infinitesimal phase transformation
\[
\delta z = i\alpha\, z_0
\]
has exactly
\[
z_0^\dagger \delta z = i\alpha.
\]
Hence, modulo phase, every physical infinitesimal fluctuation can be represented by a gauge-fixed
perturbation satisfying
\[
z_0^\dagger \delta z = 0.
\]
Therefore physical fluctuations may be represented locally as sections of \(Q_{z_0}\).

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Physical tangent representation).}
The tangent space to \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) at \([z_0]\) is represented by
\[
T_{[z_0]}\mathbb{CP}^2 \cong Q_{z_0}
\]
after choosing a local phase gauge.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The ambient variation space is \(\mathbb C^3\). The linearized unit-sphere condition removes one
real direction, and quotienting by the phase \(U(1)\) removes another real direction. The
remaining four real dimensions form the tangent space of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), and are represented
by the complex two-dimensional orthogonal complement
\[
Q_{z_0} = \{ v\in \mathbb C^3 \mid z_0^\dagger v=0\}.
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{General linearized operator and obstruction to block-diagonalization}

Let the microscopic or effective equation of motion be
\[
E[z]=0,
\]
with \(E\) covariant under phase redundancy.
Linearizing around a background \(z_0\) gives
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}\,\delta z = 0,
\qquad
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} := \left.\frac{\delta E}{\delta z}\right|_{z_0}.
\]

In the ambient decomposition \(\delta z = P\delta z + \Pi\delta z\), one may always write
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
P \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} P & P \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} \Pi \\
\Pi \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} P & \Pi \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} \Pi
\end{pmatrix}
\]
relative to \(L\oplus Q\).

Thus the \(Q\)-sector is dynamically closed if and only if
\[
P \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} \Pi = 0
\qquad\text{on admissible fluctuations,}
\]
and similarly the longitudinal sector decouples if
\[
\Pi \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} P = 0.
\]
In a completely general complex \(3\)-component PDE, these off-diagonal blocks need not vanish.

Therefore the statement
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}:\Gamma(Q)\to \Gamma(Q)
\]
is not automatic from linear algebra alone.

\subsection{Projective sigma-model structure}

Consider now the standard projector-based or \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)-sigma-type action
\[
S[z]
=
\frac{\rho}{2}\int d^dx\, (D_\mu z)^\dagger (D^\mu z),
\]
where
\[
D_\mu z := \Pi\,\partial_\mu z = \partial_\mu z - z(z^\dagger \partial_\mu z).
\]
Equivalently, in projector form,
\[
S[P]
=
\frac{\rho}{4}\int d^dx\, \mathrm{Tr}(\partial_\mu P\, \partial^\mu P).
\]

The key property is
\[
D_\mu z \in Q_z,
\qquad
z^\dagger D_\mu z = 0.
\]
Thus the dynamics depends only on derivatives projected orthogonally to the tautological line.

\subsection{Linearization in background gauge}

Let
\[
z = z_0 + \eta,
\qquad
z_0^\dagger \eta = 0.
\]
This is the natural background gauge fixing of the projective redundancy.

We now linearize \(D_\mu z\).
Since
\[
D_\mu z = \Pi(z)\,\partial_\mu z,
\qquad
\Pi(z)=\mathbf 1 - z z^\dagger,
\]
we obtain to first order
\[
\delta(D_\mu z)
=
(\delta \Pi)\,\partial_\mu z_0 + \Pi_0\,\partial_\mu \eta,
\]
where
\[
\Pi_0 = \mathbf 1 - z_0 z_0^\dagger,
\qquad
\delta \Pi = -\eta z_0^\dagger - z_0 \eta^\dagger.
\]
Using \(z_0^\dagger \eta=0\), we find
\[
\delta \Pi\, \partial_\mu z_0
=
-\eta (z_0^\dagger \partial_\mu z_0) - z_0 (\eta^\dagger \partial_\mu z_0).
\]
The second term is longitudinal, proportional to \(z_0\). It is a gauge artifact associated with
the phase bundle, and may be absorbed into the induced \(U(1)\) connection of the tautological
line. The genuinely physical transverse fluctuation is therefore the \(Q\)-projected quantity
\[
\Pi_0\,\delta(D_\mu z)
=
-\eta (z_0^\dagger \partial_\mu z_0) + \Pi_0\partial_\mu \eta.
\]
Since both terms lie in \(Q\), the principal part closes on \(Q\).

This suggests introducing the background \(U(1)\) covariant derivative
\[
\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\eta
:=
\Pi_0 \partial_\mu \eta - (z_0^\dagger \partial_\mu z_0)\,\eta.
\]
Then
\[
\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\eta \in Q_{z_0}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Closure of the principal linearized operator).}
For projective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)-sigma dynamics written in terms of \(D_\mu z=\Pi \partial_\mu z\),
the linearized physical fluctuation operator around a background \(z_0\), in the gauge
\(z_0^\dagger \eta=0\), has principal part acting within \(\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
The linearized derivative is the \(Q\)-valued object
\[
\Pi_0\,\delta(D_\mu z)
=
\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\eta.
\]
Hence the second-order principal term appearing in the Euler--Lagrange operator is of the form
\[
-(\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\nabla^{(0)\mu})\eta,
\]
which maps \(\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\) to itself. \(\square\)

\subsection{Explicit Hessian structure}

Let us now sketch the quadratic form. Writing
\[
z=z_0+\eta,\qquad z_0^\dagger\eta=0,
\]
the action expands as
\[
S[z_0+\eta]
=
S[z_0]
+
\delta S[z_0;\eta]
+
\frac12 \delta^2 S[z_0;\eta]
+\cdots.
\]
If \(z_0\) satisfies the background equation of motion, then
\[
\delta S[z_0;\eta]=0.
\]
The quadratic term has the general form
\[
\delta^2 S
=
\int d^dx\,
\eta^\dagger
\left[
-\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\nabla^{(0)\mu}
+\mathcal M[z_0]
\right]\eta,
\]
where \(\mathcal M[z_0]\) is a background-dependent Hermitian endomorphism of \(Q\).

Thus the linearized fluctuation operator takes the form
\[
\mathcal L_\perp
=
-\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\nabla^{(0)\mu}
+\mathcal M[z_0],
\qquad
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\to \Gamma(Q_{z_0}).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Restricted block decomposition theorem).}
For any projective sigma-model or projector-type internal dynamics depending only on the
\(Q\)-projected derivatives of \(z\), the physical Hessian around a background \(z_0\) defines a
closed operator
\[
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\to \Gamma(Q_{z_0}).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The proof follows from three facts:

\begin{enumerate}
\item physical fluctuations are represented by \(\eta\in \Gamma(Q_{z_0})\),
\item the projected derivative \(\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\eta\) remains \(Q\)-valued,
\item the Hessian is built from repeated applications of this covariant derivative and background
endomorphisms preserving \(Q\).
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the quadratic operator maps \(Q\)-sections to \(Q\)-sections. \(\square\)

\subsection{Why this does not yet prove the full TECT statement}

The above theorem is rigorous for sigma-model/projector-type dynamics, but not yet for an
arbitrary microscopic condensate PDE. A general TECT microscopic equation might contain:

\begin{enumerate}
\item anisotropic couplings mixing longitudinal and transverse sectors,
\item higher-derivative nonprojective terms,
\item explicit dependence on an ambient \(\mathbb C^3\) representative rather than only on the
projective class \([z]\),
\item couplings to additional condensate variables that reintroduce \(L\)-\(Q\) mixing.
\end{enumerate}

In those cases the off-diagonal blocks
\[
P\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}\Pi,\qquad \Pi\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}P
\]
need not vanish.

Hence the full microscopic theorem would require proving that the TECT PDE itself is projective
in the relevant internal sector, or at least that its linearization around the chosen background
admits a gauge-compatible reduction to \(Q\).

\subsection{Sufficient criterion for full microscopic closure}

We isolate the exact condition needed.

\paragraph{Proposition 4 (Sufficient criterion for \(Q\)-sector closure).}
Let the TECT internal equation \(E[z]=0\) satisfy the following around a background \(z_0\):

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(E[z]\) depends on \(z\) only through the projective data \([z]\), equivalently through
\(P=zz^\dagger\) or \(Q\)-projected derivatives,
\item the linearized phase gauge is fixed by \(z_0^\dagger \eta=0\),
\item all background-dependent endomorphisms preserve \(Q_{z_0}\).
\end{enumerate}

Then the physical linearized operator closes on \(Q_{z_0}\):
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}^{\mathrm{phys}}:\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\to \Gamma(Q_{z_0}).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Under assumptions (1)--(3), the equation of motion and its linearization are built only from
operators that preserve the orthogonal decomposition into the tautological line and its
orthogonal complement after gauge fixing. Therefore the projected physical Hessian acts entirely
within \(\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\). \(\square\)

\subsection{Consequences for matter-mode emergence}

Once a closed operator
\[
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q)
\]
exists, its eigenmodes
\[
\mathcal L_\perp \chi_n = \lambda_n \chi_n
\]
are genuine \(Q\)-valued physical excitations.
By the bundle-fundamental theorem, their local coefficients transform as
\[
\chi_n \mapsto U^{-1}\chi_n,
\qquad U(x)\in U(2).
\]
Therefore they are automatically local \(U(2)\) doublets.

This does not yet prove that low-lying or zero eigenmodes exist, but it does show that the
existence problem has been reduced to an honest spectral question for a closed \(Q\)-bundle
operator.

\section{Final Summary}

Let
\[
z_0(x)\in \mathbb C^3,\qquad z_0^\dagger z_0=1,
\]
and define
\[
P=z_0z_0^\dagger,\qquad \Pi=\mathbf 1-P.
\]
Then the ambient fluctuation space decomposes as
\[
\mathbb C^3 = L_{z_0}\oplus Q_{z_0},
\qquad
L_{z_0}=\mathbb C z_0,\qquad Q_{z_0}=(\mathbb C z_0)^\perp.
\]

After quotienting the normalization and phase redundancy of the projective field \( [z]\in
\mathbb{CP}^2 \), physical infinitesimal fluctuations are represented by
\[
\eta\in \Gamma(Q_{z_0}),
\qquad
z_0^\dagger \eta=0.
\]

For a general microscopic complex three-component PDE, the linearized operator has block form
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
P \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} P & P \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} \Pi \\
\Pi \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} P & \Pi \mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} \Pi
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and the off-diagonal blocks need not vanish. Therefore \(Q\)-sector closure is not automatic.

However, for projective sigma-model/projector-type dynamics depending only on \(Q\)-projected
derivatives, the physical Hessian in background gauge \(z_0^\dagger\eta=0\) takes the form
\[
\mathcal L_\perp
=
-\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\nabla^{(0)\mu}
+\mathcal M[z_0],
\qquad
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q_{z_0})\to\Gamma(Q_{z_0}),
\]
with \(\nabla_\mu^{(0)}\) the induced background covariant derivative on \(Q\).

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The }Q\text{-sector is geometrically natural and dynamically closed for projective
sigma-model-type dynamics.}
}
\]

But:

\[
\boxed{
\text{It is not yet proven that the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE has this same block
decomposition.}
}
\]

If such closure holds microscopically, then the existence of physical \(U(2)\)-doublet matter
reduces to the spectral problem
\[
\mathcal L_\perp \chi_n = \lambda_n \chi_n
\]
for a closed operator acting on \(Q\)-valued sections.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Sufficient Conditions for Zero Modes and Light Modes in the \(Q\)-Sector}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: A closed \(Q\)-sector operator exists,
\[
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in restricted projector/sigma-model frameworks}}
\]

\item A2: The operator is elliptic/self-adjoint or otherwise admits a controlled spectral theory.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption}}
\]

\item A3: Topology alone forces zero modes for a generic bosonic second-order operator.
\[
\boxed{\text{False in general}}
\]

\item A4: A \(Q\)-twisted Dirac-type operator exists.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in model constructions, not yet from full TECT}}
\]

\item A5: If the Dirac index is nonzero, then zero modes must exist.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A6: Defect backgrounds may create localized bound states through mass inversion or trapping.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable under explicit spectral assumptions}}
\]

\item A7: The resulting modes coincide exactly with Standard Model chiral fermions.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We separate three distinct mechanisms for low-energy modes in the \(Q\)-sector:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Index-forced zero modes} for Dirac-type operators,
\item \textbf{Defect-trapped bound states} for Schr\"odinger/Dirac operators in nontrivial backgrounds,
\item \textbf{Accidental or symmetry-protected light modes} from small eigenvalues of a self-adjoint operator.
\end{enumerate}

Only the first gives a robust topological theorem.
The second gives useful sufficient criteria.
The third is the weakest and most model-dependent.

\subsection{Why generic bosonic second-order operators do not admit topological zero-mode theorems}

Let
\[
\mathcal L_\perp = -\nabla^\mu \nabla_\mu + \mathcal M(x)
\]
be a second-order Hermitian operator acting on \(Q\)-valued sections over some internal or
effective defect geometry.

Even if \(Q\) is topologically nontrivial, the existence of a zero mode
\[
\mathcal L_\perp \chi = 0
\]
is not guaranteed by topology alone. Indeed, the spectrum depends on the endomorphism
\[
\mathcal M(x),
\]
and a generic positive shift
\[
\mathcal M(x)\mapsto \mathcal M(x)+m_0^2 \mathbf 1
\]
can lift all eigenvalues away from zero.

Therefore:

\paragraph{Proposition 1.}
For a generic bosonic second-order operator on \(Q\)-valued sections, topology of the bundle
\(Q\) alone does not force the existence of a zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Take any elliptic self-adjoint operator \(\mathcal L_\perp\) and add a sufficiently large positive
constant \(m_0^2\mathbf 1\). The spectrum shifts by \(m_0^2\), eliminating any zero eigenvalue.
Hence zero modes are not topologically protected for generic second-order bosonic operators.
\(\square\)

This is a crucial honesty point: we must not overclaim topological protection unless the operator
has first-order chiral structure.

\subsection{Dirac-type operators and index protection}

Now consider instead a Dirac-type operator twisted by the \(Q\)-bundle:
\[
D_Q : \Gamma(S^+\otimes Q\otimes \mathcal E)\to \Gamma(S^-\otimes Q\otimes \mathcal E),
\]
where \(S^\pm\) are chiral spinor bundles and \(\mathcal E\) denotes any additional twisting.

Its chiral index is
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)
=
\dim \ker D_Q^+ - \dim \ker D_Q^-.
\]
By the Atiyah--Singer index theorem,
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)
=
\int_M \widehat A(M)\,\mathrm{ch}(Q\otimes \mathcal E)\Big|_{\dim M}.
\]

Therefore:

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Index-forced zero-mode theorem).}
If \(D_Q\) is a Fredholm Dirac-type operator and
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)\neq 0,
\]
then \(D_Q\) has at least one zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
If both kernels were empty, then
\[
\dim\ker D_Q^+ = \dim\ker D_Q^- =0,
\]
hence \(\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=0\), contradiction. More generally, nonzero index implies that at
least one of \(\ker D_Q^\pm\) is nontrivial. \(\square\)

This is the cleanest route to protected chiral zero modes.

\subsection{Connection with the previously derived \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) index}

In the model constructions already discussed, the internal defect geometry was associated with
\(\mathbb{CP}^2\) and a twisted spin\(^c\) Dirac operator with index
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
\]
For primitive twisting
\[
(q,m)=(1,1),
\]
this gives
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
\]
Hence one obtains
\[
n_L-n_R =1,
\]
which forces a chiral zero mode.

This remains mathematically correct for the model Dirac operator, but it does not yet prove that
the same operator is derived from the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE.

Thus we have:

\paragraph{Corollary 3.}
If the effective low-energy defect operator in TECT is indeed the previously identified twisted
Dirac operator on the relevant internal geometry, then a nonzero chiral zero mode is forced.

\subsection{Defect-localized bound states without index theory}

A second mechanism for light or zero modes is trapping by a defect background.

Consider a self-adjoint operator of Schr\"odinger type
\[
\mathcal L_\perp = -\partial_\xi^2 + V(\xi)
\]
in the coordinate \(\xi\) transverse to a codimension-one defect, or more generally
\[
\mathcal L_\perp = -\nabla^2 + V(x)
\]
on the defect background.

If \(V(x)\) develops a sufficiently deep localized well, then the discrete spectrum contains a
bound state
\[
\mathcal L_\perp \chi_n = \lambda_n \chi_n,
\qquad
\lambda_n < \lambda_{\mathrm{cont}},
\]
where \(\lambda_{\mathrm{cont}}\) is the continuum threshold.

This gives:

\paragraph{Proposition 4 (Bound-state sufficient criterion).}
Suppose \(\mathcal L_\perp\) is self-adjoint and its effective potential \(V(x)\) contains a
localized attractive region strong enough to create a discrete eigenvalue below the continuum.
Then \(\mathcal L_\perp\) has a normalizable localized light mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is a standard consequence of spectral theory for Schr\"odinger-type operators: a sufficiently
attractive localized potential well creates discrete spectrum below the essential spectrum.
\(\square\)

This proves only a \emph{sufficient} criterion, not a universal theorem.

\subsection{Jackiw--Rebbi-type mechanism for first-order operators}

For a one-dimensional transverse profile, consider a Dirac-type operator
\[
\mathcal D = i\Gamma^\xi \partial_\xi + m(\xi),
\]
with asymptotic mass profile satisfying
\[
m(+\infty)\,m(-\infty)<0.
\]
Then the sign change in the mass can support a localized zero mode.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Jackiw--Rebbi-type sufficient criterion).}
If the effective first-order defect operator has a mass term whose sign flips across the defect,
then there exists a normalizable localized zero mode.

\paragraph{Sketch of proof.}
The zero-mode equation
\[
(i\Gamma^\xi\partial_\xi + m(\xi))\psi=0
\]
reduces, after choosing a definite \(\Gamma^\xi\)-eigencomponent, to
\[
\partial_\xi \psi = \mp m(\xi)\psi.
\]
Hence
\[
\psi(\xi)\propto \exp\!\left(\mp\int^\xi m(\xi')\,d\xi'\right).
\]
When \(m(\xi)\) changes sign, one choice of sign yields exponential decay at both ends, producing
a normalizable zero mode. \(\square\)

Again, this is powerful but model-dependent: one needs an explicit effective first-order operator
with such a mass profile.

\subsection{Light modes from approximate symmetry or near-BPS structure}

A third route is not exact zero modes but parametrically small eigenvalues.
Suppose
\[
\mathcal L_\perp = \mathcal L_0 + \varepsilon\,\Delta \mathcal L,
\qquad 0<\varepsilon\ll 1,
\]
and \(\mathcal L_0\) has an exact zero mode
\[
\mathcal L_0 \chi_0 = 0.
\]
Then by perturbation theory,
\[
\lambda(\varepsilon)
=
\varepsilon \langle \chi_0,\Delta\mathcal L\,\chi_0\rangle
+O(\varepsilon^2).
\]
Hence a small deformation of a symmetry-protected or BPS zero mode produces a light mode.

\paragraph{Proposition 6 (Near-zero-mode criterion).}
If an operator is a small deformation of one admitting an exact zero mode, then the zero mode
persists as a light mode with eigenvalue \(O(\varepsilon)\), provided no level crossing removes it.

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is standard analytic perturbation theory for isolated eigenvalues of self-adjoint operators.
\(\square\)

This may be relevant if the TECT defect sector is approximately BPS, approximately chiral, or
approximately factorized.

\subsection{What is sufficient for a TECT theorem}

We can now formulate the exact conditions under which a genuine TECT theorem would follow.

\paragraph{Theorem 7 (Sufficient criteria for physical \(Q\)-sector low-energy modes).}
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item a closed \(Q\)-sector operator exists,
\[
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q),
\]
\item one of the following three conditions holds:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(I)] \(\mathcal L_\perp\) is derived from a Fredholm Dirac-type operator \(D_Q\) with
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)\neq 0,
\]
or

\item[(II)] \(\mathcal L_\perp\) is a self-adjoint defect operator whose effective potential has a
localized attractive well generating discrete spectrum below the continuum, or

\item[(III)] \(\mathcal L_\perp\) is a small deformation of an operator with an exact isolated zero
mode.
\end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

Then \(\mathcal L_\perp\) possesses either an exact zero mode or a parametrically light
normalizable mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Case (I) follows from Theorem 2.
Case (II) follows from Proposition 4.
Case (III) follows from Proposition 6.
\(\square\)

\subsection{What remains missing for the full TECT program}

Even if one proves the existence of such a mode, further steps are still needed:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identification of the actual microscopic operator producing it,
\item proof that the mode is fermionic rather than merely bosonic,
\item proof of chirality selection consistent with \(SU(2)_L\),
\item hypercharge embedding,
\item anomaly cancellation.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the low-energy-mode theorem is necessary, but not sufficient, for full Standard Model
recovery.

\section{Final Summary}

Let
\[
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q)
\]
be the closed operator acting on the \(Q\)-sector.

There are three logically distinct mechanisms for low-energy modes:

\subsection*{(1) Index-forced zero modes}
If \(\mathcal L_\perp\) arises from a Fredholm Dirac-type operator \(D_Q\) with
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)\neq 0,
\]
then a zero mode is forced:
\[
\ker D_Q \neq 0.
\]

This is the strongest and most rigorous mechanism.

\subsection*{(2) Defect-trapped bound states}
If \(\mathcal L_\perp\) is a self-adjoint defect operator with a sufficiently attractive localized
effective potential, then it possesses a normalizable bound state
\[
\mathcal L_\perp \chi = \lambda \chi,
\qquad
\lambda < \lambda_{\mathrm{cont}}.
\]
This provides a light localized mode.

\subsection*{(3) Near-zero modes from deformation}
If
\[
\mathcal L_\perp = \mathcal L_0 + \varepsilon \Delta\mathcal L,
\qquad
0<\varepsilon\ll 1,
\]
and \(\mathcal L_0\) has an exact isolated zero mode, then perturbation theory yields
\[
\lambda(\varepsilon)=O(\varepsilon),
\]
so the zero mode persists as a light mode.

\subsection*{What is not true}
For a generic bosonic second-order operator,
\[
\mathcal L_\perp = -\nabla^2 + \mathcal M(x),
\]
the topology of \(Q\) alone does \emph{not} force zero modes.
Thus one must not claim topological protection without a Dirac/chiral structure.

\subsection*{Honest conclusion}
The strongest rigorous route to protected \(Q\)-sector zero modes is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{derive a \(Q\)-twisted Dirac-type operator with nonzero index.}
}
\]

Without this, one may still obtain localized light modes from defect trapping or approximate
symmetry, but these are more model-dependent and require explicit spectral analysis.

Therefore the next decisive mathematical task is not merely to postulate a \(Q\)-sector, but to
show that the effective TECT defect operator in that sector is genuinely of Dirac type, or else
to prove a concrete trapping theorem for the microscopic defect background.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Emergence of an Effective First-Order Weyl/Dirac Operator}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The microscopic or effective linearized operator admits a momentum-space or Bloch-type
description.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption}}
\]

\item A2: Around a periodic BCC background, a Bloch decomposition exists.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in periodic background models}}
\]

\item A3: There exists a momentum \(k_*\) with an isolated two-dimensional low-energy subspace.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra assumption}}
\]

\item A4: The projected operator admits a smooth expansion in \(p=k-k_*\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in current framework}}
\]

\item A5: The linear term has full rank and produces a nondegenerate Weyl crossing.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once the crossing assumptions are imposed}}
\]

\item A6: A symmetry relates \(k_*\) and \(-k_*\), producing valley doubling.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable under symmetry assumptions}}
\]

\item A7: Defect backgrounds admit an envelope-mode reduction to an effective first-order operator.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in restricted constructions}}
\]

\item A8: The full TECT BCC condensate realizes all of the above assumptions.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We aim to prove the following conditional statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If a linearized TECT sector has an isolated two-band linear crossing, then its low-energy
effective operator is of Weyl type.}
}
\]

A further symmetry pairing of opposite valleys then yields a Dirac operator.

The proof is structural and does not yet establish that the TECT microscopic PDE indeed possesses
the required valleys.

\subsection{Abstract linearized operator and spectral projection}

Let \( \mathcal L(k) \) be a Hermitian family of operators depending smoothly on momentum \(k\)
near a point \(k_*\in \mathbb R^3\). Assume that at \(k_*\) there exists an isolated two-dimensional
eigenspace
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}(k_*) = \mathrm{span}\{u_1,u_2\},
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}(k_*)=2,
\]
separated by a spectral gap from the rest of the spectrum.

Let
\[
\Pi_* : \mathcal H \to \mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}(k_*)
\]
be the corresponding spectral projector. Then for \(p=k-k_*\), degenerate perturbation theory gives
the effective Hamiltonian
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\Pi_* \mathcal L(k_*+p)\Pi_* \big|_{\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}}
=
H_0 + p_i V_i + O(|p|^2),
\]
where
\[
H_0 := \Pi_* \mathcal L(k_*) \Pi_*,
\qquad
V_i := \Pi_* \left(\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L\right)_{k_*}\Pi_*.
\]

If the two low-energy levels are degenerate at \(k_*\), then
\[
H_0 = E_* \mathbf 1_2.
\]
Subtracting the irrelevant scalar \(E_*\mathbf 1_2\), we obtain
\[
\widetilde H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
p_i V_i + O(|p|^2).
\]

\subsection{Hermitian \(2\times 2\) decomposition}

Every Hermitian \(2\times 2\) matrix may be decomposed uniquely as
\[
M = a_0 \mathbf 1_2 + a_a \sigma^a,
\qquad a_0,a_a\in \mathbb R,
\qquad a=1,2,3.
\]
Hence each velocity matrix \(V_i\) may be written as
\[
V_i = v_i^{(0)}\mathbf 1_2 + v_i^{(a)}\sigma^a.
\]
Therefore
\[
\widetilde H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + d_a(p)\sigma^a + O(|p|^2),
\]
with
\[
\varepsilon_0(p)=p_i v_i^{(0)},
\qquad
d_a(p)=p_i v_i^{(a)}.
\]

The scalar term \(\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2\) merely tilts or shifts the cone and is irrelevant
for chirality. The essential part is the traceless term
\[
H_{\mathrm{W}}(p)=d_a(p)\sigma^a.
\]

\subsection{Weyl criterion}

Define the \(3\times 3\) real matrix
\[
M_{ai} := v_i^{(a)}.
\]
Then
\[
d_a(p)=M_{ai}p_i.
\]
If
\[
\det M \neq 0,
\]
then \(d(p)\neq 0\) for all sufficiently small \(p\neq 0\), so the dispersion is conical:
\[
E_\pm(p)=\varepsilon_0(p)\pm |Mp| + O(|p|^2).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Two-band Weyl reduction).}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal L(k)\) is smooth near \(k_*\),
\item there is an isolated two-dimensional degenerate low-energy subspace at \(k_*\),
\item the linearized traceless velocity matrix \(M_{ai}\) is invertible.
\end{enumerate}

Then the projected low-energy operator is, up to linear changes of momentum coordinates and
unitary basis changes in the two-dimensional eigenspace, of Weyl form:
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + \sum_{a=1}^3 \tilde v_a p_a \sigma^a + O(|p|^2),
\]
with all \(\tilde v_a\neq 0\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Hermitian \(2\times 2\) decomposition,
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + (Mp)\cdot \sigma + O(|p|^2).
\]
If \(\det M\neq 0\), then after an invertible linear transformation of momenta
\[
p\mapsto p' = Mp,
\]
the traceless part becomes
\[
p'_a \sigma^a.
\]
A further unitary basis change rotates the Pauli matrices by an \(SO(3)\) transformation, so the
operator is equivalent to the standard Weyl form. \(\square\)

\subsection{Topological chirality of the crossing}

For a Weyl point, the map
\[
\hat d(p)=\frac{d(p)}{|d(p)|}
\]
defines
\[
\hat d : S^2_{p}\to S^2,
\]
whose degree is the chirality of the node:
\[
\chi = \mathrm{sgn}(\det M)\in \{\pm 1\}.
\]
Thus the nondegenerate two-band crossing carries a topological charge.

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem 1, the isolated crossing at \(k_*\) is a Weyl point with chirality
\[
\chi = \mathrm{sgn}(\det M).
\]

\subsection{BCC-shell-adapted coordinates}

Suppose now that the crossing occurs near a BCC reciprocal-shell momentum \(G_*\), and let
\[
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
Choose orthonormal coordinates
\[
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\]
Then the effective Hamiltonian can always be written as
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2)
\]
after suitable basis choices, provided the linear crossing is nondegenerate. The Weyl criterion is
then simply
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0.
\]

\paragraph{Corollary 3 (BCC-shell Weyl form).}
If a BCC-shell valley \(G_*\) supports an isolated nondegenerate two-band crossing, then the
projected low-energy Hamiltonian takes the shell-adapted Weyl form above.

\subsection{Valley doubling and Dirac emergence}

Assume now that there exists a symmetry \(\Theta\) relating the two valleys \(k_*\) and \(-k_*\),
for instance inversion or a generalized time-reversal/valley symmetry, such that
\[
\Theta \mathcal L(k)\Theta^{-1} = \mathcal L(-k).
\]
Suppose further that the chiralities of the two valleys are opposite, as required for a paired
Dirac structure. Let
\[
\psi_+(p)\in \mathbb C^2
\]
be the Weyl spinor near \(k_*\), and
\[
\psi_-(p)\in \mathbb C^2
\]
the partner near \(-k_*\).

Define the four-component spinor
\[
\Psi(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi_+(p)\\
\psi_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the block Hamiltonian takes the form
\[
H_D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_+(p) & 0\\
0 & H_-(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with
\[
H_+(p)= v_i p_i \sigma^i,
\qquad
H_-(p)= - v_i p_i \sigma^i
\]
after suitable conventions. This is equivalent to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(p)=\alpha^i p_i.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Valley-doubling Dirac theorem).}
If two opposite valleys \(k_*\) and \(-k_*\) carry Weyl Hamiltonians of opposite chirality and are
related by a symmetry pairing, then the combined low-energy theory is a massless Dirac theory.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Stacking the two Weyl Hamiltonians in block form gives
\[
H_D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
v_i p_i \sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -v_i p_i \sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Defining
\[
\alpha^i =
\begin{pmatrix}
\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix},
\]
one obtains
\[
H_D(p)=\alpha^i p_i.
\]
This is the standard massless Dirac form. \(\square\)

\subsection{Defect-envelope derivation}

We now give the restricted defect version. Let a background defect preserve translational symmetry
along coordinates \(y^\mu\) (\(\mu=0,1,\dots,d-1\)) and vary slowly or nontrivially in transverse
coordinates \(\xi\). Consider a linearized fluctuation equation
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}} \Phi = 0.
\]
Assume there exists a localized transverse mode \(u_0(\xi)\) satisfying
\[
\mathcal L_\perp u_0 = 0,
\]
or more generally a low-lying subspace spanned by \(\{u_a(\xi)\}_{a=1,2}\). Use the ansatz
\[
\Phi(y,\xi)=\sum_{a=1}^2 \psi_a(y)\,u_a(\xi)+\text{higher modes}.
\]
Projecting the linearized operator onto this low-energy subspace yields an effective operator on
the envelope field \(\psi(y)\in\mathbb C^2\):
\[
H_{\mathrm{env}}(-i\partial_y)
=
\langle u_a,\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}u_b\rangle_\xi.
\]
If the leading derivative couplings among the low-energy modes are linear in \(\partial_{y^\mu}\)
and produce a full-rank Pauli structure, then
\[
H_{\mathrm{env}}(p_y)
=
\varepsilon_0(p_y)\mathbf 1_2 + c_\mu p_y^\mu \sigma^\mu + O(p_y^2),
\]
which is again Weyl/Dirac type after valley doubling if a partner sector exists.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Restricted defect-envelope Weyl theorem).}
Suppose a defect background admits a two-dimensional localized low-energy subspace and that the
projected envelope operator is linear to first order in worldvolume momentum with full-rank
Pauli-matrix coefficients. Then the defect worldvolume theory is Weyl type, and Dirac type if an
opposite-chirality partner sector is present.

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is the same projected two-band argument as in Theorem 1, now applied to the effective
worldvolume operator obtained by transverse-mode projection. \(\square\)

\subsection{What is actually proven and what is not}

We emphasize the exact logical status.

\paragraph{Proved structurally.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Any isolated two-band linear crossing yields a Weyl Hamiltonian after projection.
\item Any symmetry-paired opposite-chirality valley double yields a Dirac Hamiltonian.
\item The same applies to defect-envelope reductions if a two-dimensional localized low-energy
subspace exists.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not yet proved for full TECT.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the microscopic BCC condensate operator actually has isolated valleys at \(\pm G_*\).
\item That the low-energy subspace is exactly two-dimensional.
\item That the linear velocity matrix is nondegenerate:
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0.
\]
\item That the defect-background operator produces the required localized doublet.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the Weyl/Dirac emergence theorem is mathematically correct as a conditional theorem, but its
full TECT realization remains an open spectral problem.

\section{Final Summary}

The emergence of a first-order Weyl or Dirac operator can be formulated as a precise conditional
spectral theorem.

Let \(\mathcal L(k)\) be a smooth Hermitian family near \(k_*\), and assume that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item there exists an isolated two-dimensional degenerate low-energy subspace at \(k_*\),
\item the projected linear velocity matrices generate a full-rank traceless part.
\end{enumerate}

Then the projected low-energy operator is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + d_a(p)\sigma^a + O(|p|^2),
\qquad
p=k-k_*,
\]
with
\[
d_a(p)=M_{ai}p_i.
\]
If
\[
\det M\neq 0,
\]
this is equivalent to a Weyl Hamiltonian:
\[
H_{\mathrm{W}}(p)\sim v_i p_i \sigma^i.
\]

For a BCC-shell-adapted basis,
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
and the Weyl criterion is
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0.
\]
