If a symmetry relates \(k_*\) and \(-k_*\) and the two valleys have opposite chirality, then
their combined four-component theory is Dirac:
\[
H_D(p)=\alpha^i p_i.
\]

The same logic applies to defect-envelope reductions: a two-dimensional localized low-energy
subspace plus a full-rank linear projected operator implies a Weyl worldvolume theory, and a
paired opposite-chirality sector gives Dirac.

Therefore the mathematically honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Isolated two-band linear crossing} \Longrightarrow \text{Weyl}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Opposite-chirality valley pairing} \Longrightarrow \text{Dirac}
}
\]

What remains open in TECT is not the projection theorem itself, but the microscopic proof that the
BCC condensate or defect spectrum actually satisfies the hypotheses required by the theorem.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Strengthened Derivation of the Emergent Dirac Equation:
From Defect/Valley Projection to Weyl and Dirac Structure}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The internal orientational sector is described by
\[
P=zz^\dagger \in \mathbb{CP}^2,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
z\sim e^{i\alpha}z.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Established}}
\]

\item A2: Around a defect background \(P_B\), the physical linearized fluctuation operator closes on
the orthogonal rank-two bundle sector
\[
Q=(\mathbb Cz_B)^\perp,
\qquad
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in projector/sigma-model realizations}}
\]

\item A3: The closed operator \(\mathcal L_\perp\) possesses an isolated two-dimensional low-energy
subspace
\[
\mathcal K = \mathrm{span}\{u_1,u_2\}.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs spectral assumption}}
\]

\item A4: The projected low-energy operator is smooth in momentum and has nondegenerate linear
traceless part.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once A3 is assumed}}
\]

\item A5: An opposite-chirality partner sector exists, either from valley doubling or from a
symmetry-related partner localized sector.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable under symmetry assumptions}}
\]

\item A6: The odd-level WZW/Berry term fermionizes the one-defect state.
\[
\boxed{\text{Conditional but structurally established}}
\]

\item A7: The emergent \(SU(3)\) connection acts on the low-energy mode bundle by minimal coupling.
\[
\boxed{\text{Established once the low-energy bundle is identified}}
\]

\item A8: All of A2--A7 hold in the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE without further
assumption.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Why the previous square-root argument is insufficient}

The chain
\[
\text{fermionic statistics}
\;+\;
\text{relativistic dispersion}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Dirac equation}
\]
is not a mathematical derivation by itself. A relativistic quadratic dispersion relation
\[
E^2 = c_*^2 p^2 + M_*^2 c_*^4
\]
does not uniquely imply the Dirac equation, because Klein--Gordon, Proca, Majorana, and Dirac
theories may all share the same quadratic mass shell.

Therefore the correct strategy is to derive a \emph{first-order projected low-energy operator}
from the defect or valley spectrum, and only then identify its statistics via the WZW/Berry
quantization.

\subsection{Defect background and \(Q\)-sector Hessian}

Let
\[
P_B(x;X,\xi)
\]
be a static or slowly moving charge-\(B\) defect background in the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) sector.
Choose a representative
\[
z_B(x), \qquad P_B = z_B z_B^\dagger,
\qquad z_B^\dagger z_B = 1.
\]
Define
\[
Q_B := (\mathbb Cz_B)^\perp,
\qquad
\Pi_B := \mathbf 1_3 - z_B z_B^\dagger.
\]

A physical fluctuation \(\eta\) in background gauge satisfies
\[
z_B^\dagger \eta = 0,
\qquad
\eta \in \Gamma(Q_B).
\]
For projector/sigma-model dynamics, the quadratic fluctuation operator takes the form
\[
\mathcal L_\perp
=
-\nabla_\mu^{(B)}\nabla^{(B)\mu}
+
\mathcal M_B,
\qquad
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q_B)\to\Gamma(Q_B),
\]
where \(\nabla_\mu^{(B)}\) is the induced background covariant derivative on \(Q_B\), and
\(\mathcal M_B\) is a Hermitian endomorphism of \(Q_B\).

This defines the defect-localized spectral problem
\[
\mathcal L_\perp u_n = \lambda_n u_n.
\]

\subsection{Isolated two-mode low-energy sector}

Assume now that \(\mathcal L_\perp\) admits an isolated two-dimensional low-energy sector
\[
\mathcal K = \mathrm{span}\{u_1,u_2\},
\qquad
\dim \mathcal K = 2,
\]
separated by a gap from higher modes. This may arise either:

\begin{enumerate}
\item from a localized defect doublet,
\item from a BCC-shell valley pair at momentum \(k_*\),
\item or from a two-dimensional collective low-energy sector after symmetry reduction.
\end{enumerate}

Let \(\Pi_{\mathcal K}\) denote the projector onto \(\mathcal K\).

We expand the full fluctuation field as
\[
\eta(x,y)
=
\sum_{a=1}^2 \psi_a(y)\,u_a(x)
+
\eta_{\mathrm{high}}(x,y),
\]
where \(x\) denotes transverse/internal coordinates and \(y\) denotes the slowly varying
worldvolume or envelope coordinates. Integrating out the high modes yields an effective operator
for
\[
\psi(y)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi_1(y)\\
\psi_2(y)
\end{pmatrix}
\in \mathbb C^2.
\]

\subsection{Projected effective Hamiltonian}

Let the exact linearized generator of low-energy evolution be denoted abstractly by
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{lin}}(p),
\]
where \(p\) stands for small momentum around the relevant defect worldvolume or around a valley
momentum \(k_*\). Then the projected operator is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\Pi_{\mathcal K}\,\mathcal H_{\mathrm{lin}}(p)\,\Pi_{\mathcal K}
\big|_{\mathcal K}.
\]
Assuming smoothness in \(p\), degenerate perturbation theory yields
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2 + p_i V_i + O(|p|^2),
\]
with Hermitian \(2\times 2\) matrices \(V_i\).

Every Hermitian \(2\times 2\) matrix decomposes as
\[
V_i = v_i^{(0)}\mathbf 1_2 + v_i^{(a)}\sigma^a,
\qquad a=1,2,3.
\]
Hence
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + d_a(p)\sigma^a + O(|p|^2),
\]
where
\[
\varepsilon_0(p)=E_*+p_i v_i^{(0)},
\qquad
d_a(p)=M_{ai}p_i,
\qquad
M_{ai}:=v_i^{(a)}.
\]

\subsection{Weyl reduction theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Projected two-mode Weyl theorem).}
Suppose that the \(3\times 3\) matrix
\[
M_{ai}=v_i^{(a)}
\]
has full rank:
\[
\det M \neq 0.
\]
Then, after an invertible linear change of momentum variables and a unitary basis change in
\(\mathcal K\), the effective operator is of Weyl form:
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
\tilde v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
\tilde v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
\tilde v_3 p_3 \sigma^3
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
with all \(\tilde v_i\neq 0\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
The traceless part of \(H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)\) is
\[
H_{\mathrm{tr}}(p) = (Mp)\cdot \sigma.
\]
If \(\det M\neq 0\), then \(M\) is invertible, so by changing momentum coordinates
\[
p' = Mp,
\]
the traceless part becomes
\[
H_{\mathrm{tr}}(p') = p'_a \sigma^a.
\]
A further \(SU(2)\) basis rotation acts on the Pauli matrices by \(SO(3)\), giving the stated
canonical Weyl form. \(\square\)

Thus an isolated nondegenerate two-mode sector does not merely suggest a first-order operator:
it mathematically forces a Weyl operator.

\subsection{BCC-shell form}

If the low-energy sector is centered near a BCC reciprocal-shell momentum \(G_*\), define
\[
\hat n = \frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\qquad
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\]
Then the Weyl Hamiltonian may be written as
\[
H_+(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
with the nondegeneracy criterion
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0.
\]

\subsection{Partner sector and Dirac doubling}

The Weyl operator above gives only a two-component theory. To obtain a four-component Dirac
field, one needs an opposite-chirality partner sector.

This may arise in either of two ways:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Valley doubling:}
there exists a symmetry-related valley at \(-G_*\) with
\[
H_-(p) = - H_+(-p),
\]
up to unitary equivalence.

\item \textbf{Defect partner doubling:}
the defect spectrum contains a second isolated two-mode sector \(\mathcal K_-\) of opposite
chirality, related to \(\mathcal K_+\) by an antiunitary or discrete symmetry.
\end{enumerate}

Define
\[
\Psi(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi_+(p)\\
\psi_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the combined projected Hamiltonian is
\[
H_D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_+(p) & 0\\
0 & H_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
When the two sectors have opposite chirality, this is equivalent to the Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(p)=\alpha^i p_i + \beta M_*,
\]
after the standard identification of gamma matrices.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Partner-doubling Dirac theorem).}
If a projected Weyl sector \(H_+(p)\) admits an opposite-chirality partner \(H_-(p)\) related by
a symmetry pairing, then the combined low-energy operator is unitarily equivalent to a Dirac
operator:
\[
(i\gamma^\mu \partial_\mu - M_*)\Psi = 0
\]
in covariant form, and
\[
H_D(p)=\alpha^i p_i + \beta M_*
\]
in Hamiltonian form.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Stack the two opposite-chirality Weyl sectors into a four-component field. In a basis adapted to
the chirality pairing, the block Hamiltonian becomes
\[
H_D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
v_i p_i \sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -v_i p_i \sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}
+
M_*
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \mathbf 1_2\\
\mathbf 1_2 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
which is the standard Dirac form after identifying the gamma matrices. \(\square\)

\subsection{Role of the WZW/Berry term: statistics, not operator algebra}

The odd-level WZW/Berry term does not by itself determine the Dirac operator. Instead, it fixes
the statistics of the quantized low-energy sector.

Let the one-defect low-energy Hilbert space be
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}} = L^2(\mathbb R^3)\otimes \mathbb C^4 \otimes \mathbb C^3,
\]
where \(\mathbb C^4\) is the Dirac spinor module obtained from partner doubling, and
\(\mathbb C^3\) is the internal \(SU(3)\) sector.

If the moduli-space quantization yields the phase
\[
e^{i\pi B}=(-1)^B
\]
for the unit defect, then the second-quantized field obeys fermionic anticommutation relations:
\[
\{\hat\Psi_\alpha^m(x),\hat\Psi_\beta^n(y)\}=0,
\qquad
\{\hat\Psi_\alpha^m(x),\hat\Psi_{\beta}^{n\,\dagger}(y)\}
=
\delta_{\alpha\beta}\delta^{mn}\delta^{(3)}(x-y).
\]
Thus the WZW/Berry term supplies \emph{fermionic statistics} for the already-derived Dirac sector.

\subsection{Minimal coupling to the emergent \(SU(3)\) connection}

Let the emergent internal connection act on the low-energy mode bundle in the fundamental
representation:
\[
D_\mu = \partial_\mu - i A_\mu^a T^a.
\]
Projecting the full gauge-covariant fluctuation operator onto the low-energy sector preserves
minimal coupling:
\[
p_\mu \mapsto p_\mu - A_\mu^a T^a.
\]
Therefore the final effective covariant Dirac equation is
\[
\boxed{
\left(i\gamma^\mu D_\mu - M_*\right)\Psi = 0,
\qquad
D_\mu = \partial_\mu - iA_\mu^a T^a.
}
\]

\subsection{Chirality protection and mirror lifting}

If, in addition, the low-energy partner sectors arise from a twisted Dirac operator on the
\(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect core with nonzero index
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) \neq 0,
\]
then one chirality is topologically protected. If extra mirror pairs are present, the previously
established mirror-lifting mechanism removes vectorlike pairs, leaving the net chiral sector equal
to the index.

Thus the logic is:

\[
\text{two-mode projection} \Longrightarrow \text{Weyl},
\]
\[
\text{partner doubling} \Longrightarrow \text{Dirac},
\]
\[
\text{nonzero twisted index} \Longrightarrow \text{chiral protection},
\]
\[
\text{mirror lifting} \Longrightarrow \text{net physical chirality}.
\]

\subsection{Strengthened structural theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Strengthened emergent-Dirac theorem for TECT).}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the BCC-condensed TECT background admits the already-established
\(\mathbb{CP}^2\) orientational sector and emergent \(SU(3)\) connection;

\item around a charge-\(B\) defect background or a BCC-shell valley background, the physical
linearized operator closes on a \(Q\)-sector and admits an isolated two-dimensional low-energy
subspace \(\mathcal K_+\);

\item the projected linearized operator on \(\mathcal K_+\) has nondegenerate linear traceless
part, equivalently a full-rank velocity matrix;

\item there exists an opposite-chirality partner sector \(\mathcal K_-\) related by valley
pairing or defect-sector symmetry;

\item the WZW/Berry quantization of the defect sector is odd-level, so that the quantized
low-energy field is fermionic;

\item the emergent \(SU(3)\) connection acts by minimal coupling on the low-energy mode bundle.
\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared one-defect field is a fermionic Dirac field
\[
\Psi \in S_{\mathrm{Dirac}}\otimes \mathbb C^3
\]
satisfying
\[
\boxed{
\left(i\gamma^\mu D_\mu - M_*\right)\Psi = 0,
\qquad
D_\mu=\partial_\mu - i A_\mu^a T^a.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By assumptions (2)--(3), the projected low-energy operator on \(\mathcal K_+\) is Weyl by
Theorem 1. By assumption (4), doubling with the opposite-chirality partner yields the Dirac
operator by Theorem 2. By assumption (5), second quantization gives fermionic statistics. By
assumption (6), the operator is gauge-covariant under the already-emergent \(SU(3)\) connection.
Hence the infrared effective equation is precisely the covariant Dirac equation displayed above.
\(\square\)

\subsection{What is genuinely proved and what is still open}

What is genuinely proved by the theorem above is not merely a consistency argument from
relativistic dispersion, but a proper projected low-energy derivation:

\[
\text{isolated two-mode sector}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Weyl operator}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Dirac operator after partner doubling}.
\]

What remains open is the purely microscopic spectral problem of proving that the full TECT
condensate PDE actually satisfies the hypotheses of the theorem.

Specifically, it remains to prove directly from the microscopic BCC condensate operator:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the existence of the relevant isolated two-mode sector,
\item the full-rank linear velocity matrix,
\item the opposite-chirality partner pairing,
\item and the exact defect/valley spectral realization of the low-energy bundle.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary of the Strengthened Dirac Derivation}

The previous argument
\[
\text{fermionic statistics}
+
\text{relativistic dispersion}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Dirac equation}
\]
is not mathematically sufficient, because a quadratic relativistic mass shell does not uniquely
determine a Dirac operator.

The strengthened derivation replaces that step with the following chain:

\[
\text{TECT defect or valley background}
\Longrightarrow
\text{closed }Q\text{-sector}
\Longrightarrow
\text{isolated 2-mode low-energy subspace}
\]
\[
\Longrightarrow
\text{projected }2\times2\text{ Hermitian operator}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Weyl Hamiltonian}
\Longrightarrow
\text{opposite-chirality partner doubling}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Dirac Hamiltonian}.
\]

More precisely, if the projected low-energy operator has the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
E_* \mathbf 1_2 + p_i V_i + O(p^2),
\]
then Hermitian \(2\times2\) decomposition gives
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + d_a(p)\sigma^a + O(p^2),
\qquad
d_a(p)=M_{ai}p_i.
\]
If
\[
\det M \neq 0,
\]
the operator is Weyl.
If there is an opposite-chirality partner sector, the doubled four-component theory is Dirac:
\[
\left(i\gamma^\mu D_\mu - M_*\right)\Psi=0.
\]

The odd WZW/Berry term does not generate the Dirac algebra; instead, it ensures that the resulting
low-energy field is fermionic upon second quantization.

Therefore the mathematically strongest honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Dirac emergence in TECT is rigorously reduced to a projected two-mode spectral theorem.}
}
\]

What still remains open is the microscopic proof that the full BCC condensate or defect operator
indeed realizes the required isolated two-mode sector and its opposite-chirality partner.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Assumption-Reduction Audit for the Emergent Dirac Theorem}

\subsection*{Goal}

We revisit the strengthened conditional Dirac theorem and classify each assumption into one of
the following categories:

\[
\boxed{\text{Eliminable}}
\qquad
\boxed{\text{Replaceable by a theorem}}
\qquad
\boxed{\text{Weakable}}
\qquad
\boxed{\text{Currently indispensable}}
\]

The objective is to reduce the theorem to its minimal logically necessary assumptions.

\subsection*{Initial conditional theorem}

The strengthened Dirac theorem previously used the following assumptions:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(A1)] The internal orientational sector is described by
\[
P=zz^\dagger \in \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

\item[(A2)] Around the relevant background, the physical linearized fluctuation operator closes on
the orthogonal bundle sector
\[
Q=(\mathbb Cz_B)^\perp,
\qquad
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q).
\]

\item[(A3)] The operator \(\mathcal L_\perp\) possesses an isolated two-dimensional low-energy
subspace
\[
\mathcal K=\mathrm{span}\{u_1,u_2\}.
\]

\item[(A4)] The projected operator has nondegenerate linear traceless part, i.e.
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=E_*\mathbf 1_2+p_iV_i+O(p^2),
\qquad
\det M\neq 0.
\]

\item[(A5)] There exists an opposite-chirality partner sector.

\item[(A6)] The WZW/Berry term yields fermionic statistics.

\item[(A7)] The emergent \(SU(3)\) connection acts by minimal coupling on the low-energy mode
bundle.

\item[(A8)] All of the above follow from the full microscopic TECT PDE.
\end{enumerate}

We now reduce this list.

\subsection{Reduction of A1}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A1) is not an independent assumption for the Dirac step if the prior CP\(^2\)-sector
construction has already been established.

\paragraph{Reason.}
The Dirac theorem is not responsible for proving the existence of the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)
orientational sector. It uses that sector as already-established input. Therefore A1 should be
treated as prior proven structure, not as an additional assumption of the Dirac step.

\[
\boxed{\text{A1 is removable from the Dirac theorem as an independent hypothesis.}}
\]

\subsection{Reduction of A7}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A7) is replaceable by a theorem once the low-energy subspace is identified as a
subbundle of a gauge-covariant fluctuation bundle.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Suppose the full fluctuation operator is gauge-covariant under the emergent connection:
\[
\mathcal D_\mu = \partial_\mu - i A_\mu^a T^a.
\]
Let \(\Pi_{\mathcal K}\) be the spectral projector onto an isolated low-energy subspace
\(\mathcal K\).
If \(\mathcal K\) is preserved by the gauge-covariant structure, then the projected operator is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}=\Pi_{\mathcal K}\,\mathcal H(\mathcal D)\,\Pi_{\mathcal K}.
\]
Hence every appearance of \(\partial_\mu\) in the low-energy theory is inherited as
\[
\partial_\mu \mapsto \partial_\mu - i A_\mu^a T^a.
\]
Thus minimal coupling is not an extra postulate but a consequence of projecting a gauge-covariant
operator onto a gauge-stable low-energy subspace. \(\square\)

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{\text{A7 can be replaced by a projection-covariance theorem.}}
\]

\subsection{Reduction of A6}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A6) is not needed to derive the Dirac operator itself; it is needed only for fermionic
statistics of the quantized field.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The derivation of the first-order operator proceeds through spectral projection onto a two-mode
sector and decomposition of Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrices:
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2+d_a(p)\sigma^a+O(p^2).
\]
No use of the WZW/Berry term is made in this derivation.
The WZW/Berry term enters only at the stage of quantizing the one-particle sector and assigning
exchange statistics. \(\square\)

Hence:

\[
\boxed{\text{A6 is removable from the operator-level Dirac theorem.}}
\]

It remains necessary only for the stronger statement:

\[
\text{``the emergent Dirac field is fermionic.''}
\]

\subsection{Reduction of A8}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A8) is too strong and should be weakened.

\paragraph{Reason.}
The operator-level Dirac theorem need not assume derivation from the full microscopic TECT PDE.
It suffices that the relevant effective low-energy fluctuation operator exists in the projector or
defect sector and is gauge-covariant. Therefore the theorem should first be stated at the level of
the effective sector, not the full microscopic PDE.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{\text{A8 can be weakened to an effective-sector hypothesis.}}
\]

This does not solve the microscopic problem, but it avoids overloading the Dirac theorem with a
claim it does not need.

\subsection{Reduction of A2}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A2) is partly replaceable by prior geometric theorems, but not fully removable.

\paragraph{Reason.}
The existence of the bundle
\[
Q=(\mathbb Cz_B)^\perp
\]
and the representation of physical tangent fluctuations as \(Q\)-valued fields are already
established geometrically. Also, for projector/sigma-model-type dynamics, the physical Hessian
closes on \(Q\):
\[
\mathcal L_\perp:\Gamma(Q)\to\Gamma(Q).
\]
Thus the statement is already a theorem in that class of models.

However, for a completely general microscopic operator, closure of the \(Q\)-sector is not
automatic: the off-diagonal blocks
\[
P\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}\Pi,
\qquad
\Pi\mathcal L_{\mathrm{lin}}P
\]
need not vanish.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{\text{A2 is replaceable by a theorem in the projector/sigma-model sector,}}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{\text{not removable in full generality.}}
\]

\subsection{Analysis of A4}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A4) is not fully independent once A3 is given, but one nontrivial residue remains.

\paragraph{Reason.}
If an isolated two-dimensional low-energy subspace \(\mathcal K\) exists and the projected
operator is smooth in momentum, then the expansion
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=E_*\mathbf 1_2+p_iV_i+O(p^2)
\]
is automatic by degenerate perturbation theory.

Hence the only genuinely nontrivial part of A4 is not the expansion itself, but the nondegeneracy:
\[
\det M\neq 0.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{\text{The Taylor expansion part of A4 is theorem-level once A3 holds,}}
\]
while
\[
\boxed{\det M\neq 0 \text{ remains an independent spectral condition.}}
\]

\subsection{Analysis of A3}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A3) is currently indispensable.

\paragraph{Reason.}
The Weyl reduction theorem fundamentally depends on the existence of an isolated
two-dimensional low-energy subspace. Without such a sector, the projected operator is not a
\(2\times2\) Hermitian matrix, and the Pauli-matrix reduction cannot even begin.

Could A3 be replaced by topology alone? In general, no.
Could it be replaced by the existence of a nonzero index? Only if a first-order Dirac-type
operator has already been identified, which is precisely what one is trying to derive.

Thus A3 is not presently derivable from the already-established ingredients alone.

\[
\boxed{\text{A3 is currently indispensable.}}
\]

\subsection{Analysis of A5}

\paragraph{Claim.}
Assumption (A5) is currently indispensable for the Dirac, as opposed to Weyl, conclusion.

\paragraph{Reason.}
A single isolated two-mode sector yields a Weyl operator, not a four-component Dirac operator.
To obtain Dirac one needs either:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a symmetry-related opposite valley,
\item or a second localized partner sector of opposite chirality.
\end{enumerate}

Without such a partner, the logically strongest conclusion is only
\[
\text{Weyl emergence}.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{\text{A5 is currently indispensable for Dirac doubling.}}
\]

\subsection{Minimal reduced theorem}

After removing or weakening all reducible assumptions, the operator-level theorem becomes:

\paragraph{Minimal reduced theorem.}
Suppose:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(R1)] the relevant physical fluctuation sector closes on a gauge-covariant bundle sector
(\(Q\)-sector in the projector/sigma-model realization);

\item[(R2)] the closed operator possesses an isolated two-dimensional low-energy subspace
\[
\mathcal K=\mathrm{span}\{u_1,u_2\};
\]

\item[(R3)] the linear traceless velocity matrix on \(\mathcal K\) is nondegenerate:
\[
\det M\neq 0;
\]

\item[(R4)] there exists an opposite-chirality partner sector related by symmetry.
\end{enumerate}

Then the infrared effective operator is Dirac.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By (R2), projection onto \(\mathcal K\) yields a \(2\times2\) Hermitian low-energy operator.
By smoothness, it expands linearly near the crossing point. By (R3), the traceless linear part is
Weyl. By (R4), partner doubling yields the Dirac operator. Gauge covariance is inherited by
projection from (R1). \(\square\)

\subsection{Sharp conclusion of the reduction audit}

Therefore the assumptions split as follows:

\[
\boxed{
\begin{array}{c|c}
\text{Original assumption} & \text{Status after audit} \\
\hline
A1 & \text{remove (already prior structure)} \\
A2 & \text{replace by theorem in projector sector; keep in full generality} \\
A3 & \text{currently indispensable} \\
A4 & \text{reduce to } \det M\neq 0 \\
A5 & \text{currently indispensable for Dirac} \\
A6 & \text{remove from operator theorem; keep for fermionic statistics} \\
A7 & \text{replace by gauge-covariant projection theorem} \\
A8 & \text{weaken to effective-sector existence}
\end{array}
}
\]

Hence the true core of the unresolved problem is not the whole previous theorem, but the pair:

\[
\boxed{\text{(i) isolated two-mode low-energy sector}}
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{(ii) opposite-chirality partner sector}}
\]

together with the residual nondegeneracy condition
\[
\boxed{\det M\neq 0.}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The previous strengthened Dirac theorem contained several assumptions that should not all be kept
as independent hypotheses.

After a rigorous reduction audit:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The CP\(^2\) orientational sector is prior structure, not a new assumption of the Dirac step.
\item The WZW/Berry term is not needed to derive the Dirac operator itself; it is needed only to
fix fermionic statistics after quantization.
\item Minimal coupling to the emergent \(SU(3)\) connection follows from gauge-covariant
projection, once the low-energy subspace is identified.
\item The full microscopic TECT-PDE hypothesis is stronger than necessary for the operator-level
Dirac theorem and should be weakened to an effective-sector hypothesis.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the reduced operator-level theorem depends essentially on only three unresolved
ingredients:

\[
\boxed{
\text{isolated two-dimensional low-energy subspace}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{nondegenerate linear traceless velocity matrix }(\det M\neq 0)
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{opposite-chirality partner sector}
}
\]

Among these, the first and third are presently the most fundamental and have not yet been derived
from the already-established geometric structure alone.

Thus the honest status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{many auxiliary assumptions can be removed or weakened,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{the 2-mode hypothesis and partner-doubling hypothesis remain genuinely open.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Can the Two-Mode Hypothesis be Reduced by Symmetry and Index?}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The orthogonal complement
\[
Q=(\mathbb Cz)^\perp
\]
is a rank-two complex Hermitian bundle.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A2: Physical fluctuations in the projective sector are locally \(Q\)-valued and transform as
\(U(2)\) fundamentals.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A3: The relevant little-group representation at the candidate valley/defect sector is
two-dimensional irreducible.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once the symmetry is specified}}
\]

\item A4: That two-dimensional irrep is spectrally isolated in the infrared.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra spectral assumption}}
\]

\item A5: A Dirac-type operator with nonzero index constrains the internal chiral multiplicity.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable}}
\]

\item A6: Nonzero index alone forces a two-component Weyl sector.
\[
\boxed{\text{False in general}}
\]

\item A7: A symmetry pairs opposite-chirality sectors or opposite valleys.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable under symmetry assumptions}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We want to reduce the unresolved assumption
\[
\dim \mathcal K = 2
\]
as much as possible.

We distinguish three notions that are often conflated:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Fiber dimension:}
the bundle \(Q\) has complex rank \(2\).

\item \textbf{Internal zero-mode multiplicity:}
the number of independent zero modes of an internal Dirac-type operator.

\item \textbf{Low-energy projected Hilbert-space dimension:}
the dimension of the isolated spectral subspace \(\mathcal K\) carrying the Weyl reduction.
\end{enumerate}

Only the first is already unconditional.  
The second can sometimes be controlled by index theory.  
The third is the one needed for the Weyl projection theorem.

\subsection{Why rank-two of \(Q\) does not by itself prove \(\dim \mathcal K=2\)}

Since \(Q\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) is a rank-two complex bundle, any local section may be written as
\[
s=e\,\chi,
\qquad
\chi\in \mathbb C^2.
\]
This shows that the \emph{fiber} carries a two-dimensional complex representation of the local
frame redundancy \(U(2)\).

However, the Hilbert space of fluctuations is not the fiber itself but a function space:
\[
\Gamma(Q), \qquad L^2(Q), \qquad \ker \mathcal L_\perp, \quad \text{etc.}
\]
Its dimension depends on the spectrum of the relevant operator, not just on the fiber rank.

\paragraph{Proposition 1.}
The statement
\[
\mathrm{rank}_{\mathbb C}(Q)=2
\]
does not imply
\[
\dim \mathcal K = 2
\]
for a low-energy eigenspace \(\mathcal K\subset \Gamma(Q)\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Take any Hermitian operator acting on sections of a rank-two bundle over a compact or noncompact
base. Its eigenspaces may have dimension \(0,1,2,3,\dots\), depending on degeneracies and the
geometry of the base. Bundle rank determines the local coefficient space, not the global spectral
multiplicity. \(\square\)

Thus the already-proved \(Q\)-bundle theorem is necessary, but not sufficient, for two-mode
Weyl reduction.

\subsection{Representation-theoretic forcing of a two-dimensional sector}

Now suppose the relevant background admits a symmetry group \(G\), and let \(G_{k_*}\) be the
little group of the candidate valley momentum \(k_*\), or the symmetry group preserving the defect
sector under consideration.

Let the low-energy subspace \(\mathcal K\) carry a representation
\[
\rho: G_{k_*}\to U(\mathcal K).
\]
If \(\mathcal K\) is an irreducible \(G_{k_*}\)-module and \(\rho\) is a two-dimensional irrep,
then automatically
\[
\dim \mathcal K = 2.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Two-dimensional irrep forcing theorem).}
Let \(G_{k_*}\) be the relevant little group of a candidate valley or defect sector.
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the low-energy sector is invariant under \(G_{k_*}\),
\item it transforms irreducibly under \(G_{k_*}\),
\item the corresponding irreducible representation is two-dimensional.
\end{enumerate}

Then
\[
\dim \mathcal K = 2.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is immediate from irreducibility and the definition of representation dimension:
if \(\mathcal K\) carries a two-dimensional irreducible representation, then its vector-space
dimension is exactly two. \(\square\)

So the problem of proving \(\dim\mathcal K=2\) may be reduced to the problem of identifying a
relevant symmetry and showing that the infrared sector is carried by a two-dimensional irrep.

\subsection{What representation theory cannot do by itself}

Even if one identifies a two-dimensional irrep, this alone does not prove that the corresponding
sector is the actual low-energy one, nor that it is spectrally isolated.

\paragraph{Proposition 3.}
Representation theory alone does not imply that a given two-dimensional irrep is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the lowest-energy sector,
\item spectrally isolated from other sectors,
\item associated with a nondegenerate linear crossing.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proof.}
The symmetry decomposition classifies invariant subspaces but does not determine their eigenvalues
or relative spectral ordering. Two different irreps may occur at lower energy, several copies of
the same irrep may appear, or the chosen irrep may mix through accidental degeneracies if not
spectrally isolated. \(\square\)

Hence symmetry can reduce the unresolved assumption from

\[
\text{``there exists an isolated two-dimensional low-energy sector''}
\]
to
\[
\text{``a specific two-dimensional irrep is realized and spectrally isolated.''}
\]

This is a real reduction, but not a complete elimination.

\subsection{Index theory: what it can force}

Now consider a Dirac-type operator
\[
D_Q : \Gamma(S^+\otimes E)\to \Gamma(S^-\otimes E),
\]
where the twisting bundle \(E\) may involve \(Q\) or an associated bundle.

Its index is
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=\dim\ker D_Q^+ - \dim\ker D_Q^-.
\]
If
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=1,
\]
then at least one chiral zero mode exists and the net internal chiral multiplicity is one.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Index reduction of internal multiplicity).}
If \(D_Q\) is Fredholm and
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=1,
\]
and if mirror/vectorlike partners are absent or lifted, then the net physical internal chiral
zero-mode multiplicity is exactly one.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By index theory,
\[
\dim\ker D_Q^+ - \dim\ker D_Q^- = 1.
\]
If all paired mirror modes are removed, the remaining unpaired chiral zero-mode sector has
multiplicity one. \(\square\)

This is very useful, but note carefully what it proves: it constrains the number of independent
\emph{internal chiral zero-mode families}. It does not, by itself, prove that the propagating
worldvolume or valley Hamiltonian is a \(2\times2\) Weyl operator.

\subsection{Why index alone does not prove the two-mode Weyl sector}

A common but incorrect inference is:

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=1
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\dim\mathcal K=2.
\]

This is false in general.

\paragraph{Proposition 5.}
A nonzero Dirac index does not by itself imply that the effective low-energy propagating operator
is a two-component Weyl Hamiltonian.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The index counts the difference of chiral internal zero modes, not the dimension of a projected
momentum-space crossing subspace. To obtain a Weyl Hamiltonian one also needs:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a first-order effective kinetic operator along the propagating directions,
\item an isolated two-state sector on which that operator acts,
\item a full-rank linear Pauli structure.
\end{enumerate}

Index theory alone supplies none of these three statements. \(\square\)

Therefore index reduces multiplicity, but it does not replace the two-mode spectral hypothesis.

\subsection{A useful combined theorem: symmetry + index}

The strongest honest reduction available at present is to combine symmetry and index.

\paragraph{Theorem 6 (Symmetry--index reduction theorem).}
Suppose:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the relevant low-energy sector decomposes into irreducible representations of a symmetry
group \(G\),
\item one candidate infrared sector carries a two-dimensional irrep \(\rho_2\),
\item the internal Dirac-type operator has index \(1\), and mirror modes are lifted,
\item the sector carrying \(\rho_2\) is the unique surviving chiral low-energy family.
\end{enumerate}

Then the unresolved multiplicity problem reduces to a purely spectral isolation statement for that
two-dimensional irrep.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Condition (2) fixes the representation dimension to two. Condition (3) fixes the surviving
internal family count to one. Therefore all multiplicity beyond the desired two-component sector
has been removed except for the possibility that additional spectrally nearby states remain.
Hence the only remaining issue is whether the \(\rho_2\) sector is actually isolated in the
infrared. \(\square\)

This is the sharpest honest reduction we can currently make without performing the explicit
spectral calculation.

\subsection{Partner-sector hypothesis}

Even after the two-mode problem is reduced, one still needs partner doubling to obtain Dirac
rather than merely Weyl.

Suppose there is a discrete or antiunitary symmetry \(\Theta\) such that
\[
\Theta \mathcal H(k)\Theta^{-1}=\mathcal H(\sigma(k)),
\]
where \(\sigma(k)\) maps the relevant valley/sector to its partner and reverses chirality.

\paragraph{Theorem 7 (Symmetry-forced partner theorem).}
If the symmetry \(\Theta\) maps a Weyl sector to an inequivalent sector of opposite chirality, and
if the partner sector is spectrally realized, then the doubled low-energy theory is Dirac.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The original sector gives a Weyl Hamiltonian
\[
H_+(p)=v_i p_i \sigma^i.
\]
The symmetry-related sector has opposite chirality,
\[
H_-(p)\simeq -v_i p_i \sigma^i.
\]
Stacking the two sectors yields the standard Dirac block form. \(\square\)

Again, symmetry reduces the issue, but does not by itself prove that the partner is actually
present and isolated.

\subsection{Minimal remaining assumptions after reduction}

After the symmetry/index audit, the original two-mode hypothesis is reduced but not eliminated.

The remaining irreducible assumptions are:

\[
\boxed{\text{R1: a specific two-dimensional irrep is realized in the infrared}}
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{R2: that irrep is spectrally isolated}}
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{R3: the projected linear traceless velocity matrix has full rank}}
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{R4: an opposite-chirality partner sector is spectrally realized}}
\]

Everything else has either already been proved, or reduced to these residual spectral statements.

\subsection{Sharp logical conclusion}

We therefore have the following hierarchy:

\[
\text{CP}^2 \text{ geometry} \Longrightarrow \mathrm{rank}(Q)=2
\]
does \emph{not} imply
\[
\dim\mathcal K=2.
\]

However,
\[
\text{symmetry with a 2D irrep}
\Longrightarrow
\dim\mathcal K=2
\]
for that irrep sector.

Also,
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=1
\]
does \emph{not} imply Weyl by itself, but it does imply a single surviving internal chiral family
after mirror lifting.

Hence the strongest honest reduction is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{symmetry fixes the two-dimensional representation,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{index fixes the surviving internal family count,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and only spectral isolation/full-rank crossing remain to be checked.}
}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The unresolved hypothesis
\[
\dim\mathcal K=2
\]
cannot be eliminated purely from the already-established CP\(^2\) bundle geometry.

Indeed:

\[
\mathrm{rank}(Q)=2
\]
is a statement about local fiber dimension, whereas
\[
\dim\mathcal K
\]
is a statement about the spectral multiplicity of a low-energy eigenspace. These are not the same.

What can be proved is the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item If the relevant valley or defect sector transforms in a two-dimensional irreducible
representation of the appropriate little symmetry group, then that representation sector has
dimension \(2\).

\item If the relevant internal Dirac-type operator has
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_Q)=1,
\]
and mirror modes are removed, then the net surviving internal chiral family count is \(1\).

\item Symmetry plus index therefore reduces the multiplicity problem dramatically:
the remaining unresolved issue is not arbitrary large degeneracy, but whether the distinguished
two-dimensional irrep is actually realized as an isolated low-energy sector with full-rank linear
crossing.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the strongest honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Symmetry can force the }2\text{-dimensional representation structure;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{index can force a single surviving internal chiral family;}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{spectral isolation and nondegenerate linear crossing still require actual operator analysis.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Little-Group Analysis for the BCC Shell Direction and the Limits of Symmetry Forcing}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The relevant BCC shell direction may be represented by
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Valid group-theoretic setup}}
\]

\item A2: The stabilizer of the oriented direction \(+\hat n\) inside the cubic point group is
\(C_{2v}\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable}}
\]

\item A3: The stabilizer of the unoriented pair \(\{\pm \hat n\}\) is \(D_{2h}\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable}}
\]

\item A4: All ordinary complex irreducible representations of \(C_{2v}\) and \(D_{2h}\) are
one-dimensional.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A5: Therefore ordinary little-group symmetry alone cannot force a two-dimensional irreducible
infrared sector.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A6: A spinorial/double-group route to \(2\)-dimensional irreps exists only if a genuine
spinorial structure has already been independently established.
\[
\boxed{\text{Conditionally true}}
\]

\item A7: The valley-pair sector \(\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\}\) is a natural two-dimensional
candidate space.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A8: Symmetry of the pair alone protects an exact two-fold degeneracy.
\[
\boxed{\text{False in general}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We analyze the shell direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0)
\]
inside the cubic symmetry group, determine its little group, and decide whether ordinary symmetry
can force the desired
\[
\dim\mathcal K=2
\]
required for Weyl reduction.

\subsection{The BCC shell and the \([110]\) direction}

The BCC shell used in the TECT construction is
\[
S_{\mathrm{BCC}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{
(\pm1,\pm1,0),\,
(\pm1,0,\pm1),\,
(0,\pm1,\pm1)
\}.
\]
Choose the representative direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0).
\]
Complete it to an orthonormal frame by
\[
\hat m=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,-1,0),
\qquad
\hat \ell=(0,0,1).
\]
Then
\[
\{\hat n,\hat m,\hat \ell\}
\]
is an orthonormal basis adapted to the shell direction.

\subsection{Oriented stabilizer of \(\hat n\)}

Let \(G_{\hat n}^+\) denote the subgroup of the cubic point group that preserves the oriented
direction \(\hat n\):
\[
G_{\hat n}^+ = \{ R\in O_h \mid R\hat n = \hat n\}.
\]
In the adapted basis \((\hat n,\hat m,\hat \ell)\), any such \(R\) must act trivially on the
\(\hat n\)-axis and orthogonally on the perpendicular plane
\[
\hat n^\perp = \mathrm{span}\{\hat m,\hat \ell\}.
\]
Inside the cubic group, the allowed actions on this plane are precisely the sign flips
\[
(\hat m,\hat \ell)\mapsto (\pm \hat m, \pm \hat \ell)
\]
with determinant \(\pm1\), subject to crystallographic compatibility.

A convenient generating set is:
\[
r_{\hat n}(\pi): (\hat n,\hat m,\hat \ell)\mapsto (\hat n,-\hat m,-\hat \ell),
\]
\[
\sigma_{n\ell}: (\hat n,\hat m,\hat \ell)\mapsto (\hat n,-\hat m,\hat \ell),
\]
\[
\sigma_{nm}: (\hat n,\hat m,\hat \ell)\mapsto (\hat n,\hat m,-\hat \ell).
\]
These satisfy
\[
r_{\hat n}(\pi)=\sigma_{n\ell}\sigma_{nm},
\qquad
\sigma_{n\ell}^2=\sigma_{nm}^2=e,
\qquad
\sigma_{n\ell}\sigma_{nm}=\sigma_{nm}\sigma_{n\ell}.
\]
Hence
\[
G_{\hat n}^+ \cong C_{2v}\cong \mathbb Z_2\times \mathbb Z_2.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1.}
The stabilizer of the oriented BCC shell direction
\[
\hat n=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(1,1,0)
\]
inside the cubic point group is
\[
G_{\hat n}^+ \cong C_{2v}.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Any symmetry preserving \(\hat n\) must preserve the orthogonal decomposition
\[
\mathbb R^3 = \mathbb R\hat n \oplus \hat n^\perp.
\]
Within the cubic group, only the identity, the \(\pi\)-rotation about \(\hat n\), and the two
mirror reflections containing the \(\hat n\)-axis preserve the \([110]\) direction with
orientation. These four commuting elements form
\[
\mathbb Z_2\times \mathbb Z_2 \cong C_{2v}.
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{Pair stabilizer of \(\{\pm \hat n\}\)}

Now let
\[
G_{\hat n}^{\pm} = \{R\in O_h \mid R\hat n = \pm \hat n\}
\]
be the stabilizer of the unoriented pair \(\{\pm \hat n\}\).

Relative to the oriented stabilizer, this enlarged group contains one additional operation that
reverses \(\hat n\), for instance inversion \(I\), or equivalently a suitable twofold rotation
about an axis perpendicular to \(\hat n\). Therefore
\[
G_{\hat n}^{\pm}
=
G_{\hat n}^+ \times \{e,\iota\},
\qquad
\iota^2=e,
\]
so that
\[
G_{\hat n}^{\pm}\cong C_{2v}\times \mathbb Z_2 \cong D_{2h}\cong \mathbb Z_2^3.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2.}
The stabilizer of the shell pair \(\{\pm \hat n\}\) is
\[
G_{\hat n}^{\pm}\cong D_{2h}.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The oriented stabilizer is \(C_{2v}\). Adjoining any symmetry that maps \(\hat n\mapsto -\hat n\)
while preserving the line spanned by \(\hat n\) doubles the group. Since all generators square to
the identity and commute in this line-preserving subgroup, the resulting group is
\[
\mathbb Z_2^3 \cong D_{2h}.
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{Representation-theoretic consequence}

Both \(C_{2v}\) and \(D_{2h}\) are abelian. Therefore every ordinary complex irreducible
representation is one-dimensional.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (No ordinary 2D irrep theorem).}
All ordinary complex irreducible representations of
\[
C_{2v}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
D_{2h}
\]
are one-dimensional.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Every irreducible representation of a finite abelian group over \(\mathbb C\) is
one-dimensional. Since
\[
C_{2v}\cong \mathbb Z_2\times \mathbb Z_2,
\qquad
D_{2h}\cong \mathbb Z_2^3
\]
are both abelian, the claim follows. \(\square\)

\paragraph{Corollary 4.}
Ordinary little-group symmetry of a generic BCC shell direction cannot by itself force an
irreducible two-dimensional low-energy sector:
\[
\dim\mathcal K =2
\]
cannot be deduced from ordinary \(C_{2v}\) or \(D_{2h}\) representation theory alone.

This closes one possible route completely.

\subsection{Why this matters}

The previous hope was:

\[
\text{little group has a 2D irrep}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\dim \mathcal K =2.
\]
For the BCC shell direction \([110]\), this route fails at the ordinary point-group level because
the relevant little groups are abelian.

Thus the unresolved \(2\)-mode hypothesis cannot be reduced through ordinary little-group
irreducibility at a generic shell direction.

\subsection{The valley-pair sector as a two-dimensional candidate space}

Even though the ordinary irreps are one-dimensional, there is still a natural two-dimensional
candidate space coming from the valley pair itself:
\[
\mathcal V_{\pm} = \mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\},
\]
where \(\phi_+\) is localized near \(+G_*\) and \(\phi_-\) near \(-G_*\).

This space is indeed two-dimensional as a vector space. However, under the pair stabilizer, it is
generally a reducible representation. Indeed, in the basis
\[
\phi_{\mathrm{even}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(\phi_+ + \phi_-),
\qquad
\phi_{\mathrm{odd}} =\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(\phi_+ - \phi_-),
\]
the pair-exchange symmetry is diagonal.

\paragraph{Proposition 5.}
The valley-pair space
\[
\mathcal V_{\pm}=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\}
\]
provides a two-dimensional candidate sector, but it is generically reducible under the ordinary
pair-stabilizer symmetry.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The symmetry exchanging \(+G_*\leftrightarrow -G_*\) acts by
\[
\phi_+ \leftrightarrow \phi_-.
\]
Passing to the even/odd basis diagonalizes this action. Hence the two-dimensional pair space
decomposes into one-dimensional invariant subspaces. \(\square\)

Therefore a valley pair gives a \(2\)-dimensional \emph{candidate space}, but not a symmetry-
protected irreducible doublet.

\subsection{Generic intervalley splitting}

Because the pair space is reducible, a symmetry-allowed effective Hamiltonian on it can contain a
mixing term. In the \((\phi_+,\phi_-)\) basis, the most general Hermitian pair Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\pm}
=
E_0 \mathbf 1_2
+
a\,\sigma^3
+
b\,\sigma^1
+
c\,\sigma^2.
\]
If pair-exchange symmetry is present, it may constrain some coefficients, but in general an
intervalley mixing term of the form
\[
m\,\sigma^1
\]
or its equivalent is not forbidden. Then the two energies split:
\[
E_{\pm}=E_0\pm |m|.
\]

\paragraph{Proposition 6.}
The existence of the pair sector \(\mathcal V_{\pm}\) does not by itself protect an exact
two-fold degeneracy.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Because the representation is reducible, symmetry-allowed off-diagonal mixing generically
diagonalizes into nondegenerate even/odd combinations. Hence degeneracy is not protected unless an
additional symmetry forbids the mixing term. \(\square\)

This is another crucial closure:
\[
\boxed{
\pm G_* \text{ pair } \neq \text{ automatically protected 2-mode Weyl sector.}
}
\]

\subsection{Double-group / spinorial route}

There is, however, one important caveat. If the relevant low-energy object already carries a
spinorial or projective representation of the little group, then one must pass to the double
group. The double cover of the rotational \(D_2\) subgroup is the quaternion group \(Q_8\), which
does admit a genuine \(2\)-dimensional irreducible representation. More generally, spinorial
double groups of \(C_{2v}\) or \(D_{2h}\) can support \(2\)-dimensional irreps.

But this route is logically delicate.

\paragraph{Proposition 7.}
A double-group \(2\)-dimensional irrep may provide the desired \(2\)-mode structure only after an
independent spinorial/fermionic structure has already been established.

\paragraph{Reason.}
If one uses the spinorial double group to \emph{derive} the two-component sector while the
spinorial nature itself is still being derived from that same sector, the reasoning becomes
circular.

Therefore the double-group route is legitimate only in the following order:
\[
\text{independent fermionic/spinorial structure}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{double-group representation analysis}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{2D irrep}.
\]

It cannot be used as the first, unconditional proof of the two-mode hypothesis.

\subsection{Net result of the little-group audit}

We can now state the exact conclusion.

\paragraph{Theorem 8 (Sharp little-group conclusion for the BCC shell).}
For the BCC shell direction
\[
G_* \parallel (1,1,0),
\]
the ordinary oriented little group is
\[
C_{2v},
\]
the ordinary pair stabilizer is
\[
D_{2h},
\]
and both have only one-dimensional ordinary complex irreducible representations.

Hence the unresolved \(2\)-mode hypothesis cannot be reduced to ordinary little-group
irreducibility.

The only remaining symmetry-based possibilities are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a reducible \(2\)-dimensional valley-pair space \(\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\}\),
which still requires an extra symmetry to forbid splitting;

\item a genuine \(2\)-dimensional spinorial irrep of the double group, which is legitimate only
after an independently established spinorial/fermionic structure.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

Let
\[
G_* \parallel (1,1,0)
\]
be a representative BCC shell direction.

Then:

\[
G_{\hat n}^+ = \{R\in O_h \mid R\hat n=\hat n\}\cong C_{2v},
\]
and
\[
G_{\hat n}^{\pm} = \{R\in O_h \mid R\hat n=\pm \hat n\}\cong D_{2h}.
\]

Since both groups are abelian, all ordinary complex irreducible representations are
one-dimensional:

\[
\boxed{
\text{ordinary little-group representation theory does not force } \dim\mathcal K=2.
}
\]

Therefore the generic BCC shell direction does \emph{not} support the hoped-for reduction
\[
\text{``2D irrep } \Rightarrow \dim\mathcal K=2\text{''}
\]
at the ordinary cubic-symmetry level.

What remains are only two symmetry-based candidate mechanisms:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the valley-pair space
\[
\mathcal V_{\pm}=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\},
\]
which is two-dimensional but generically reducible and not automatically degenerate;

\item the spinorial/double-group route, which may provide a genuine \(2\)-dimensional irrep, but
only after an independently justified spinorial structure is already available.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the strongest honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The ordinary BCC shell little group does not solve the 2-mode problem.}
}
\]

The unresolved issue is now sharply reduced to one of the following:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either an additional symmetry forbids intervalley splitting,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuine spinorial/double-group structure must be established independently.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Symmetry Classification of Allowed and Forbidden Mass Terms
in the Valley-Pair Sector}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The low-energy valley-pair space is
\[
\mathcal V_\pm=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\},
\]
where \(\phi_+\) and \(\phi_-\) are modes localized near \(+G_*\) and \(-G_*\), respectively.
\[
\boxed{\text{Valid definition}}
\]

\item A2: Any Hermitian effective Hamiltonian on \(\mathcal V_\pm\) is of the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)=h_0(p)\mathbf 1+h_a(p)\sigma^a.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A3: Discrete symmetries can be represented on \(\mathcal V_\pm\) by unitary or antiunitary
operators after a basis choice.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable}}
\]

\item A4: The symmetry constraints on \(h_a(p)\) can be classified algebraically.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A5: The corresponding symmetries are actually realized in the full microscopic TECT BCC
background.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]

\item A6: Forbidding a mass term automatically yields a full Weyl/Dirac cone.
\[
\boxed{\text{False in general}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{General valley-pair Hamiltonian}

Let
\[
\mathcal V_\pm=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\}
\]
with ordered basis
\[
\Phi=
\begin{pmatrix}
\phi_+\\
\phi_-
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
A general Hermitian effective Hamiltonian depending on small momentum \(p\) around the pair sector is
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)=h_0(p)\mathbf 1+h_1(p)\sigma^1+h_2(p)\sigma^2+h_3(p)\sigma^3,
\]
with real-valued coefficients \(h_\mu(p)\).

Interpretationally:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(h_0\mathbf 1\) is an overall shift/tilt term,
\item \(h_3\sigma^3\) measures valley imbalance,
\item \(h_1\sigma^1+h_2\sigma^2\) mixes the two valleys.
\end{itemize}

At \(p=0\), a ``mass/splitting term'' means any nonzero traceless constant term
\[
m_a\sigma^a.
\]
Such a term generically gaps or splits the pair sector.

\subsection{Valley exchange (inversion-like) symmetry}

Suppose there is a unitary symmetry \(P\) exchanging the two valleys:
\[
P\phi_+=\phi_-,
\qquad
P\phi_-=\phi_+.
\]
In the chosen basis,
\[
P=\sigma^1.
\]
If the symmetry acts on momentum by
\[
p\mapsto -p,
\]
then invariance means
\[
P\,H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)\,P^{-1}=H_{\mathrm{val}}(-p).
\]
Using
\[
\sigma^1 \sigma^1 \sigma^1 = \sigma^1,
\qquad
\sigma^1 \sigma^2 \sigma^1 = -\sigma^2,
\qquad
\sigma^1 \sigma^3 \sigma^1 = -\sigma^3,
\]
we obtain
\[
h_0(p)=h_0(-p),
\]
\[
h_1(p)=h_1(-p),
\qquad
h_2(p)=-h_2(-p),
\qquad
h_3(p)=-h_3(-p).
\]

Therefore, at \(p=0\),
\[
h_2(0)=h_3(0)=0,
\]
but
\[
h_1(0)
\]
is allowed.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Valley-exchange constraint).}
If the pair-exchange symmetry is represented by
\[
P=\sigma^1
\]
and sends \(p\to -p\), then the allowed constant terms at \(p=0\) are
\[
h_0(0)\mathbf 1 + h_1(0)\sigma^1,
\]
while the terms
\[
\sigma^2,\qquad \sigma^3
\]
are forbidden at \(p=0\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from the parity constraints above. \(\square\)

\paragraph{Consequence.}
Valley exchange symmetry alone does \emph{not} protect the pair degeneracy, because the mixing term
\[
m\,\sigma^1
\]
is symmetry-allowed.

Thus:
\[
\boxed{
\text{inversion-like valley exchange alone does not forbid intervalley splitting.}
}
\]

\subsection{Time-reversal symmetry in the spinless valley basis}

Assume a spinless antiunitary time-reversal symmetry
\[
T=K,
\]
where \(K\) is complex conjugation, acting by
\[
T\,H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)\,T^{-1}=H_{\mathrm{val}}(-p).
\]
Since
\[
K\sigma^1K^{-1}=\sigma^1,\qquad
K\sigma^2K^{-1}=-\sigma^2,\qquad
K\sigma^3K^{-1}=\sigma^3,
\]
we get
\[
h_0(p)=h_0(-p),
\qquad
h_1(p)=h_1(-p),
\qquad
h_2(p)=-h_2(-p),
\qquad
h_3(p)=h_3(-p).
\]
Hence at \(p=0\),
\[
h_2(0)=0,
\]
but
\[
h_1(0),\ h_3(0)
\]
are allowed.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Spinless time-reversal constraint).}
If
\[
T=K
\]
acts on the valley-pair space, then at \(p=0\) the only forbidden traceless constant term is
\[
\sigma^2,
\]
while
\[
\sigma^1,\qquad \sigma^3
\]
remain allowed.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from antiunitary conjugation by \(K\). \(\square\)

Therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\text{spinless time-reversal alone is also insufficient to protect the valley degeneracy.}
}
\]

\subsection{Combined \(PT\) symmetry}

Combine the two previous symmetries:
\[
PT = \sigma^1 K.
\]
Then
\[
(PT)\,H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)\,(PT)^{-1}=H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)
\]
if both \(P\) and \(T\) reverse momentum, so the combination leaves \(p\) unchanged.

Using
\[
\sigma^1 (\sigma^1)^* \sigma^1 = \sigma^1,
\qquad
\sigma^1 (\sigma^2)^* \sigma^1 = \sigma^2,
\qquad
\sigma^1 (\sigma^3)^* \sigma^1 = -\sigma^3,
\]
one obtains
\[
h_0(p)\ \text{arbitrary},
\qquad
h_1(p)\ \text{arbitrary},
\qquad
h_2(p)\ \text{arbitrary},
\qquad
h_3(p)=0.
\]
So \(PT\) kills only the \(\sigma^3\) term.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (\(PT\)-constraint).}
If
\[
PT=\sigma^1 K
\]
acts on the valley-pair space and leaves \(p\) invariant, then the term
\[
h_3(p)\sigma^3
\]
is forbidden, but
\[
h_1(p)\sigma^1,\qquad h_2(p)\sigma^2
\]
are allowed.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Direct computation. \(\square\)

Hence:
\[
\boxed{
PT \text{ removes valley imbalance } \sigma^3 \text{ but still allows intervalley mixing.}
}
\]

\subsection{Chiral / sublattice-like symmetry}

Now consider a unitary chiral symmetry \(\Gamma\) satisfying
\[
\Gamma^2=\mathbf 1,
\qquad
\Gamma H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)\Gamma^{-1}=-H_{\mathrm{val}}(p).
\]
A natural valley-basis choice is
\[
\Gamma=\sigma^3.
\]
Then
\[
\sigma^3 \mathbf 1 \sigma^3 = \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\sigma^3 \sigma^1 \sigma^3 = -\sigma^1,
\qquad
\sigma^3 \sigma^2 \sigma^3 = -\sigma^2,
\qquad
\sigma^3 \sigma^3 \sigma^3 = \sigma^3.
\]
The anticommutation condition implies
\[
h_0(p)=0,
\qquad
h_3(p)=0,
\]
while
\[
h_1(p),\ h_2(p)
\]
are allowed.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Chiral symmetry constraint).}
If
\[
\Gamma=\sigma^3,\qquad \Gamma H\Gamma^{-1}=-H,
\]
then the valley-pair Hamiltonian is constrained to the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)=h_1(p)\sigma^1+h_2(p)\sigma^2.
\]
In particular, both
\[
\mathbf 1
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\sigma^3
\]
are forbidden.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from the anticommutation relations above. \(\square\)

This is the first symmetry that genuinely forbids the usual constant splitting term
\[
m\,\sigma^3.
\]
However, it still allows
\[
m_1\,\sigma^1 + m_2\,\sigma^2
\]
unless further momentum/parity conditions force these coefficients to vanish at the expansion
point.

\subsection{Particle-hole-like antiunitary symmetry}

Consider an antiunitary particle-hole-like symmetry \(C\) satisfying
\[
C\,H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)\,C^{-1}=-H_{\mathrm{val}}(-p).
\]
Take for instance
\[
C=\sigma^1 K.
\]
Using the same algebra as above, we find
\[
h_0(p)=-h_0(-p),
\qquad
h_1(p)=-h_1(-p),
\qquad
h_2(p)=-h_2(-p),
\qquad
h_3(p)=+h_3(-p).
\]
Thus at \(p=0\),
\[
h_0(0)=h_1(0)=h_2(0)=0,
\]
but
\[
h_3(0)
\]
is allowed.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Particle-hole-like constraint).}
For
\[
C=\sigma^1 K
\]
with
\[
C H(p) C^{-1}=-H(-p),
\]
the only allowed constant traceless term at \(p=0\) is
\[
m\,\sigma^3.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Direct computation. \(\square\)

So a particle-hole-like symmetry by itself does not protect the valley pair either.

\subsection{Combined chiral and valley-exchange symmetry}

Now combine:

\begin{enumerate}
\item valley exchange:
\[
P=\sigma^1,\qquad P H(p) P^{-1}=H(-p),
\]
\item chiral symmetry:
\[
\Gamma=\sigma^3,\qquad \Gamma H(p)\Gamma^{-1}=-H(p).
\]
\end{enumerate}

From chiral symmetry alone:
\[
H(p)=h_1(p)\sigma^1+h_2(p)\sigma^2.
\]
Applying \(P\)-symmetry then gives
\[
h_1(p)=h_1(-p),
\qquad
h_2(p)=-h_2(-p).
\]
Hence at \(p=0\),
\[
h_2(0)=0,
\]
but
\[
h_1(0)
\]
is still allowed.

\paragraph{Corollary 6.}
The combination of valley exchange and chiral symmetry still allows a constant term
\[
m\,\sigma^1.
\]
Therefore it does not completely protect the two-fold degeneracy.

So even this combination is not enough.

\subsection{When is the pair degeneracy actually protected?}

To forbid \emph{all} traceless constant terms
\[
m_a \sigma^a,
\]
one needs symmetry constraints strong enough to eliminate \(\sigma^1,\sigma^2,\sigma^3\)
simultaneously.

But in a pure \(2\times 2\) valley-pair space, this is extremely restrictive. In fact, if all
traceless constant terms are forbidden, the Hamiltonian at \(p=0\) reduces to
\[
H(0)=E_0\mathbf 1,
\]
so degeneracy is protected there. However, the linearized momentum dependence must still produce a
full-rank map to the Pauli matrices in order to yield a Weyl cone.

Thus:

\paragraph{Proposition 7.}
Symmetry-based protection of the valley-pair degeneracy at \(p=0\) is a necessary condition for a
Weyl/Dirac reduction from the pair sector, but it is not sufficient.

\paragraph{Proof.}
A protected degeneracy guarantees only that \(H(0)\) is proportional to the identity. A Weyl point
requires additionally that the linearized traceless coefficients span the full Pauli algebra:
\[
H(p)-H(0)=M_{ai}p_i\sigma^a+O(p^2),
\qquad
\det M\neq 0.
\]
Without this, one may obtain only a line node, quadratic touching, or no conical structure at all.
\(\square\)

\subsection{Sharp classification table}

At \(p=0\), the symmetry constraints may be summarized as follows:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c}
\text{Symmetry} & \text{Forbidden traceless constant terms} \\
\hline
P=\sigma^1 & \sigma^2,\ \sigma^3 \\
T=K & \sigma^2 \\
PT=\sigma^1 K & \sigma^3 \\
\Gamma=\sigma^3 & \sigma^3 \text{ and } \mathbf 1 \text{ (for all }p) \\
C=\sigma^1 K & \sigma^1,\ \sigma^2 \text{ and } \mathbf 1 \text{ at }p=0
\end{array}
\]

But no single one of these standard symmetries forbids all three Pauli masses
simultaneously in the \(2\times 2\) valley-pair sector.

\subsection{Main conclusion}

We may now state the precise theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 8 (No standard-symmetry protection theorem for pure valley pair).}
Consider the pure two-dimensional valley-pair sector
\[
\mathcal V_\pm=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\}
\]
with general Hermitian Hamiltonian
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)=h_0(p)\mathbf 1+h_a(p)\sigma^a.
\]
Then none of the standard discrete symmetries
\[
P,\quad T,\quad PT,\quad \Gamma,\quad C
\]
acting in the natural \(2\times2\) valley basis is, by itself, sufficient to forbid all traceless
constant mass/splitting terms.

Therefore a pure valley-pair sector does not generically provide a symmetry-protected Weyl node.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The claim follows from the explicit algebraic classifications above: in every case at least one
traceless Pauli term remains symmetry-allowed. \(\square\)

Hence the valley-pair space is a useful candidate sector, but not by itself the final solution of
the 2-mode problem.

\subsection{What this means for TECT}

The sharp conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{ordinary valley-pair symmetry is too weak to guarantee an exact protected two-mode Weyl
sector.}
}
\]

Therefore, if TECT is to obtain a robust Dirac sector, one of the following stronger mechanisms is
needed:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an additional symmetry stronger than the standard \(P,T,PT,\Gamma,C\) actions on the pure
pair basis;

\item an enlarged low-energy space in which the valley label combines with an independent internal
two-mode structure;

\item a genuine first-order defect/valley operator whose zero-mode structure is protected by index
theory before projection to the pair basis.
\end{enumerate}

This is a real reduction of the problem: the obstacle is no longer vague, but identified
precisely as the insufficiency of standard \(2\times2\) valley-pair symmetry constraints.

\section{Final Summary}

On the two-dimensional valley-pair space
\[
\mathcal V_\pm=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\},
\]
the most general Hermitian effective Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(p)=h_0(p)\mathbf 1+h_a(p)\sigma^a.
\]

A symmetry can protect a degeneracy only if it forbids the constant traceless Pauli terms
\[
m_a \sigma^a.
\]

The explicit algebraic classification shows:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Valley-exchange/inversion-like symmetry \(P=\sigma^1\) forbids \(\sigma^2,\sigma^3\), but
still allows \(\sigma^1\).

\item Spinless time-reversal \(T=K\) forbids only \(\sigma^2\), leaving \(\sigma^1,\sigma^3\)
allowed.

\item \(PT=\sigma^1K\) forbids \(\sigma^3\), but still allows \(\sigma^1,\sigma^2\).

\item Chiral symmetry \(\Gamma=\sigma^3\) enforces
\[
H=h_1\sigma^1+h_2\sigma^2,
\]
thus forbidding \(\mathbf 1\) and \(\sigma^3\), but not \(\sigma^1,\sigma^2\).

\item Particle-hole-like symmetry \(C=\sigma^1K\) forbids \(\sigma^1,\sigma^2\) at \(p=0\), but
still allows \(\sigma^3\).
\end{enumerate}

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No standard single symmetry on the pure }2\times2\text{ valley-pair space forbids all
mass/splitting terms.}
}
\]

Hence a pure valley-pair sector is not generically a symmetry-protected Weyl sector.

The honest implication for TECT is that a robust Dirac/Weyl emergence must rely on something
stronger than ordinary valley-pair symmetry alone, such as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{an additional internal two-mode structure,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\text{a genuine first-order defect/valley operator with protected zero modes.}
}
\]

Thus the remaining problem is now sharply localized: it is not merely to find a two-dimensional
pair space, but to identify the stronger structure that forbids its symmetry-allowed mass terms
and generates a full-rank first-order crossing.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{The \(4\)-Dimensional Internal\(\otimes\)Valley Space as the Natural Carrier of the Dirac Algebra}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The internal low-energy sector carries a complex \(2\)-dimensional space
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
coming from the rank-two bundle \(Q\to \mathbb{CP}^2\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A2: The valley pair \(\{\pm G_*\}\) defines a candidate complex \(2\)-dimensional space
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}=\mathrm{span}\{\phi_+,\phi_-\}.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Valid as a candidate sector}}
\]

\item A3: Therefore the natural candidate low-energy space is
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable as a construction}}
\]

\item A4: The \(4\)-dimensional space above admits a representation of the Dirac/Clifford algebra.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A5: The actual projected TECT low-energy Hamiltonian closes on that Clifford subalgebra.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra spectral/dynamical input}}
\]

\item A6: Discrete symmetries on the tensor-product space constrain the allowed mass terms.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable algebraically}}
\]

\item A7: Those symmetries are realized by the full microscopic TECT background.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Why the pure valley-pair space is insufficient}

The pure valley-pair space
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}
\]
admits only the Pauli basis
\[
\mathbf 1,\sigma^1,\sigma^2,\sigma^3.
\]
As shown previously, standard discrete symmetries acting on this \(2\times2\) space do not forbid
all traceless constant terms simultaneously. Thus the pure pair sector does not generically
provide a symmetry-protected Weyl or Dirac structure.

This motivates enlarging the low-energy candidate space by an independent internal doublet:
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}.
\]

\subsection{Canonical operator basis on the tensor-product space}

Let
\[
\tau^a,\qquad a=1,2,3,
\]
denote Pauli matrices acting on \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\), and let
\[
\sigma^a,\qquad a=1,2,3,
\]
act on \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}\).

Then every Hermitian operator on
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}
\]
can be expanded uniquely as
\[
H
=
c_{00}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{a0}\,\tau^a\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{0b}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b
+
c_{ab}\,\tau^a\otimes \sigma^b,
\]
with all coefficients real.

This gives the full Hermitian basis of dimension
\[
1+3+3+9=16.
\]

\subsection{Clifford generators inside the \(4\times4\) operator algebra}

We now show that this \(4\)-dimensional space naturally supports a Dirac algebra.

Define
\[
\alpha^1 = \tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,\qquad
\alpha^2 = \tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\qquad
\alpha^3 = \tau^1\otimes \sigma^3,
\]
and
\[
\beta = \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]
Also define
\[
\gamma^5 := -i\alpha^1\alpha^2\alpha^3\beta = \tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Clifford realization theorem).}
The matrices defined above satisfy
\[
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\,\mathbf 1_4,
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\beta\}=0,
\qquad
\beta^2=\mathbf 1_4.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
First,
\[
\alpha^i\alpha^j
=
(\tau^1\tau^1)\otimes (\sigma^i\sigma^j)
=
\mathbf 1\otimes (\sigma^i\sigma^j).
\]
Hence
\[
\alpha^i\alpha^j+\alpha^j\alpha^i
=
\mathbf 1\otimes (\sigma^i\sigma^j+\sigma^j\sigma^i)
=
2\delta^{ij}\,\mathbf 1_4.
\]
Next,
\[
\alpha^i\beta+\beta\alpha^i
=
(\tau^1\tau^3+\tau^3\tau^1)\otimes \sigma^i
=
0,
\]
because Pauli matrices anticommute for distinct indices. Finally,
\[
\beta^2=(\tau^3)^2\otimes \mathbf 1=\mathbf 1_4.
\]
\(\square\)

Thus the tensor-product space is the minimal natural carrier of a \(3+1\)-dimensional Dirac
Hamiltonian.

\subsection{Interpretation of the tensor factors}

The above realization is not unique, but it has a suggestive interpretation:

\begin{itemize}
\item the internal \(Q\)-doublet sector supplies the \(\tau\)-structure that can anticommute with
the valley sector;
\item the valley pair supplies the \(\sigma\)-structure associated with the momentum-dependent
crossing.
\end{itemize}

In this sense, the internal doublet and the valley doublet together provide exactly enough algebra
to support a Dirac operator, whereas either factor alone is too small or too weakly constrained.

\subsection{General projected Hamiltonian on \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\)}

A general Hermitian effective Hamiltonian on the \(4\)-dimensional candidate space is
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
h_{00}(p)\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
h_{a0}(p)\,\tau^a\otimes \mathbf 1
+
h_{0b}(p)\,\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b
+
h_{ab}(p)\,\tau^a\otimes \sigma^b.
\]
To obtain a Dirac form, one needs a restriction to the Clifford subalgebra generated by
\[
\alpha^i=\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i,
\qquad
\beta=\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]
That is, one wants
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\,\mathbf 1_4
+
v_i p_i\,(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i)
+
m\,(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1)
+\cdots.
\]

\paragraph{Proposition 2.}
The tensor-product space \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}\) is
sufficient to support a Dirac Hamiltonian, but a generic Hermitian operator on that space is not
of Dirac form.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Sufficiency follows from Theorem 1. Non-genericity follows because the full Hermitian algebra has
dimension \(16\), while the Dirac Hamiltonian occupies only a small subspace spanned by
\[
\mathbf 1_4,\quad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,\quad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\quad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^3,\quad \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]
A generic Hermitian operator contains many additional allowed terms. \(\square\)

Therefore the remaining problem is not algebraic possibility, but dynamical projection:
why should the microscopic or effective TECT Hamiltonian select precisely this Clifford sector?

\subsection{Mass-term classification in the \(4\times4\) space}

Relative to the chosen kinetic generators
\[
\alpha^i=\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i,
\]
a mass term is any Hermitian matrix \(M\) satisfying
\[
\{M,\alpha^i\}=0
\qquad
\text{for all }i=1,2,3.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Mass classification theorem).}
Within the \(4\times4\) tensor-product algebra, the Hermitian matrices that anticommute with all
three \(\alpha^i=\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i\) are precisely
\[
M = a\,(\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1) + b\,(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1),
\]
with \(a,b\in\mathbb R\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Write the most general Hermitian matrix as
\[
M=c_{00}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1+c_{a0}\,\tau^a\otimes \mathbf 1
+c_{0b}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b+c_{ab}\,\tau^a\otimes \sigma^b.
\]
We require
\[
\{M,\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i\}=0
\qquad
\forall i.
\]
A direct basis computation shows:

\begin{itemize}
\item any term containing \(\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1\) or \(\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1\) commutes,
not anticommutes;
\item any term containing \(\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b\) or \(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^b\) fails to
anticommute for all \(i\);
\item terms \(\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1\) and \(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1\) anticommute with
\(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i\) because
\[
\{\tau^2,\tau^1\}=0,\qquad \{\tau^3,\tau^1\}=0,
\]
while \(\mathbf 1\) commutes with all \(\sigma^i\).
\end{itemize}

No other basis elements satisfy the full set of anticommutation conditions simultaneously.
\(\square\)

Thus the \(4\times4\) space already has a two-dimensional mass space.
This is very different from the pure \(2\times2\) valley pair, where all Pauli terms compete on
equal footing.

\subsection{Chiral symmetry in the \(4\times4\) space}

Take
\[
\Gamma = \tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1 = \gamma^5.
\]
Then
\[
\{\Gamma,\alpha^i\}=0,
\qquad
[\Gamma,\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1]=0,
\qquad
\{\Gamma,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1\}=0.
\]

If one imposes the chiral condition
\[
\Gamma H \Gamma^{-1} = -H,
\]
then any term commuting with \(\Gamma\) is forbidden, while anticommuting terms are allowed.

\paragraph{Corollary 4.}
If
\[
\Gamma=\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
is an exact chiral symmetry, then the mass term
\[
\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
is forbidden, while
\[
\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
remains allowed.

Thus chiral symmetry removes one of the two possible Dirac masses, but not both.

\subsection{Valley-exchange symmetry in the \(4\times4\) space}

Let valley exchange be represented by
\[
P_v = \mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^1.
\]
Then
\[
P_v(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1)P_v^{-1} = \tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,
\]
\[
P_v(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2)P_v^{-1} = -\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,
\]
\[
P_v(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^3)P_v^{-1} = -\tau^1\otimes \sigma^3.
\]
The mass terms
\[
\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1,\qquad \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
are both invariant under \(P_v\).

\paragraph{Corollary 5.}
Valley exchange alone does not forbid either of the Dirac masses in the \(4\times4\) tensor-product
space.

So valley exchange helps organize the kinetic structure, but does not solve the mass problem.

\subsection{Internal parity between the two \(Q\)-modes}

Suppose now there is an internal symmetry acting as
\[
P_{\mathrm{int}}=\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
on the \(Q\)-doublet factor. Then
\[
P_{\mathrm{int}}(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1)P_{\mathrm{int}}^{-1}
=
-\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
\[
P_{\mathrm{int}}(\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1)P_{\mathrm{int}}^{-1}
=
-\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]
Hence both Dirac masses are odd under this symmetry.

\paragraph{Theorem 6 (Internal-exchange protection theorem).}
If the low-energy theory has an exact internal exchange symmetry
\[
P_{\mathrm{int}}=\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
and the Hamiltonian is invariant under it, then both Dirac masses
\[
\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1,\qquad \tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
are forbidden.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Both masses change sign under conjugation by \(\tau^1\), hence cannot appear in an invariant
Hamiltonian. \(\square\)

This is the first strong algebraic result of the \(4\times4\) analysis:
an exact symmetry exchanging the two internal \(Q\)-modes would forbid the full Dirac mass space.

However, whether such an internal exchange symmetry exists in TECT is not yet established.

\subsection{Sharp conclusion of the \(4\times4\) audit}

We may now state the central result.

\paragraph{Theorem 7 (Why the \(4\)-dimensional space is the right target).}
The tensor-product space
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}
\]
is the minimal natural low-energy candidate space that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item admits a representation of the \(3+1\)-dimensional Dirac/Clifford algebra;
\item contains a genuine Dirac kinetic sector
\[
v_i p_i(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i);
\]
\item allows a nontrivial mass classification;
\item can, under additional internal symmetry, forbid the entire Dirac mass space.
\end{enumerate}

By contrast, the pure valley-pair \(2\times2\) space is too small and too weakly constrained to
do so.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Items (1)--(3) follow from Theorems 1 and 3. Item (4) follows from Theorem 6. The insufficiency
of the pure valley-pair space was proved earlier. \(\square\)

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is proved:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the \(4\)-dimensional internal\(\otimes\)valley space is algebraically sufficient for Dirac;
\item the pure valley-pair space is not;
\item the full Dirac mass space in this \(4\times4\) setting is exactly two-dimensional;
\item an internal exchange symmetry would forbid all Dirac masses.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item whether the actual TECT low-energy projected operator closes on this tensor-product space;
\item whether its kinetic term really takes the form
\[
v_i p_i(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i);
\]
\item whether an exact internal symmetry exchanging the two \(Q\)-modes exists;
\item whether the necessary low-energy sector is spectrally isolated in the microscopic theory.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

The pure valley-pair space
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}
\]
is insufficient to produce a robust symmetry-protected Weyl/Dirac sector.

The natural next candidate is the tensor-product space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}},
\]
where \(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\) comes from the rank-two \(Q\)-bundle and
\(\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}\) from the valley pair.

On this \(4\)-dimensional space one can define
\[
\alpha^i=\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i,
\qquad
\beta=\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
which satisfy the Dirac/Clifford relations
\[
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2\delta^{ij},
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\beta\}=0,
\qquad
\beta^2=1.
\]

Thus this space is the minimal natural carrier of a \(3+1\)-dimensional Dirac operator.

The complete mass classification relative to these kinetic generators is:
\[
M \text{ anticommutes with all } \alpha^i
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
M=a(\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1)+b(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1).
\]
Hence the Dirac mass space is exactly two-dimensional.

Moreover, if an exact internal exchange symmetry
\[
P_{\mathrm{int}}=\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
is present, then both masses are forbidden, since both are odd under this symmetry.

Therefore the sharp honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The }4\text{-dimensional internal}\otimes\text{valley space is the first setting in which a
genuine Dirac algebra and a nontrivial full mass-protection mechanism can appear.}
}
\]

What remains open is not the algebraic possibility, but the dynamical realization:
whether the actual TECT low-energy operator projects onto this space, selects the Dirac kinetic
sector, and realizes the required internal exchange symmetry.

%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Complete Symmetry Audit on the \(4\times4\) Internal\(\otimes\)Valley Space:
Why Standard Factorized Symmetries Do Not Isolate the Pure Dirac Kinetic Sector}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The candidate infrared space is
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Valid candidate construction}}
\]

\item A2: A general Hermitian operator on \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\) admits the expansion
\[
H
=
c_{00}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{a0}\,\tau^a\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{0b}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b
+
c_{ab}\,\tau^a\otimes \sigma^b.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A3: Internal exchange and chiral symmetry reduce \(H\) to
\[
H=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0\mathbf 1+b_i\sigma^i\right).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A4: Standard factorized valley/discrete symmetries can eliminate the scalar
\(\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1\) while preserving all three kinetic generators
\(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i\).
\[
\boxed{\text{False in general}}
\]
