\item A5: A non-factorized symmetry mixing internal and valley factors may do better.
\[
\boxed{\text{Conditionally true}}
\]

\item A6: A microscopic first-order projection may dynamically select the Dirac sector without
symmetry isolation.
\[
\boxed{\text{Conditionally true}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We want to answer the following precise question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Which minimal exact symmetry set, if any, forces }
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_4 + v_i p_i(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i)
}
\]
up to higher-order corrections?

We show that standard \emph{factorized} symmetries do not suffice.

\subsection{General Hermitian Hamiltonian}

Let
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}},
\]
with Pauli matrices
\[
\tau^a \text{ acting on } \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\qquad
\sigma^b \text{ acting on } \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}.
\]
The most general Hermitian Hamiltonian is
\[
H(p)
=
c_{00}(p)\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{a0}(p)\,\tau^a\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{0b}(p)\,\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b
+
c_{ab}(p)\,\tau^a\otimes \sigma^b.
\]

\subsection{Step 1: internal exchange symmetry}

Impose an exact internal exchange symmetry
\[
X=\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1,
\qquad
X H(p) X^{-1}=H(p).
\]
Using
\[
\tau^1\tau^1\tau^1=\tau^1,
\qquad
\tau^1\tau^2\tau^1=-\tau^2,
\qquad
\tau^1\tau^3\tau^1=-\tau^3,
\]
we see that all terms containing \(\tau^2\) or \(\tau^3\) are forbidden, while terms containing
\(\mathbf 1\) or \(\tau^1\) survive. Thus
\[
H(p)
=
a_0(p)\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
a_b(p)\,\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b
+
b_0(p)\,\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
b_b(p)\,\tau^1\otimes \sigma^b.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1.}
Internal exchange invariance under \(X=\tau^1\otimes\mathbf 1\) reduces the \(16\)-dimensional
Hermitian operator space to the \(8\)-dimensional subspace
\[
\mathrm{span}\{\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1,\ \mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^b,\ \tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1,\ \tau^1\otimes \sigma^b\}.
\]

\subsection{Step 2: chiral symmetry}

Now impose chiral symmetry
\[
\Gamma=\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\Gamma H(p)\Gamma^{-1}=-H(p).
\]
Since
\[
\tau^2 \mathbf 1 \tau^2 = \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\tau^2 \tau^1 \tau^2 = -\tau^1,
\]
the anticommutation condition kills all terms with internal factor \(\mathbf 1\), and retains
terms with internal factor \(\tau^1\). Therefore
\[
H(p)=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0(p)\mathbf 1+b_i(p)\sigma^i\right).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2.}
The simultaneous imposition of internal exchange
\[
X=\tau^1\otimes\mathbf 1
\]
and chiral symmetry
\[
\Gamma=\tau^2\otimes\mathbf 1
\]
reduces the effective Hamiltonian to
\[
\boxed{
H(p)=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0(p)\mathbf 1+b_i(p)\sigma^i\right).
}
\]

This is the maximal reduction obtainable from these two exact symmetries alone.

\subsection{Interpretation}

The surviving terms are exactly:

\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\quad\text{(scalar in valley space)},
\]
and
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,\qquad
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\qquad
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^3
\quad\text{(valley-vector sector)}.
\]

Hence the problem is reduced to a \(4\)-parameter family.  
If one could eliminate the scalar \(b_0\) while keeping all three \(b_i\), then one would indeed
arrive at the desired Dirac kinetic form.

\subsection{Step 3: standard factorized valley symmetry}

Consider a unitary valley exchange
\[
P_v=\mathbf 1\otimes \sigma^1,
\qquad
P_v H(p) P_v^{-1}=H(-p).
\]
Using
\[
\sigma^1 \sigma^1 \sigma^1=\sigma^1,
\qquad
\sigma^1 \sigma^2 \sigma^1=-\sigma^2,
\qquad
\sigma^1 \sigma^3 \sigma^1=-\sigma^3,
\]
we obtain
\[
b_0(p)=b_0(-p),\qquad
b_1(p)=b_1(-p),\qquad
b_2(p)=-b_2(-p),\qquad
b_3(p)=-b_3(-p).
\]
Thus \(P_v\) alone leaves
\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1,\qquad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^1
\]
even, and
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\qquad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^3
\]
odd.

So the scalar term survives.

\subsection{Step 4: standard factorized antiunitary symmetries}

We now test the standard antiunitary factorized possibilities.

\subsubsection*{Case A: spinless time reversal}
Let
\[
\Theta_0 = \mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1\, K.
\]
Then
\[
\Theta_0(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1)\Theta_0^{-1}=+\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,
\]
\[
\Theta_0(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2)\Theta_0^{-1}=-\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,
\]
\[
\Theta_0(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^3)\Theta_0^{-1}=+\tau^1\otimes \sigma^3,
\]
and
\[
\Theta_0(\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1)\Theta_0^{-1}=+\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]
Thus
\[
b_0,b_1,b_3 \text{ are even},\qquad b_2 \text{ is odd}.
\]
Combining with valley exchange \(P_v\), we get
\[
b_0 \text{ even},\qquad
b_1 \text{ even},\qquad
b_2 \text{ odd},\qquad
b_3 \equiv 0.
\]
Hence
\[
H(p)=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0(p)\mathbf 1+b_1(p)\sigma^1+b_2(p)\sigma^2\right).
\]
This is not the pure Dirac kinetic sector.

\subsubsection*{Case B: Kramers-like valley antiunitary symmetry}
Let
\[
\Theta_v = \mathbf 1\otimes i\sigma^2 K.
\]
Since
\[
(i\sigma^2) \sigma^i{}^* (-i\sigma^2) = -\sigma^i,
\]
we have
\[
\Theta_v(\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1)\Theta_v^{-1}=+\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\Theta_v(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i)\Theta_v^{-1}=-\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i.
\]
Therefore
\[
b_0 \text{ is even},\qquad b_i \text{ are odd}.
\]
Combining with \(P_v\), we obtain
\[
b_0 \text{ even},\qquad b_1\equiv 0,\qquad b_2,b_3 \text{ odd}.
\]
Hence
\[
H(p)=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0(p)\mathbf 1+b_2(p)\sigma^2+b_3(p)\sigma^3\right).
\]
Again, the scalar survives and only two Pauli directions remain.

\subsubsection*{Case C: mixed internal-valley antiunitary symmetry}
Let
\[
\widetilde\Theta = \tau^3\otimes i\sigma^2 K.
\]
Then
\[
\tau^3 \tau^1 \tau^3 = -\tau^1,
\]
so
\[
\widetilde\Theta(\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1)\widetilde\Theta^{-1}=-\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
while
\[
\widetilde\Theta(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i)\widetilde\Theta^{-1}=+\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i.
\]
Thus
\[
b_0 \text{ is odd},\qquad b_i \text{ are even}.
\]
Combining with \(P_v\), we obtain
\[
b_0\equiv 0,\qquad b_1 \text{ even},\qquad b_2\equiv 0,\qquad b_3\equiv 0.
\]
So
\[
H(p)=\tau^1\otimes b_1(p)\sigma^1.
\]
This kills the scalar, but collapses the kinetic sector to only one Pauli direction.

\paragraph{Theorem 3.}
With \(X\) and \(\Gamma\) already imposed, none of the standard factorized antiunitary choices
\[
\Theta_0,\qquad \Theta_v,\qquad \widetilde\Theta
\]
together with valley exchange \(P_v\) isolates the full pure Dirac kinetic sector
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,\quad
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\quad
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^3
\]
while simultaneously removing the scalar
\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Direct from the three cases above. \(\square\)

\subsection{A more general no-go statement for factorized symmetries}

The previous computation suggests a stronger structural statement.

Let \(\mathcal S\) be any additional symmetry that acts \emph{factorizably} on the reduced
subspace:
\[
\mathcal S = U_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes U_{\mathrm{val}}
\]
or
\[
\mathcal S = U_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes U_{\mathrm{val}}\,K.
\]
After imposing \(X\) and \(\Gamma\), the Hamiltonian has the form
\[
H=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0\mathbf 1+b_i\sigma^i\right).
\]

Suppose the symmetry preserves the full valley-vector triplet, meaning that under conjugation it
acts as
\[
\sigma^i \mapsto R_{ij}\sigma^j,
\qquad
R\in O(3),
\]
while
\[
\mathbf 1\mapsto \mathbf 1.
\]
The internal factor contributes only an overall sign \(s=\pm1\) on \(\tau^1\).

Then the scalar term and the vector triplet transform with the same internal sign \(s\); the only
difference is that the vector rotates by \(R\), while the scalar does not rotate. Therefore a
single such factorized symmetry cannot impose a parity rule that eliminates
\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
while preserving all three independent components of
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i
\]
as a full triplet.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Factorized-symmetry no-go theorem).}
After reduction by internal exchange \(X\) and chiral symmetry \(\Gamma\), no additional single
factorized symmetry that preserves the valley Pauli triplet as a whole can kill the scalar
\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
while retaining all three kinetic generators
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,\qquad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\qquad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^3.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
In the reduced sector, every term has the common internal factor \(\tau^1\). A factorized symmetry
acts on that internal factor by a single sign \(s=\pm1\), common to both the scalar and vector
terms. On the valley factor, the identity is fixed, while the vector \((\sigma^1,\sigma^2,\sigma^3)\)
transforms as an orthogonal triplet. Therefore the symmetry can at most distinguish the scalar
from the vector by rotation structure, but not by an independent sign rule that removes the scalar
while leaving the entire vector triplet intact. Any additional constraint strong enough to kill the
scalar either also kills some vector components, or requires non-factorized action mixing the two
tensor factors. \(\square\)

This is the cleanest mathematical statement of the obstruction.

\subsection{Consequences}

At this point the logic is sharp:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Internal exchange \(X\) and chiral symmetry \(\Gamma\) reduce the problem to the \(4\)-term
family
\[
\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0\mathbf 1+b_i\sigma^i\right).
\]

\item Standard factorized valley/discrete symmetries can reduce this further, but always leave one
of the following problems:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the scalar \(b_0\) survives, or
\item one or two of the three kinetic directions are killed.
\end{enumerate}

\item Therefore the exact pure Dirac kinetic sector
\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)=v_i p_i(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i)
\]
cannot be isolated by standard factorized symmetry alone.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What can still work?}

There remain only two logically viable routes.

\paragraph{Route I: non-factorized symmetry.}
A symmetry that mixes internal and valley indices nontrivially may distinguish
\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
from
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i
\]
more strongly than any factorized symmetry can. Such a symmetry could, in principle, kill the
scalar while keeping a full Clifford triplet.

\paragraph{Route II: dynamical first-order projection.}
Even without symmetry isolation, a microscopic first-order operator or an explicit projected
defect/valley spectral calculation may show that the actual low-energy Hamiltonian closes on the
Dirac sector
\[
v_i p_i(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i)
\]
because all other reduced-sector operators have vanishing matrix elements in the relevant
low-energy subspace.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Sharp remaining alternatives).}
After the no-go result above, the pure Dirac kinetic sector can be justified only by one of the
following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an exact non-factorized symmetry acting on
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}},
\]
or

\item an explicit dynamical projection theorem for the microscopic or defect linearized operator.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proof.}
The factorized route has been exhausted by Theorem 4. Therefore any successful remaining
mechanism must lie outside that class. \(\square\)

\section{Final Summary}

On the \(4\)-dimensional candidate low-energy space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}},
\]
the exact imposition of internal exchange
\[
X=\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
and chiral symmetry
\[
\Gamma=\tau^2\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
reduces the most general Hermitian effective Hamiltonian to
\[
H(p)=\tau^1\otimes\left(b_0(p)\mathbf 1+b_i(p)\sigma^i\right).
\]

This is the maximal universal algebraic reduction obtainable from those two symmetries.

The desired pure Dirac kinetic sector would be
\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)=v_i p_i(\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i),
\]
which requires:

\begin{enumerate}
\item elimination of the scalar term
\[
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
\item retention of all three kinetic generators
\[
\tau^1\otimes \sigma^1,\quad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^2,\quad \tau^1\otimes \sigma^3.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Explicit classification shows that standard factorized valley/discrete symmetries
(valley exchange, spinless time reversal, Kramers-like antiunitary valley symmetry, and mixed
factorized antiunitary symmetries) cannot achieve both goals simultaneously:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either the scalar survives, or one/two of the three kinetic directions are lost.}
}
\]

More generally:

\[
\boxed{
\text{no standard factorized symmetry preserving the valley Pauli triplet as a whole can remove }
\tau^1\otimes \mathbf 1
\text{ while preserving all } \tau^1\otimes \sigma^i.
}
\]

Therefore the problem is now sharply localized. The remaining viable mechanisms are only:

\[
\boxed{
\text{(i) an exact non-factorized symmetry mixing internal and valley sectors,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\text{(ii) an explicit dynamical first-order projection of the microscopic/defect operator.}
}
\]

This is the strongest honest conclusion available at the present stage.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Operator-Structure Theorem:
Block-Off-Diagonal First-Order Form Implies Projected Dirac Kinetics}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The relevant infrared sector is represented on a complex \(4\)-dimensional space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\cong \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}}.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Valid candidate low-energy construction}}
\]

\item A2: There exists a chiral grading
\[
\Gamma^2=\mathbf 1,\qquad \Gamma^\dagger=\Gamma,\qquad \{\Gamma,\mathcal D(p)\}=0
\]
for the low-energy operator.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs extra structural assumption}}
\]

\item A3: In the chiral decomposition, the off-diagonal block is first-order in momentum:
\[
A(p)=p_i M_i+O(|p|^2).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once a first-order structure is assumed}}
\]

\item A4: The principal symbol is nondegenerate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Residual nondegeneracy assumption}}
\]

\item A5: A \(2\times2\) complex linear symbol can be reduced to Pauli form by basis changes.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A6: Therefore the full \(4\times4\) operator is reducible to Dirac kinetic form.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable from A2--A5}}
\]

\item A7: The full microscopic TECT defect/valley operator actually realizes A2--A4.
\[
\boxed{\text{Currently unproved}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We seek a theorem of the following type:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If the projected low-energy operator is block-off-diagonal and first-order, then Dirac
kinetics follows automatically.}
}
\]

This avoids relying on an insufficient symmetry-isolation argument.

\subsection{Chiral grading and block decomposition}

Let \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\) be a complex \(4\)-dimensional Hilbert space with a grading
operator
\[
\Gamma=\Gamma^\dagger,\qquad \Gamma^2=\mathbf 1.
\]
Then \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\) splits as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\qquad
\Gamma|_{\mathcal H_\pm}=\pm 1,
\]
with
\[
\dim_\mathbb C \mathcal H_+ = \dim_\mathbb C \mathcal H_- = 2.
\]

If the effective operator \(\mathcal D(p)\) anticommutes with \(\Gamma\),
\[
\{\Gamma,\mathcal D(p)\}=0,
\]
then in the decomposition \(\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-\) it has the form
\[
\mathcal D(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A(p) \\
A(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
A(p):\mathcal H_-\to \mathcal H_+
\]
is a \(2\times2\) complex matrix.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Off-diagonalization theorem).}
If a Hermitian operator \(\mathcal D\) anticommutes with a grading \(\Gamma\), then in the
\(\Gamma\)-eigenbasis it is block-off-diagonal.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Write
\[
\mathcal D=
\begin{pmatrix}
D_{++} & D_{+-}\\
D_{-+} & D_{--}
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\Gamma=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf 1_2 & 0\\
0 & -\mathbf 1_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
\Gamma\mathcal D+\mathcal D\Gamma
=
\begin{pmatrix}
2D_{++} & 0\\
0 & -2D_{--}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Thus \(\{\Gamma,\mathcal D\}=0\) implies
\[
D_{++}=D_{--}=0.
\]
Hermiticity gives \(D_{-+}=D_{+-}^\dagger\). \(\square\)

\subsection{First-order principal symbol}

Assume now that
\[
A(p)=p_i M_i + O(|p|^2),
\qquad
i=1,2,3,
\]
for some complex \(2\times2\) matrices \(M_i\). Then the principal part of the low-energy
operator is
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_i M_i\\
p_i M_i^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The spectrum of \(\mathcal D_1(p)\) is determined by
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)^2
=
\begin{pmatrix}
(p_i M_i)(p_j M_j)^\dagger & 0\\
0 & (p_i M_i)^\dagger (p_j M_j)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence the low-energy dispersion is governed by the singular values of the \(2\times2\) matrix
\[
A_1(p):=p_i M_i.
\]

\subsection{Classification of \(2\times2\) complex linear symbols}

Every complex \(2\times2\) matrix admits the decomposition
\[
A_1(p)=a_0(p)\mathbf 1_2+a_i(p)\sigma^i,
\]
with complex coefficients \(a_0,a_i\) linear in \(p\).

We now distinguish the traceless and scalar parts. The scalar term \(a_0(p)\mathbf 1_2\) tilts or
rescales, but does not obstruct the Weyl structure of the traceless part. The essential point is
that, after invertible basis changes on \(\mathcal H_+\) and \(\mathcal H_-\), a nondegenerate
linear symbol may be brought to canonical form.

\paragraph{Lemma 2 (Biunitary reduction of the principal symbol).}
Let
\[
A_1(p)=p_i M_i
\]
be a \(2\times2\) complex linear symbol. Suppose its determinant is not identically zero and that
its zero at \(p=0\) is isolated and linear. Then there exist invertible changes of basis
\[
U_+\in U(2),\qquad U_-\in U(2),
\]
and an invertible linear change of momentum variables \(p\mapsto p'\), such that
\[
U_+\,A_1(p)\,U_-^\dagger
=
p'_1\sigma^1+p'_2\sigma^2+p'_3\sigma^3
\]
up to an irrelevant scalar linear term proportional to \(\mathbf 1_2\).

\paragraph{Sketch of proof.}
The space of traceless \(2\times2\) complex matrices is spanned by the Pauli matrices. The linear
map
\[
p\mapsto A_1(p)-\frac12\mathrm{tr}(A_1(p))\mathbf 1_2
\]
defines a linear map \(\mathbb R^3\to \mathfrak{sl}(2,\mathbb C)\). Under left-right unitary basis
changes and momentum redefinition, any full-rank such map may be put into canonical Pauli form.
\(\square\)

\subsection{Canonical Dirac form}

After the reduction above, the principal operator becomes
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)
\sim
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_i\sigma^i\\
p_i\sigma^i & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\]
in an appropriate basis, where we have absorbed velocities into the momentum coordinates.

Now define
\[
\alpha^i=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \sigma^i\\
\sigma^i & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\beta=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf 1_2 & 0\\
0 & -\mathbf 1_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4,
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\beta\}=0,
\qquad
\beta^2=\mathbf 1_4.
\]
Hence
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=p_i\alpha^i
\]
is exactly the massless Dirac kinetic operator.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Block-off-diagonal Dirac theorem).}
Suppose a Hermitian low-energy operator on a \(4\)-dimensional complex space satisfies:

\begin{enumerate}
\item there exists a grading \(\Gamma\) with \(\{\Gamma,\mathcal D(p)\}=0\);
\item in the \(\Gamma\)-decomposition,
\[
\mathcal D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A(p)\\
A(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix};
\]
\item the principal part of \(A(p)\) is linear,
\[
A(p)=p_iM_i+O(|p|^2);
\]
\item the linear symbol is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

Then, after basis changes and linear momentum redefinition, the principal part of \(\mathcal D\)
is the massless Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=p_i\alpha^i.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Theorem 1, the operator is off-diagonal. By Lemma 2, the principal symbol of the off-diagonal
block can be reduced to Pauli form. Substituting into the full \(4\times4\) completion yields
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_i\sigma^i\\
p_i\sigma^i & 0
\end{pmatrix}
=
p_i\alpha^i.
\]
\(\square\)

\subsection{Relation to the internal\(\otimes\)valley basis}

The canonical Dirac basis above is abstract. In the concrete tensor-product basis
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{val}},
\]
one convenient choice is
\[
\Gamma=\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
so that the grading is carried by the internal factor. Then the off-diagonal generators are
naturally
\[
\alpha^i=\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i
\]
or an equivalent Clifford triple obtained by unitary conjugation.

Thus the earlier algebraic construction of the Dirac generators is recovered as a concrete
realization of the abstract block-off-diagonal theorem.

\subsection{Mass terms}

A mass term \(m\beta\) is compatible with the grading if \(\beta\) anticommutes with all
\(\alpha^i\) and commutes with \(\Gamma\). In the canonical basis one has
\[
\beta=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf 1_2 & 0\\
0 & -\mathbf 1_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence the massive Dirac operator is
\[
\mathcal D(p)=p_i\alpha^i + m\beta + O(|p|^2).
\]

The important point here is that the kinetic sector no longer needs to be extracted by forbidding
all possible terms via symmetry. Once the low-energy operator is known to be first-order and
block-off-diagonal, the Dirac kinetic structure is automatic.

\subsection{What remains to be proved in TECT}

The block-off-diagonal Dirac theorem shows that the genuinely load-bearing unresolved ingredients
are now reduced to:

\begin{enumerate}
\item existence of a \(4\)-dimensional isolated infrared sector;
\item existence of a grading \(\Gamma\) for the projected operator;
\item derivation of a first-order off-diagonal principal symbol from the TECT defect/valley
linearization;
\item nondegeneracy of that symbol.
\end{enumerate}

This is much sharper than the previous symmetry-only formulations.

\paragraph{Corollary 4 (Sharp remaining TECT problem).}
To derive the Dirac kinetic term in TECT, it is sufficient to show that the projected microscopic
or defect low-energy operator has a chiral block-off-diagonal first-order principal symbol with
nondegenerate \(2\times2\) off-diagonal block.

No further symmetry-isolation theorem is required.

\subsection{Why this is stronger than the previous route}

Previously the strategy was:

\[
\text{symmetry constraints}
\Longrightarrow
\text{few allowed terms}
\Longrightarrow
\text{hopefully Dirac}.
\]

Now the structure theorem is:

\[
\text{chiral grading}
+
\text{first-order off-diagonal symbol}
\Longrightarrow
\text{Dirac kinetic automatically}.
\]

This is mathematically stronger because it derives the operator form directly rather than trying to
eliminate all competitors one by one by symmetry.

\section{Final Summary}

The previous symmetry-based route was too weak to isolate the pure Dirac kinetic sector on the
\(4\times4\) internal\(\otimes\)valley space.

A stronger operator-theoretic route is available.

Let the projected infrared operator \(\mathcal D(p)\) act on a \(4\)-dimensional complex space and
suppose:

\begin{enumerate}
\item there exists a grading operator
\[
\Gamma^2=\mathbf 1,\qquad \{\Gamma,\mathcal D(p)\}=0;
\]

\item in the \(\Gamma\)-eigenbasis, \(\mathcal D(p)\) is block-off-diagonal:
\[
\mathcal D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A(p)\\
A(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix};
\]

\item the off-diagonal block has first-order principal symbol
\[
A(p)=p_i M_i+O(|p|^2);
\]

\item the symbol is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

Then, after basis changes and linear momentum redefinition, the principal part is exactly the
massless Dirac kinetic operator:
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=p_i\alpha^i.
\]

Equivalently, in a concrete internal\(\otimes\)valley realization one may take
\[
\Gamma=\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1,
\qquad
\alpha^i=\tau^1\otimes \sigma^i,
\]
up to unitary equivalence.

Therefore the mathematically decisive unresolved problem is no longer a symmetry-selection problem,
but the following operator-structure problem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does the projected TECT defect/valley operator possess a chiral block-off-diagonal
first-order nondegenerate symbol?}
}
\]

If yes, Dirac kinetics follows automatically.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{When Does a Chiral Block-Off-Diagonal First-Order Structure Actually Emerge
from Defect/Valley Linearization?}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The relevant infrared sector is a spectrally isolated complex \(4\)-dimensional space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\cong \mathbb C^4.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs spectral input}}
\]

\item A2: This space admits a \(2+2\) decomposition
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_\pm=2.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs structural input}}
\]

\item A3: The leading reduced operator is odd with respect to this grading:
\[
\Pi_\pm \mathcal D_1 \Pi_\pm=0
\quad\Leftrightarrow\quad
\{\Gamma,\mathcal D_1\}=0.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once one of several sufficient mechanisms holds}}
\]

\item A4: The off-diagonal block is first-order in the propagating momentum:
\[
A(p)=p_iM_i.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in first-order/envelope reductions}}
\]

\item A5: The principal symbol is nondegenerate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Residual nondegeneracy condition}}
\]

\item A6: A generic second-order bosonic Hessian automatically has the above properties.
\[
\boxed{\text{False in general}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Goal}

We want to isolate minimal sufficient conditions under which the reduced defect/valley operator
takes the form
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_iM_i\\
p_iM_i^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
so that the previous block-off-diagonal Dirac theorem applies.

The logical point is crucial:

\[
\text{CP}^2 \text{ geometry alone}
\;\not\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Dirac operator},
\]
but
\[
\text{graded first-order reduced operator}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{Dirac kinetic}.
\]

Thus the issue is to identify the origin of the grading and the vanishing of the diagonal blocks.

\subsection{General reduced low-energy operator}

Let \(\mathcal L\) denote the linearized microscopic or effective operator around a defect or
valley background. Let
\[
\Pi_{\mathrm{low}}
\]
be the projector onto a \(4\)-dimensional isolated low-energy sector
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\Pi_{\mathrm{low}}\mathcal H.
\]
The reduced operator is
\[
\mathcal D(p):=\Pi_{\mathrm{low}}\,\mathcal L(p)\,\Pi_{\mathrm{low}}.
\]

Assume now that \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}\) is decomposed as
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\qquad
\dim\mathcal H_\pm=2,
\]
with projectors \(\Pi_\pm\). Then the reduced operator splits into blocks:
\[
\mathcal D(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\Pi_+\mathcal D(p)\Pi_+ & \Pi_+\mathcal D(p)\Pi_-\\
\Pi_-\mathcal D(p)\Pi_+ & \Pi_-\mathcal D(p)\Pi_-
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The Dirac route becomes available precisely when the diagonal blocks vanish to leading order.

\subsection{Abstract grading criterion}

Define the grading operator
\[
\Gamma:=\Pi_+ - \Pi_-.
\]
Then
\[
\Gamma^\dagger=\Gamma,
\qquad
\Gamma^2=\mathbf 1.
\]
A direct computation gives:

\paragraph{Proposition 1.}
The following are equivalent:

\begin{enumerate}
\item
\[
\Pi_+\mathcal D_1\Pi_+=0
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\Pi_-\mathcal D_1\Pi_-=0;
\]

\item
\[
\{\Gamma,\mathcal D_1\}=0;
\]

\item \(\mathcal D_1\) is block-off-diagonal in the decomposition
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proof.}
Write
\[
\Gamma=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf 1_2 & 0\\
0 & -\mathbf 1_2
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\mathcal D_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
D_{++} & D_{+-}\\
D_{-+} & D_{--}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
\Gamma\mathcal D_1+\mathcal D_1\Gamma
=
\begin{pmatrix}
2D_{++} & 0\\
0 & -2D_{--}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence the anticommutation condition is equivalent to \(D_{++}=D_{--}=0\), which is exactly the
block-off-diagonal statement. \(\square\)

Thus everything reduces to proving or explaining the oddness of the leading operator.

\subsection{Why generic second-order Hessians do not suffice}

Consider a generic bosonic second-order reduced operator
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{bos}}(p)=K_{ij}p_ip_j + B_i p_i + M,
\]
acting on a finite-dimensional internal space. Even if a grading exists on that space, there is no
general reason for either \(M\) or the second-order symbol \(K_{ij}p_ip_j\) to anticommute with
the grading. In fact, diagonal blocks are generically present.

\paragraph{Proposition 2.}
A generic second-order bosonic Hessian does not automatically produce a chiral block-off-diagonal
first-order reduced operator.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The operator contains diagonal mass/endormorphism terms and second-order kinetic terms with no
generic parity under a grading. Without a further structural constraint, there is no reason for
\[
\Pi_\pm \mathcal H_{\mathrm{bos}}\Pi_\pm
\]
to vanish. \(\square\)

This is an important no-go statement: the Dirac structure cannot be read off from arbitrary
quadratic fluctuation theory.

\subsection{Sufficient mechanism I: exact grading symmetry}

The cleanest route is an exact involution \(J\) on the full or reduced theory satisfying:

\[
J^2=\mathbf 1,\qquad J^\dagger=J,
\]
and for the leading reduced operator,
\[
J\mathcal D_1 J^{-1} = -\mathcal D_1.
\]
If the low-energy sector is \(J\)-invariant, then letting
\[
\mathcal H_\pm := \ker(J\mp \mathbf 1)
\]
gives a grading and the diagonal blocks vanish.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Exact-grading sufficient criterion).}
Suppose the leading reduced operator \(\mathcal D_1\) admits an exact involutive symmetry \(J\)
with
\[
J\mathcal D_1J^{-1}=-\mathcal D_1.
\]
Then \(\mathcal D_1\) is odd with respect to the \(J\)-grading and hence block-off-diagonal.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By decomposing the low-energy space into the \(\pm1\) eigenspaces of \(J\), one obtains a grading
operator \(\Gamma=J\). The anticommutation relation is exactly
\[
\{\Gamma,\mathcal D_1\}=0.
\]
Proposition 1 then implies block-off-diagonality. \(\square\)

This is the most rigorous operator route.

\subsection{Sufficient mechanism II: constrained mode reduction with opposite parity sectors}

A second route is more concrete and often more physical. Suppose the low-energy sector is spanned by
modes
\[
u_a^+,\qquad a=1,2,
\]
and
\[
u_b^-,\qquad b=1,2,
\]
which are eigenmodes of some internal parity/exchange operator \(J\):
\[
J u_a^\pm = \pm u_a^\pm.
\]
Suppose further that the leading perturbation operator \(V_1(p)\) is odd under \(J\):
\[
J V_1(p) J^{-1} = -V_1(p).
\]
Then the matrix elements within the same parity sector vanish:
\[
\langle u_a^\pm, V_1(p) u_b^\pm\rangle = 0.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Selection-rule criterion).}
Let \(J\) be an involution on the low-energy sector and let \(V_1(p)\) be odd under \(J\).
Then the projected leading matrix elements satisfy
\[
\Pi_\pm V_1(p)\Pi_\pm=0.
\]
Hence the reduced leading operator is block-off-diagonal.

\paragraph{Proof.}
For \(u,v\in \mathcal H_+\),
\[
\langle u,V_1v\rangle
=
\langle Ju, V_1 Jv\rangle
=
\langle u, J V_1 J v\rangle
=
\langle u,-V_1 v\rangle
=
-\langle u,V_1 v\rangle,
\]
so the matrix element vanishes. The same argument applies on \(\mathcal H_-\). \(\square\)

This is especially useful because it does not require a full anticommuting operator identity on
the whole space; it suffices to know the parity of the leading reduced perturbation.

\subsection{Sufficient mechanism III: envelope/worldvolume reduction of first-order transport}

Now consider a defect background depending on transverse coordinates \(\xi\), with slow
worldvolume coordinates \(y^\mu\). Suppose there are four low-energy modes grouped into two
doublets \(u_a^\pm(\xi)\), and use the ansatz
\[
\Phi(y,\xi)=\sum_{a=1}^2 \psi_a^+(y)u_a^+(\xi)+\sum_{b=1}^2 \psi_b^-(y)u_b^-(\xi)+\cdots.
\]
Assume the projected worldvolume operator is first-order:
\[
\langle u_a^\pm,\mathcal L\,u_b^\pm\rangle_\xi = O(p^2),
\qquad
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L\,u_b^-\rangle_\xi = p_i(M_i)_{ab}+O(p^2),
\]
where \(p\) is worldvolume momentum.

Then the reduced operator has the form
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_iM_i\\
p_iM_i^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Envelope-reduction sufficient criterion).}
If the low-energy defect/worldvolume reduction yields two \(J\)-even and two \(J\)-odd modes, and
the leading projected transport operator is \(J\)-odd and first-order in worldvolume momentum,
then the reduced principal operator is block-off-diagonal first-order.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Block-off-diagonality follows from Theorem 4. First-order structure follows from the assumed
worldvolume expansion of the mixed matrix elements. \(\square\)

This theorem is close to what one actually wants for defect-bound Dirac fermions.

\subsection{Sufficient mechanism IV: valley linearization at a symmetry-related crossing pair}

Now consider a valley-based construction. Suppose at \(+G_*\) there is a two-dimensional internal
doublet, and similarly at \(-G_*\). Let the total low-energy space be
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}
=
\mathcal H_{+G_*}\oplus \mathcal H_{-G_*},
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_{\pm G_*}=2.
\]
Assume there exists an involution \(J\) distinguishing the two valley sectors,
\[
J|_{\mathcal H_{\pm G_*}}=\pm1.
\]
Let the linearized perturbation in momentum about the valley pair be odd under \(J\), so that it
maps \(+G_*\) modes to \(-G_*\) modes at leading order while same-valley leading diagonal terms
vanish after subtraction of the common energy.

Then again
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_iM_i\\
p_iM_i^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 6 (Valley-pair odd-coupling criterion).}
If a valley-pair low-energy sector admits a grading by valley label and the leading reduced
perturbation is odd with respect to that grading, then the principal reduced operator is
block-off-diagonal. If the off-diagonal block is a nondegenerate linear \(2\times2\) symbol, the
principal reduced operator is Dirac.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The first statement is Theorem 4 with \(J\) taken as the valley grading. The second follows from
the previously established block-off-diagonal Dirac theorem. \(\square\)

This is a clean statement, but whether the odd-coupling hypothesis is actually satisfied in the
TECT BCC valley problem remains open.

\subsection{Nondegeneracy and Dirac completion}

Once
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_iM_i\\
p_iM_i^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\]
is established, the only remaining issue is symbol nondegeneracy.

Assume:
\[
\det(p_iM_i)\neq 0
\qquad
\text{for generic }p\neq 0 \text{ near }0.
\]
Then, by the earlier \(2\times2\) symbol classification, one may bring \(p_iM_i\) to Pauli form,
and thus the full principal operator becomes
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)\sim p_i\alpha^i.
\]

\paragraph{Corollary 7.}
Under any of Theorems 3--6 plus symbol nondegeneracy, the reduced principal operator is the
massless Dirac operator.

\subsection{What is actually proved and what is not}

We have proved several \emph{sufficient criteria} for the desired structure. But we have not yet
shown that the full microscopic TECT operator necessarily satisfies them.

What is proved:

\begin{enumerate}
\item exact oddness under a grading implies block-off-diagonalization;
\item a selection rule from parity/exchange-odd leading perturbations implies vanishing diagonal
matrix elements;
\item envelope/worldvolume reduction with \(J\)-odd first-order transport yields the desired
reduced structure;
\item valley-pair grading plus odd leading coupling also yields the desired structure.
\end{enumerate}

What is not proved:

\begin{enumerate}
\item that the actual TECT defect Hessian possesses such a grading \(J\);
\item that the actual reduced leading perturbation is \(J\)-odd;
\item that the diagonal blocks vanish in the microscopic defect or valley calculation;
\item that the off-diagonal \(2\times2\) symbol is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the theorem has reduced the problem to a very specific operator-analysis task.

\section{Final Summary}

To obtain Dirac kinetics from the TECT defect/valley sector, one does \emph{not} need to isolate the
Dirac form purely by discrete symmetry elimination.

A stronger operator route is available.

Let the reduced infrared sector be a \(4\)-dimensional space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{low}}=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_\pm=2.
\]
If the leading reduced operator \(\mathcal D_1\) is odd with respect to the grading
\[
\Gamma=\Pi_+-\Pi_-,
\qquad
\{\Gamma,\mathcal D_1\}=0,
\]
then
\[
\mathcal D_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A_1(p)\\
A_1(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
If, moreover,
\[
A_1(p)=p_iM_i
\]
is first-order and nondegenerate, then the principal reduced operator is unitarily equivalent to
the massless Dirac operator:
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)\sim p_i\alpha^i.
\]

Several sufficient mechanisms can produce the oddness condition:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an exact involutive grading symmetry \(J\) with
\[
J\mathcal D_1J^{-1}=-\mathcal D_1;
\]

\item a parity/exchange selection rule forcing same-sector matrix elements to vanish;

\item a defect envelope reduction with two even and two odd low-energy modes and a \(J\)-odd
first-order transport operator;

\item a valley-pair reduction in which the leading coupling is odd under the valley grading.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the decisive unresolved TECT problem is now sharply localized:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does the actual defect/valley reduced operator admit such a grading and odd first-order
principal coupling?}
}
\]

If yes, Dirac kinetics follows automatically.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Projected Matrix-Element Theorem for Defect Envelope Reduction}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The linearized operator around a defect background admits an expansion
\[
\mathcal L(p)=\mathcal L_0+\mathcal L_1(p)+\mathcal L_{\ge2}(p),
\]
where \(\mathcal L_1(p)\) is linear in the propagating momentum/worldvolume derivatives, and
\(\mathcal L_{\ge2}(p)=O(|p|^2)\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs structural input}}
\]

\item A2: The low-energy subspace \(\mathcal K=\ker \mathcal L_0\) (or the isolated near-kernel of
\(\mathcal L_0\)) is four-dimensional and decomposes as
\[
\mathcal K=\mathcal K_+\oplus \mathcal K_-,
\qquad
\dim \mathcal K_\pm=2.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs spectral input}}
\]

\item A3: There exists an involution \(J\) such that
\[
J^2=\mathbf 1,\qquad J^\dagger=J,\qquad [J,\mathcal L_0]=0,
\]
and
\[
\mathcal K_\pm=\ker(J\mp \mathbf 1)\cap \mathcal K.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs structural identification of }J}
\]

\item A4: The leading perturbation is odd under \(J\):
\[
J\mathcal L_1(p)J^{-1}=-\mathcal L_1(p).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once a parity/exchange rule is established}}
\]

\item A5: The complement of \(\mathcal K\) is spectrally gapped for \(\mathcal L_0\), so that
Schur-complement corrections from high modes begin at \(O(|p|^2)\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable once a gap exists}}
\]

\item A6: \(\mathcal L_1(p)\) is first-order in the propagating derivatives.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in envelope/transport reductions}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Setup}

Let \(P_B\) be a static defect background, and let \(\mathcal L(p)\) denote the linearized
operator acting on fluctuations around \(P_B\), where \(p\) stands for the slow worldvolume
momentum (or envelope momentum near a valley pair).

Assume
\[
\mathcal L(p)=\mathcal L_0+\mathcal L_1(p)+\mathcal L_{\ge2}(p),
\qquad
\mathcal L_{\ge2}(p)=O(|p|^2).
\]
Let \(\mathcal K\) be the isolated low-energy space of \(\mathcal L_0\), and choose orthonormal
bases
\[
\{u_a^+\}_{a=1}^2 \subset \mathcal K_+,
\qquad
\{u_b^-\}_{b=1}^2 \subset \mathcal K_-.
\]
Define the low-energy projector
\[
\Pi=\Pi_++\Pi_-,
\]
with
\[
\Pi_\pm = \sum_{a=1}^2 |u_a^\pm\rangle\langle u_a^\pm|.
\]

Our goal is to compute the effective projected operator
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=\Pi_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)\,\mathcal L(p)\,\Pi_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
\]
to leading order, where \(\Pi_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)\) is the exact low-energy spectral projector.

\subsection{Matrix elements of the leading perturbation}

Define the matrix elements
\[
(\mathcal M_{++}(p))_{ab}
:=
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^+\rangle,
\]
\[
(\mathcal M_{--}(p))_{ab}
:=
\langle u_a^-,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^-\rangle,
\]
\[
(\mathcal M_{+-}(p))_{ab}
:=
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^-\rangle,
\qquad
\mathcal M_{-+}(p)=\mathcal M_{+-}(p)^\dagger
\]
if \(\mathcal L_1(p)\) is Hermitian.

We first prove that the diagonal blocks vanish under \(J\)-oddness.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Diagonal-block vanishing theorem).}
Assume
\[
J\mathcal L_1(p)J^{-1}=-\mathcal L_1(p),
\qquad
Ju_a^\pm = \pm u_a^\pm.
\]
Then
\[
\mathcal M_{++}(p)=0,
\qquad
\mathcal M_{--}(p)=0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
For \(u_a^+,u_b^+\in\mathcal K_+\),
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^+\rangle
=
\langle Ju_a^+, \mathcal L_1(p) Ju_b^+\rangle
=
\langle u_a^+, J\mathcal L_1(p)J u_b^+\rangle.
\]
Since \(J\) is Hermitian and involutive and \(\mathcal L_1\) is \(J\)-odd,
\[
J\mathcal L_1(p)J = -\mathcal L_1(p),
\]
so
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^+\rangle
=
-\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^+\rangle,
\]
hence it vanishes. The same argument applies on \(\mathcal K_-\). \(\square\)

Therefore the leading projected operator on \(\mathcal K\) is purely off-diagonal:
\[
\Pi \mathcal L_1(p)\Pi
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \mathcal M_{+-}(p)\\
\mathcal M_{+-}(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\subsection{First-order structure of the off-diagonal block}

Assume now that \(\mathcal L_1(p)\) is linear in the propagating momentum:
\[
\mathcal L_1(p)=p_\mu \mathcal V^\mu
\]
for some operators \(\mathcal V^\mu\). Then
\[
(\mathcal M_{+-}(p))_{ab}
=
p_\mu \langle u_a^+,\mathcal V^\mu u_b^-\rangle.
\]
Define the \(2\times2\) coefficient matrices
\[
(M^\mu)_{ab}
:=
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal V^\mu u_b^-\rangle.
\]
Then
\[
\mathcal M_{+-}(p)=p_\mu M^\mu.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Off-diagonal symbol theorem).}
If
\[
\mathcal L_1(p)=p_\mu \mathcal V^\mu
\]
and the diagonal blocks vanish by Theorem 1, then the projected first-order operator on
\(\mathcal K\) has the form
\[
\Pi\mathcal L_1(p)\Pi
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & p_\mu M^\mu\\
p_\mu (M^\mu)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from the definitions of the projected matrix elements. \(\square\)

This is the exact matrix-element origin of the \(2\times2\) off-diagonal symbol.

\subsection{Inclusion of high-mode corrections: Feshbach reduction}

Let
\[
Q:=\mathbf 1-\Pi
\]
denote the high-energy projector. Then, formally, the Schur complement / Feshbach reduced operator
on the low-energy subspace is
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\Pi \mathcal L(p)\Pi
-
\Pi \mathcal L(p)Q
\bigl(Q\mathcal L(p)Q\bigr)^{-1}
Q\mathcal L(p)\Pi.
\]
Assume that \(Q\mathcal L_0Q\) is invertible with spectral gap \(\Delta>0\), so the inverse exists
and is bounded. Since \(\mathcal L_1(p)=O(|p|)\), the correction term is
\[
O(|p|^2).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Leading-order stability under Feshbach reduction).}
Suppose \(Q\mathcal L_0Q\) is invertible on the high-energy sector. Then
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\Pi\mathcal L_1(p)\Pi
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
Hence the leading first-order effective operator is exactly the projected matrix-element operator
of Theorem 2.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Expand the Schur complement in powers of \(p\). Since
\[
\Pi \mathcal L_0 \Pi = 0
\]
on the low-energy kernel (or after subtraction of the common low-energy eigenvalue), and
\[
\Pi \mathcal L_1 Q = O(|p|),\qquad
Q \mathcal L_1 \Pi = O(|p|),
\]
the correction
\[
\Pi \mathcal L Q (Q\mathcal L Q)^{-1} Q\mathcal L \Pi
\]
is quadratic in \(p\). \(\square\)

This is crucial: once the symmetry/selection rule kills the diagonal first-order blocks, high-mode
elimination does not reintroduce them at \(O(p)\).

\subsection{Projected Dirac theorem in matrix-element form}

Combining the previous results gives the main theorem of this step.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Projected matrix-element Dirac theorem).}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal L(p)=\mathcal L_0+\mathcal L_1(p)+O(|p|^2)\);
\item the low-energy space \(\mathcal K\) is four-dimensional and splits as
\[
\mathcal K=\mathcal K_+\oplus \mathcal K_-,
\qquad \dim\mathcal K_\pm=2;
\]
\item there exists an involution \(J\) such that
\[
[J,\mathcal L_0]=0,
\qquad Ju_a^\pm=\pm u_a^\pm;
\]
\item the leading perturbation is \(J\)-odd:
\[
J\mathcal L_1(p)J^{-1}=-\mathcal L_1(p);
\]
\item \(\mathcal L_1(p)=p_\mu\mathcal V^\mu\) is first-order in the propagating momentum;
\item the high-energy sector is spectrally gapped;
\item the \(2\times2\) symbol
\[
A_1(p):=p_\mu M^\mu,
\qquad
(M^\mu)_{ab}=\langle u_a^+,\mathcal V^\mu u_b^-\rangle
\]
is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

Then the effective reduced operator satisfies
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A_1(p)\\
A_1(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}
+O(|p|^2),
\]
and, after basis changes and linear momentum redefinition, its principal part is the massless
Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_1(p)\sim p_i\alpha^i.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Theorem 1 kills the diagonal projected blocks at \(O(p)\). Theorem 2 identifies the off-diagonal
projected block as the linear symbol \(A_1(p)\). Theorem 3 shows that high-mode elimination
changes the effective operator only at \(O(p^2)\). Finally, the previously proved block-off-
diagonal Dirac theorem converts a nondegenerate \(2\times2\) first-order symbol into the massless
Dirac principal operator. \(\square\)

\subsection{Concrete defect-envelope realization}

Now let us rewrite the theorem in the envelope language most relevant for defect-bound modes.

Take coordinates \(x=(y,\xi)\), where \(y\) are slow worldvolume coordinates and \(\xi\) are
transverse coordinates. Let
\[
\Phi(y,\xi)
=
\sum_{a=1}^2 \psi_a^+(y)u_a^+(\xi)
+
\sum_{b=1}^2 \psi_b^-(y)u_b^-(\xi)
+\Phi_{\mathrm{high}}.
\]
Assume the projected transport operator is of the form
\[
\langle u_a^\pm,\mathcal L\,u_b^\pm\rangle_\xi = O(\partial_y^2),
\]
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L\,u_b^-\rangle_\xi
=
\mathsf C_{ab}^\mu (-i\partial_{y^\mu})+O(\partial_y^2),
\]
with
\[
\det(\mathsf C^\mu p_\mu)\neq 0
\]
for generic small \(p\neq0\). Then the worldvolume effective equation is
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \mathsf C^\mu (-i\partial_{y^\mu})\\
\mathsf C^{\mu\dagger} (-i\partial_{y^\mu}) & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\Psi(y)
=
0
+O(\partial_y^2),
\]
where
\[
\Psi(y)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi^+(y)\\
\psi^-(y)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
By Theorem 4, this is Dirac at principal order.

\paragraph{Corollary 5 (Defect-envelope Dirac corollary).}
If the defect low-mode basis splits into two \(J\)-even and two \(J\)-odd modes, and the leading
worldvolume transport operator is \(J\)-odd and first-order, then the defect worldvolume theory is
Dirac at principal order, provided the \(2\times2\) mixed symbol is nondegenerate.

\subsection{What this does not prove}

This theorem is strong, but not magic. It does \emph{not} prove:

\begin{enumerate}
\item that the actual TECT defect background has the required \(4\)-dimensional isolated low-mode
sector;
\item that the actual defect linearization admits the involution \(J\);
\item that the leading transport operator is \(J\)-odd;
\item that the mixed symbol is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

These are now the true remaining microscopic questions.

\section{Final Summary}

Let \(\mathcal L(p)\) be the linearized operator around a defect background, expanded as
\[
\mathcal L(p)=\mathcal L_0+\mathcal L_1(p)+O(|p|^2).
\]
Assume the low-energy subspace \(\mathcal K\) is four-dimensional and decomposes as
\[
\mathcal K=\mathcal K_+\oplus \mathcal K_-,
\qquad
\dim\mathcal K_\pm=2,
\]
with respect to an involution \(J\).

If the leading perturbation is odd under \(J\),
\[
J\mathcal L_1(p)J^{-1}=-\mathcal L_1(p),
\]
then the projected same-sector matrix elements vanish:
\[
\langle u_a^\pm,\mathcal L_1(p)u_b^\pm\rangle=0.
\]
If, furthermore,
\[
\mathcal L_1(p)=p_\mu \mathcal V^\mu,
\]
then the mixed matrix elements define a \(2\times2\) first-order symbol
\[
A_1(p)=p_\mu M^\mu,
\qquad
(M^\mu)_{ab}=\langle u_a^+,\mathcal V^\mu u_b^-\rangle.
\]
After Feshbach reduction, the effective operator is
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A_1(p)\\
A_1(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}
+O(|p|^2).
\]
If \(A_1(p)\) is nondegenerate, the principal reduced operator is unitarily equivalent to the
massless Dirac operator.

Thus the decisive reduced microscopic problem is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Find a defect low-mode basis }u_a^\pm\text{ and an involution }J
\text{ such that the leading projected transport operator is }J\text{-odd.}
}
\]

If that is achieved, Dirac kinetics follows automatically from the projected matrix elements.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Concrete \(CP^2\) Defect Low-Mode Ansatz and the Projected Overlap Theorem}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: Near the defect core, the coordinates split as
\[
x=(y,\xi),
\]
where \(y^\mu\) are worldvolume coordinates and \(\xi\) is the transverse coordinate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Local geometric setup}}
\]

\item A2: The defect background is reflection-symmetric across the core:
\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi)
\]
or gauge-equivalent to this form.
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs structural input}}
\]

\item A3: The orthogonal bundle
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp
\]
admits a local orthonormal frame \(e_a(\xi)\), \(a=1,2\), chosen in a reflection-even gauge:
\[
e_a(-\xi)=e_a(\xi).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Locally achievable once A2 holds}}
\]

\item A4: There exist two localized scalar profiles
\[
f_+(\xi),\qquad f_-(\xi),
\]
with parities
\[
f_+(-\xi)=f_+(\xi),\qquad
f_-(-\xi)=-f_-(\xi).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs spectral input}}
\]

\item A5: The leading worldvolume transport operator is first-order,
\[
\mathcal L_1=-i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu},
\]
and is odd under reflection:
\[
\mathcal V^\mu(-\xi)=-\mathcal V^\mu(\xi).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Needs operator-structure input}}
\]

\item A6: The induced mixed symbol is nondegenerate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Residual nondegeneracy condition}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Defect background and \(Q\)-frame}

Let the defect background be represented by
\[
P_B(\xi)=z_B(\xi)z_B(\xi)^\dagger,
\qquad
z_B^\dagger z_B=1.
\]
Assume that the defect is centered at \(\xi=0\) and reflection-symmetric:
\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi).
\]
Then the rank-two orthogonal complement bundle
\[
Q_B(\xi):=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp
\]
is well-defined along the transverse line.

Choose a local orthonormal frame
\[
e(\xi)=\bigl(e_1(\xi),e_2(\xi)\bigr),
\qquad
z_B^\dagger e_a=0,
\qquad
e_a^\dagger e_b=\delta_{ab},
\]
in a reflection-even gauge:
\[
e_a(-\xi)=e_a(\xi).
\]

\subsection{Concrete low-mode ansatz}

Let
\[
f_+(\xi),\qquad f_-(\xi)
\]
be two real localized scalar profiles satisfying
\[
\int d\xi\, |f_\pm(\xi)|^2 = 1,
\qquad
\int d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)=0,
\]
and parity conditions
\[
f_+(-\xi)=f_+(\xi),
\qquad
f_-(-\xi)=-f_-(\xi).
\]

Define four low modes by
\[
u_a^+(\xi):=f_+(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad
u_a^-(\xi):=f_-(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad
a=1,2.
\]
These satisfy orthonormality
\[
\langle u_a^\pm,u_b^\pm\rangle
=
\delta_{ab},
\qquad
\langle u_a^+,u_b^-\rangle=0,
\]
with inner product
\[
\langle u,v\rangle = \int d\xi\, u(\xi)^\dagger v(\xi).
\]

Define reflection
\[
(Ju)(\xi):=u(-\xi).
\]
Then, using the parities of \(f_\pm\) and the even gauge for \(e_a\),
\[
Ju_a^\pm = \pm u_a^\pm.
\]
Hence
\[
\mathcal K_+ = \mathrm{span}\{u_1^+,u_2^+\},
\qquad
\mathcal K_- = \mathrm{span}\{u_1^-,u_2^-\},
\]
gives a concrete \(2+2\) graded low-energy ansatz.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Concrete \(2+2\) ansatz theorem).}
Under the assumptions above, the four low modes
\[
u_a^\pm(\xi)=f_\pm(\xi)e_a(\xi)
\]
provide a concrete realization of a \(4\)-dimensional low-energy candidate space with reflection
grading
\[
\mathcal K=\mathcal K_+\oplus\mathcal K_-,
\qquad
Ju_a^\pm=\pm u_a^\pm.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from orthonormality of \(e_a\), normalization/orthogonality of \(f_\pm\), and parity of
the profiles. \(\square\)

\subsection{Leading projected transport operator}

Assume the linearized operator around the defect has the worldvolume expansion
\[
\mathcal L
=
\mathcal L_0 - i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu} + O(\partial_y^2).
\]
We define the leading transport piece
\[
\mathcal L_1:=-i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu}.
\]
Assume now that \(\mathcal V^\mu\) is reflection-odd:
\[
\mathcal V^\mu(-\xi)=-\mathcal V^\mu(\xi).
\]

We compute the projected matrix elements.

\subsection{Diagonal block vanishing}

For the \(+\) sector:
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1 u_b^+\rangle
=
-i\,\partial_{y^\mu}
\int d\xi\,
f_+(\xi)^2\, e_a(\xi)^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi).
\]
Under \(\xi\mapsto -\xi\):

\begin{itemize}
\item \(f_+(\xi)^2\) is even,
\item \(e_a^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu e_b\) is odd because \(e_a,e_b\) are even and \(\mathcal V^\mu\) is odd.
\end{itemize}

Hence the integrand is odd, so
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1 u_b^+\rangle = 0.
\]

Similarly, for the \(-\) sector:
\[
\langle u_a^-,\mathcal L_1 u_b^-\rangle
=
-i\,\partial_{y^\mu}
\int d\xi\,
f_-(\xi)^2\, e_a(\xi)^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi)=0,
\]
because \(f_-(\xi)^2\) is also even.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Diagonal overlap vanishing theorem).}
If the low-mode ansatz is
\[
u_a^\pm=f_\pm e_a
\]
with \(f_+\) even, \(f_-\) odd, \(e_a\) even, and \(\mathcal V^\mu\) odd, then
\[
\langle u_a^\pm,\mathcal L_1 u_b^\pm\rangle=0
\]
for all \(a,b\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
The diagonal integrands are products of an even scalar density and an odd operator kernel, hence
odd under \(\xi\mapsto -\xi\). Their integrals vanish. \(\square\)

This is the concrete matrix-element realization of the abstract \(J\)-odd theorem.

\subsection{Mixed block}

Now compute the mixed overlap:
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1 u_b^-\rangle
=
-i\,\partial_{y^\mu}
\int d\xi\,
f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,e_a(\xi)^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi).
\]
Under reflection:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(f_+f_-\) is odd,
\item \(e_a^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu e_b\) is odd.
\end{itemize}

Therefore the integrand is even, so the integral may be nonzero.

Define
\[
(M^\mu)_{ab}
:=
\int d\xi\,
f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,e_a(\xi)^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi).
\]
Then
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1 u_b^-\rangle
=
-i\,(M^\mu)_{ab}\,\partial_{y^\mu}.
\]
Similarly,
\[
\langle u_a^-,\mathcal L_1 u_b^+\rangle
=
-i\,(M^{\mu\dagger})_{ab}\,\partial_{y^\mu}
\]
if \(\mathcal V^\mu\) is Hermitian in the internal indices.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Mixed overlap theorem).}
Under the same assumptions,
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1 u_b^-\rangle
=
-i\,(M^\mu)_{ab}\partial_{y^\mu},
\]
with
\[
(M^\mu)_{ab}
=
\int d\xi\,
f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,e_a(\xi)^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi).
\]
No parity argument forces \(M^\mu\) to vanish.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The mixed integrand is reflection-even, so it is generically nonzero. The first-order derivative
factor is inherited directly from \(\mathcal L_1\). \(\square\)

\subsection{Effective projected operator}

Let
\[
\Psi(y)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\psi^+(y)\\
\psi^-(y)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\psi^\pm(y)\in \mathbb C^2,
\]
and use the low-mode ansatz
\[
\Phi(y,\xi)=\sum_{a=1}^2 \psi_a^+(y)u_a^+(\xi)
+\sum_{b=1}^2 \psi_b^-(y)u_b^-(\xi)
+\Phi_{\mathrm{high}}.
\]
The projected leading operator on \(\mathcal K\) is therefore
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{proj}}^{(1)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & -i\,M^\mu\partial_{y^\mu}\\
-i\,M^{\mu\dagger}\partial_{y^\mu} & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Concrete projected overlap theorem).}
Under assumptions A1--A6, the leading projected defect-envelope operator on the low-mode ansatz
\[
u_a^\pm=f_\pm e_a
\]
takes the block-off-diagonal first-order form
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{proj}}^{(1)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & -i\,M^\mu\partial_{y^\mu}\\
-i\,M^{\mu\dagger}\partial_{y^\mu} & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The diagonal blocks vanish by Theorem 2. The mixed blocks are given by Theorem 3. \(\square\)

\subsection{Inclusion of high modes}

If the complement of \(\mathcal K\) is gapped for \(\mathcal L_0\), then Feshbach/Schur-complement
reduction gives
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{proj}}^{(1)} + O(\partial_y^2).
\]
Thus the leading \(O(\partial_y)\) structure is not spoiled by high-mode elimination.

\paragraph{Corollary 5.}
If the high-energy complement is spectrally gapped, then the principal worldvolume operator equals
the projected overlap operator of Theorem 4 up to \(O(\partial_y^2)\).

\subsection{Dirac completion}

If the symbol
\[
A_1(p)=p_\mu M^\mu
\]
is nondegenerate for generic small \(p\neq 0\), then by the previously established
block-off-diagonal symbol theorem, there exist basis changes and linear momentum redefinitions such
that
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{proj}}^{(1)}(p)
\sim
p_i \alpha^i.
\]

\paragraph{Corollary 6 (Concrete defect-envelope Dirac corollary).}
If the mixed symbol
\[
A_1(p)=p_\mu M^\mu
\]
constructed from the overlap integrals is nondegenerate, then the principal defect-envelope
effective operator is the massless Dirac operator.

\subsection{What is genuinely achieved}

This construction does not merely postulate an abstract grading. It produces an explicit \(J\)-graded
low-mode ansatz
\[
u_a^\pm=f_\pm e_a
\]
and reduces the entire Dirac question to concrete overlap data:
\[
\int d\xi\,f_+f_-\,e_a^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu e_b.
\]

Thus the previously abstract conditions become explicit computable objects.

\subsection{What is still not proved}

What remains open is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the existence of an actual TECT defect background with the assumed reflection structure;
\item the existence of a localized even/odd low-mode pair \(f_\pm\);
\item the oddness of the true projected transport kernel \(\mathcal V^\mu\);
\item the nondegeneracy of the overlap symbol \(M^\mu\).
\end{enumerate}

These are now sharply posed concrete questions.

\section{Final Summary}

A concrete low-mode ansatz for a reflection-symmetric \(CP^2\) defect is
\[
u_a^+(\xi)=f_+(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad
u_a^-(\xi)=f_-(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad a=1,2,
\]
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(e_a(\xi)\) is an even local orthonormal frame of the rank-two bundle
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp;
\]
\item \(f_+\) is an even localized scalar profile;
\item \(f_-\) is an odd localized scalar profile.
\end{itemize}

For a leading first-order transport operator
\[
\mathcal L_1=-i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu},
\]
with \(\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\) odd under reflection,
the projected same-sector overlaps vanish:
\[
\langle u_a^\pm,\mathcal L_1 u_b^\pm\rangle=0.
\]
The mixed overlaps survive and define a \(2\times2\) first-order symbol:
\[
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal L_1 u_b^-\rangle
=
-i(M^\mu)_{ab}\partial_{y^\mu},
\]
with
\[
(M^\mu)_{ab}
=
\int d\xi\,f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,e_a(\xi)^\dagger \mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi).
\]

Therefore the principal projected operator is
\[
\mathcal D_{\mathrm{proj}}^{(1)}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & -iM^\mu\partial_{y^\mu}\\
-iM^{\mu\dagger}\partial_{y^\mu} & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
If the mixed symbol
\[
A_1(p)=p_\mu M^\mu
\]
is nondegenerate, then the principal effective operator is Dirac.

Thus the Dirac problem for the defect sector is reduced to a concrete overlap computation:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Find }f_\pm,\ e_a,\ \mathcal V^\mu
\text{ such that the mixed overlap symbol }M^\mu\text{ is nondegenerate.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{A Minimal Reflection-Symmetric \(CP^2\) Defect Ansatz and Explicit Evaluation of the Mixed Symbol}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: In a tubular neighborhood of the defect core, one may use local coordinates
\[
x=(y,\xi),
\]
where \(y^\mu\) are worldvolume coordinates and \(\xi\in\mathbb R\) is the transverse coordinate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Local setup}}
\]

\item A2: The defect admits a reflection-symmetric local profile in the transverse coordinate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Model assumption}}
\]

\item A3: The rank-two orthogonal bundle
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp
\]
is locally trivial along the transverse line and admits a smooth orthonormal frame.
\[
\boxed{\text{Locally proved}}
\]

\item A4: The low-energy scalar profiles may be modeled by one even and one odd localized function.
\[
\boxed{\text{Model ansatz}}
\]

\item A5: The projected transport kernel can be approximated in factorized form
\[
e_a^\dagger(\xi)\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi)=g(\xi)\,\Sigma^\mu_{ab}.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Model assumption}}
\]

\item A6: The matrices \(\Sigma^\mu\) are chosen so that the induced symbol is nondegenerate.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved once chosen}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Minimal local defect profile}

We choose the simplest reflection-symmetric representative
\[
z_B(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\sin\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\theta(-\xi)=\theta(\xi),
\]
with
\[
z_B^\dagger z_B=1.
\]
Then the associated projector is
\[
P_B(\xi)=z_B(\xi)z_B(\xi)^\dagger
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos^2\theta(\xi) & 0 & \cos\theta(\xi)\sin\theta(\xi)\\
0 & 0 & 0\\
\cos\theta(\xi)\sin\theta(\xi) & 0 & \sin^2\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Because \(\theta\) is even, \(P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi)\).

\subsection{Explicit \(Q_B\)-frame}

The orthogonal complement bundle
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp
\]
admits the explicit orthonormal frame
\[
e_1(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
-\sin\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\cos\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
e_2(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0\\
1\\
0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Indeed,
\[
z_B^\dagger e_1
=
-\cos\theta\sin\theta+\sin\theta\cos\theta=0,
\qquad
z_B^\dagger e_2=0,
\]
and
\[
e_a^\dagger e_b=\delta_{ab}.
\]
Moreover, since \(\theta(\xi)\) is even,
\[
e_1(-\xi)=e_1(\xi),
\qquad
e_2(-\xi)=e_2(\xi).
\]
Thus this is a concrete reflection-even gauge.

\paragraph{Proposition 1.}
The pair \((e_1,e_2)\) above is a reflection-even orthonormal frame of \(Q_B(\xi)\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Direct computation. \(\square\)

\subsection{Minimal even/odd localized scalar profiles}

We choose the standard localized even/odd pair
\[
f_+(\xi)=N_+\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\qquad
f_-(\xi)=N_-\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\]
with \(\kappa>0\).

Normalize them by
\[
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|f_+(\xi)|^2\,d\xi=1,
\qquad
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}|f_-(\xi)|^2\,d\xi=1.
\]
Using
\[
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)\,d\xi
=
\frac{2}{\kappa},
\]
we obtain
\[
N_+=\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{2}}.
\]
Similarly,
\[
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\tanh^2(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)\,d\xi
=
\frac{2}{3\kappa},
\]
so
\[
N_-=\sqrt{\frac{3\kappa}{2}}.
\]
Therefore
\[
\boxed{
f_+(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{2}}\,\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\qquad
f_-(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{3\kappa}{2}}\,\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi).
}
\]

These satisfy
\[
f_+(-\xi)=f_+(\xi),
\qquad
f_-(-\xi)=-f_-(\xi),
\]
and
\[
\int d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)=0
\]
by parity.

\subsection{Concrete low-mode ansatz}

Define
\[
u_a^+(\xi)=f_+(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad
u_a^-(\xi)=f_-(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad
a=1,2.
\]
Then
\[
Ju_a^\pm=\pm u_a^\pm,
\qquad
(Ju)(\xi):=u(-\xi),
\]
and
\[
\mathcal K_+=\mathrm{span}\{u_1^+,u_2^+\},
\qquad
\mathcal K_-=\mathrm{span}\{u_1^-,u_2^-\}.
\]

\subsection{Factorized odd transport kernel}

We now impose the simplest concrete transport ansatz:
\[
\mathcal L_1=-i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu},
\]
with
\[
e_a^\dagger(\xi)\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi)
=
g(\xi)\,\Sigma^\mu_{ab},
\]
where:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(g(\xi)\) is a real odd scalar profile,
\[
g(-\xi)=-g(\xi);
\]

\item \(\Sigma^\mu\) is a fixed \(2\times2\) Hermitian matrix acting on the internal doublet.
\end{enumerate}

Then the mixed symbol becomes
\[
M^\mu
=
\int d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)g(\xi)\,\Sigma^\mu
=
I_g\,\Sigma^\mu,
\]
where
\[
I_g
:=
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)g(\xi).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2.}
Under the factorized ansatz above, the projected mixed symbol factorizes as
\[
M^\mu=I_g\,\Sigma^\mu.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from the factorized kernel assumption. \(\square\)

Thus the entire question reduces to the scalar overlap \(I_g\).

\subsection{Explicit evaluation of the scalar overlap}

Choose the simplest odd profile
\[
g(\xi)=g_0\tanh(\kappa\xi),
\qquad g_0\in\mathbb R.
\]
Then
\[
I_g
=
g_0\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} d\xi\,f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\tanh(\kappa\xi).
\]
Substituting the explicit \(f_\pm\),
\[
f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)
=
\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{2}}\sqrt{\frac{3\kappa}{2}}
\,\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)
=
\frac{\sqrt{3}\kappa}{2}\,\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi).
\]
Hence
\[
I_g
=
\frac{\sqrt{3}\kappa g_0}{2}
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} d\xi\,
\tanh^2(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi).
\]
Using
\[
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\tanh^2(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)\,d\xi
=
\frac{2}{3\kappa},
\]
we obtain
\[
I_g
=
\frac{\sqrt{3}\kappa g_0}{2}\cdot \frac{2}{3\kappa}
=
\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}.
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
M^\mu=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}\,\Sigma^\mu.
}
\]

In particular, \(M^\mu\neq0\) whenever \(g_0\neq0\) and \(\Sigma^\mu\neq0\).

\paragraph{Corollary 3.}
For the explicit choice
\[
g(\xi)=g_0\tanh(\kappa\xi),
\]
the mixed overlap is manifestly nonzero:
\[
I_g=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}\neq0
\qquad (g_0\neq0).
\]

\subsection{Nondegenerate symbol condition}

Now suppose the propagating directions are labeled by \(i=1,2,3\), and choose
\[
\Sigma^i=v_i\sigma^i,
\qquad v_i\in\mathbb R\setminus\{0\}.
\]
Then
\[
A_1(p):=p_iM^i
=
\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}
\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i \sigma^i.
\]
Therefore
\[
\det A_1(p)
=
-\frac{g_0^2}{3}
\left(
v_1^2p_1^2+v_2^2p_2^2+v_3^2p_3^2
\right).
\]
Hence
\[
\det A_1(p)\neq 0
\qquad
\text{for generic }p\neq0
\]
provided
\[
v_1v_2v_3\neq0.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Explicit nondegeneracy theorem).}
Under the explicit ansatz
\[
M^i=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}\,v_i\sigma^i,
\qquad
v_i\neq0,
\]
the mixed first-order symbol is nondegenerate and therefore yields the Dirac principal operator.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The determinant formula above shows nondegeneracy away from \(p=0\). The earlier block-off-diagonal
Dirac theorem then applies. \(\square\)

\subsection{What has and has not been shown}

What has been shown is an explicit existence model:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a concrete reflection-symmetric \(CP^2\)-type background;
\item a concrete even \(Q\)-frame;
\item a concrete even/odd localized low-mode pair;
\item a concrete odd transport kernel leading to
\[
M^\mu\neq0;
\]
\item a concrete choice of \(\Sigma^\mu\) producing a nondegenerate Dirac symbol.
\end{enumerate}

What has \emph{not} been shown is that this specific ansatz is the unique or exact microscopic TECT
defect profile.

Thus the present section proves the \emph{existence and consistency} of a fully explicit model
realizing the desired projected Dirac mechanism, but not yet its necessity in the full TECT PDE.

\subsection{Dimension caveat}

One should also be explicit about dimensional interpretation:

\begin{itemize}
\item if the propagating coordinates \(y^\mu\) provide only two spatial directions, the result is a
\(2+1\)-dimensional Dirac operator;

\item to obtain a full \(3+1\)-dimensional Dirac kinetic term from a defect-envelope picture, one
needs three independent propagating spatial directions in the reduced effective symbol.
\end{itemize}

Therefore the defect-envelope construction is certainly sufficient for lower-dimensional Dirac
structure, and for full \(3+1\)-dimensional Dirac one must verify that the relevant reduced sector
indeed carries three independent momentum directions.


\section{Final Summary}

A concrete minimal reflection-symmetric local \(CP^2\) defect ansatz is
\[
z_B(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\sin\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\theta(-\xi)=\theta(\xi),
\]
with an explicit even orthonormal frame of
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp
\]
given by
\[
e_1(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
-\sin\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\cos\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
e_2(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0\\
1\\
0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

A concrete even/odd low-mode pair is
\[
f_+(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{2}}\,\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\qquad
f_-(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{3\kappa}{2}}\,\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi).
\]
Thus
\[
u_a^\pm(\xi)=f_\pm(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad a=1,2,
\]
gives a concrete \(2+2\) graded low-energy ansatz.

If the leading transport kernel is factorized as
\[
e_a^\dagger(\xi)\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi)=g(\xi)\Sigma^\mu_{ab},
\qquad
g(-\xi)=-g(\xi),
\]
then the mixed symbol is
\[
M^\mu
=
\left(
\int d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)g(\xi)
\right)\Sigma^\mu.
\]

For the explicit odd profile
\[
g(\xi)=g_0\tanh(\kappa\xi),
\]
one finds
\[
\int d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)g(\xi)
=
\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}.
\]
Hence
\[
M^\mu=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}\Sigma^\mu.
\]
In particular, \(M^\mu\neq0\) whenever \(g_0\neq0\).

If moreover
\[
\Sigma^i=v_i\sigma^i,
\qquad v_i\neq0,
\]
then
\[
A_1(p)=p_iM^i
=
\frac{g_0}{\sqrt{3}}\sum_i v_i p_i\sigma^i
\]
is nondegenerate, and the principal projected operator is Dirac.

Thus the explicit model demonstrates:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the projected Dirac mechanism is concretely realizable in a minimal reflection-symmetric
\(CP^2\)-defect ansatz.}
}
\]

What remains open is whether the actual microscopic TECT defect linearization realizes this same
structure without further assumptions.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Reverse Engineering a Minimal Defect Operator Model for the Explicit Low-Mode Ansatz}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The defect infrared sector may be modeled as a graded \(2\)-channel operator acting on
\[
\mathcal K=\mathcal K_+\oplus \mathcal K_-,
\qquad
\dim \mathcal K_\pm = 2.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Valid as a minimal effective model}}
\]

\item A2: The low modes may be written as
\[
u_a^\pm(\xi)=f_\pm(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad a=1,2,
\]
with a common local \(Q_B\)-frame \(e_a(\xi)\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Locally valid}}
\]

\item A3: Given explicit profiles \(f_\pm\), one may reconstruct scalar channel operators
\[
H_\pm=-\partial_\xi^2+U_\pm(\xi)
\]
for which \(f_\pm\) are exact zero modes.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved}}
\]

\item A4: The leading transport operator may be modeled by an odd kernel derived from the
transverse derivative of an even defect profile.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable in the model}}
\]

\item A5: This model reproduces the nonzero mixed overlap symbol \(M^\mu\).
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved in the model}}
\]

\item A6: This already proves the full microscopic TECT operator.
\[
\boxed{\text{False}}
\]
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Step 1: explicit defect profile and \(Q_B\)-frame}

We retain the minimal reflection-symmetric defect profile
\[
z_B(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\sin\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\theta(-\xi)=\theta(\xi),
\]
with associated orthogonal frame
\[
e_1(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
-\sin\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\cos\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
e_2(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0\\
1\\
0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
This gives a reflection-even local orthonormal frame of
\[
Q_B(\xi)=(\mathbb C z_B(\xi))^\perp.
\]

\subsection{Step 2: chosen explicit low-mode profiles}

We keep the previously chosen normalized even/odd profiles
\[
f_+(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{2}}\,\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\qquad
f_-(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{3\kappa}{2}}\,\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi).
\]
Then the concrete low-mode ansatz is
\[
u_a^+(\xi)=f_+(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad
u_a^-(\xi)=f_-(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\qquad a=1,2.
\]

\subsection{Step 3: reverse-engineered scalar channel operators}

We now ask for scalar one-dimensional operators
\[
H_\pm=-\partial_\xi^2+U_\pm(\xi)
\]
such that
\[
H_+ f_+ = 0,
\qquad
H_- f_- = 0.
\]
This requirement determines the potentials uniquely:
\[
U_+(\xi)=\frac{f_+''(\xi)}{f_+(\xi)},
\qquad
U_-(\xi)=\frac{f_-''(\xi)}{f_-(\xi)}.
\]

For
\[
f_+(\xi)\propto \operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\]
one computes
\[
\frac{f_+''(\xi)}{f_+(\xi)}
=
\kappa^2 - 2\kappa^2 \operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi).
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
H_+=-\partial_\xi^2 + \kappa^2 - 2\kappa^2\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi).
}
\]

For
\[
f_-(\xi)\propto \tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\]
one computes
\[
\frac{f_-''(\xi)}{f_-(\xi)}
=
\kappa^2 - 6\kappa^2 \operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi).
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
H_-=-\partial_\xi^2 + \kappa^2 - 6\kappa^2\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi).
}
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Reverse-engineered zero-mode theorem).}
The chosen profiles \(f_\pm\) are exact zero modes of the scalar operators \(H_\pm\) above:
\[
H_+f_+=0,
\qquad
H_-f_-=0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is immediate from the construction \(U_\pm=f_\pm''/f_\pm\). \(\square\)

\subsection{Step 4: graded minimal defect operator}

Define the reflection grading
\[
J=
\begin{pmatrix}
+1 & 0\\
0 & -1
\end{pmatrix}
\]
on the \((+,-)\) channel space. The minimal static defect operator is
\[
\mathcal L_0
=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_+ & 0\\
0 & H_-
\end{pmatrix}
\otimes \mathbf 1_2^{(\mathrm{int})}.
\]
Then
\[
[J,\mathcal L_0]=0,
\]
and the four modes
\[
u_a^\pm(\xi)=f_\pm(\xi)e_a(\xi)
\]
belong to \(\ker \mathcal L_0\).

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
The explicit ansatz \(u_a^\pm=f_\pm e_a\) is not merely ad hoc: it is the exact zero-mode basis of
the minimal graded defect operator \(\mathcal L_0\).

\subsection{Step 5: origin of the odd transport kernel}

We now model the leading worldvolume transport operator as arising from the transverse variation of
the defect background. The most natural local source is
\[
\partial_\xi P_B(\xi)
=
z_B' z_B^\dagger + z_B z_B'{}^\dagger.
\]
Since \(P_B(\xi)\) is even under reflection,
\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi),
\]
it follows that
\[
\partial_\xi P_B(-\xi)=-\partial_\xi P_B(\xi).
\]
Thus any leading projected transport coefficient constructed linearly from \(\partial_\xi P_B\)
is automatically odd.

Equivalently, because
\[
z_B(\xi)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\cos\theta(\xi)\\
0\\
\sin\theta(\xi)
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\theta(-\xi)=\theta(\xi),
\]
we have
\[
\theta'(-\xi)=-\theta'(\xi).
\]
Hence a transport kernel of the form
\[
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)=c^\mu \theta'(\xi)\,\Sigma^\mu
\]
is automatically reflection-odd.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Oddness-from-defect-gradient theorem).}
If the leading transport kernel is linear in the transverse gradient of an even defect profile,
e.g.
\[
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\propto \partial_\xi P_B(\xi)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\theta'(\xi),
\]
then \(\mathcal V^\mu\) is odd under reflection:
\[
\mathcal V^\mu(-\xi)=-\mathcal V^\mu(\xi).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Derivative of an even function is odd. \(\square\)

This gives the physical origin of the \(J\)-oddness assumption used previously.

\subsection{Step 6: explicit mixed overlap in the reverse-engineered model}

Assume the factorized transport ansatz
\[
e_a^\dagger(\xi)\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)e_b(\xi)
=
g(\xi)\Sigma^\mu_{ab},
\qquad
g(\xi)=g_0\tanh(\kappa\xi).
\]
Then
\[
M^\mu_{ab}
=
\int d\xi\, f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)\,g(\xi)\,\Sigma^\mu_{ab}.
\]
Using the explicit profiles,
\[
f_+(\xi)f_-(\xi)
=
\frac{\sqrt3\kappa}{2}\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi),
\]
hence
\[
M^\mu
=
\frac{\sqrt3\kappa g_0}{2}
\left(
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}d\xi\,
\tanh^2(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)
\right)\Sigma^\mu.
\]
Since
\[
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}d\xi\,
\tanh^2(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)
=
\frac{2}{3\kappa},
\]
we obtain
\[
\boxed{
M^\mu = \frac{g_0}{\sqrt3}\,\Sigma^\mu.
}
\]

\paragraph{Corollary 4.}
The reverse-engineered operator model reproduces the previously derived nonzero mixed overlap
symbol exactly.

\subsection{Step 7: nondegenerate Dirac symbol}

Choose
\[
\Sigma^i=v_i\sigma^i,
\qquad
v_i\neq0.
\]
Then
\[
A_1(p)=p_iM^i
=
\frac{g_0}{\sqrt3}\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i p_i\sigma^i,
\]
so
\[
\det A_1(p)
=
-\frac{g_0^2}{3}\sum_{i=1}^3 v_i^2p_i^2.
\]
Therefore \(A_1(p)\) is nondegenerate for generic \(p\neq0\), and the projected principal operator
is Dirac.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Explicit minimal effective Dirac realization).}
The minimal graded defect operator
\[
\mathcal L_0
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-\partial_\xi^2 + \kappa^2 - 2\kappa^2\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi) & 0\\
0 & -\partial_\xi^2 + \kappa^2 - 6\kappa^2\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)
\end{pmatrix}
\otimes \mathbf 1_2
\]
together with the odd first-order transport kernel
\[
\mathcal L_1=-i\,g_0\tanh(\kappa\xi)\,\Sigma^\mu\partial_{y^\mu}
\]
admits the exact low-mode basis
\[
u_a^\pm(\xi)=f_\pm(\xi)e_a(\xi),
\]
and induces the projected principal symbol
\[
A_1(p)=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt3}\,p_i\Sigma^i.
\]
If
\[
\Sigma^i=v_i\sigma^i,\qquad v_i\neq0,
\]
then the principal reduced operator is Dirac.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Combine Theorem 1, Theorem 3, Corollary 4, and the nondegeneracy of the Pauli symbol. \(\square\)

\subsection{What this achieves and what it does not}

This construction proves that the previously chosen explicit ansatz is realizable as the exact
low-energy sector of a concrete minimal graded defect operator model.

However, it does not prove that the full microscopic TECT defect linearization is literally equal
to this model. At present, the correct honest statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the explicit ansatz is derivable from a consistent minimal effective defect operator model.}
}
\]

This is stronger than a mere trial function argument, but weaker than a full microscopic TECT
derivation.

\subsection{Why this still matters}

The present step closes an important logical gap. Previously one might object that the explicit
profiles \(f_\pm\) and the odd overlap kernel were simply guessed. Now we have shown:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the profiles arise as exact zero modes of explicit channel operators;
\item the odd transport kernel arises naturally from the transverse derivative of an even defect
profile;
\item the nonzero mixed overlap and Dirac symbol follow by direct computation.
\end{enumerate}

Hence the entire mechanism is not a formal possibility only, but an explicitly realizable
operator model.

\section{Final Summary}

The explicit defect-envelope ansatz
\[
u_a^\pm(\xi)=f_\pm(\xi)e_a(\xi)
\]
can be reverse-engineered from a concrete minimal graded defect operator.

For the chosen profiles
\[
f_+(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{2}}\,\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\qquad
f_-(\xi)=\sqrt{\frac{3\kappa}{2}}\,\tanh(\kappa\xi)\operatorname{sech}(\kappa\xi),
\]
the scalar channel operators
\[
H_+=-\partial_\xi^2+\kappa^2-2\kappa^2\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi),
\]
\[
H_-=-\partial_\xi^2+\kappa^2-6\kappa^2\operatorname{sech}^2(\kappa\xi)
\]
satisfy
\[
H_+f_+=0,
\qquad
H_-f_-=0.
\]
Thus the full graded static defect operator
\[
\mathcal L_0=
\begin{pmatrix}
H_+ & 0\\
0 & H_-
\end{pmatrix}
\otimes \mathbf 1_2
\]
has the four exact zero modes \(u_a^\pm=f_\pm e_a\).

The leading transport kernel becomes naturally odd if it is built from the transverse gradient of an
even defect profile:
\[
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\propto \partial_\xi P_B(\xi)
\quad\text{or}\quad
\theta'(\xi).
\]
With the concrete odd model
\[
g(\xi)=g_0\tanh(\kappa\xi),
\]
the mixed symbol evaluates explicitly to
\[
M^\mu=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt3}\,\Sigma^\mu.
\]
Choosing
\[
\Sigma^i=v_i\sigma^i,\qquad v_i\neq0,
\]
gives
\[
A_1(p)=\frac{g_0}{\sqrt3}\sum_i v_i p_i\sigma^i,
\]
which is nondegenerate and therefore yields the Dirac principal operator.

Hence the honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the explicit low-mode ansatz and nonzero Dirac symbol are realized by a concrete minimal
effective defect operator model.}
}
\]

What remains open is whether the full microscopic TECT defect linearization reduces to this same
model or to a sufficiently close effective approximation.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Matching Theorem Between the Minimal Effective Defect Operator
and the \(CP^2\) Projector Hessian}

\subsection*{Assumption Audit}

\begin{itemize}
\item A1: The relevant internal effective sector is described by a projector action
\[
S[P]
=
\int d^{d+1}x\,
\left[
\frac{\rho}{4}\,\mathrm{Tr}(\partial_\mu P\,\partial^\mu P)
-
U(P,\partial P,\ldots)
\right],
\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P,\quad \mathrm{Tr}\,P=1.
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Effective-sector assumption}}
\]

\item A2: There exists a static defect background depending only on one transverse coordinate:
\[
P_B=P_B(\xi).
\]
\[
\boxed{\text{Local defect setup}}
\]

\item A3: Physical fluctuations are tangent to the projector manifold and may be represented by
\(Q_B\)-valued fields.
\[
\boxed{\text{Proved in the projector framework}}
\]

\item A4: The defect background is reflection-symmetric:
\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi)
\]
up to gauge.
\[
\boxed{\text{Structural assumption}}
\]

\item A5: The Hessian may be expanded into static channel operators plus worldvolume transport
terms.
\[
\boxed{\text{Provable}}
\]
