\section{Microscopic Origin of the Transport Kernel}

\subsection{TECT condensate dynamics}

In the elastic regime, the TECT condensate is described by an effective
continuum action for the projector field
\[
P(x,t)\in \mathrm{Herm}(3),
\qquad
P^2=P,
\qquad
\mathrm{Tr}(P)=1.
\]

The leading dynamical functional has the form

\[
S
=
\int dt\,d^3x
\left[
\frac{\rho}{2}\,\mathrm{Tr}(\partial_t P\,\partial_t P)
-
\frac{1}{2}
C_{ij}\,
\mathrm{Tr}(\partial_i P\,\partial_j P)
-
V(P)
\right].
\]

Here

\[
\rho>0
\]

is the effective inertial density and

\[
C_{ij}
\]

is the elastic tensor of the condensate.

The Euler–Lagrange equation is

\[
\rho\,\partial_t^2 P
-
C_{ij}\partial_i\partial_j P
+
\frac{\delta V}{\delta P}
=
0.
\]

\subsection{Defect background}

Consider a static defect configuration depending on a transverse coordinate

\[
\xi=x^3
\]

with profile

\[
P_B(\xi)=z_B(\xi)z_B(\xi)^\dagger .
\]

Reflection symmetry implies

\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi).
\]

Define fluctuations

\[
P=P_B+\delta P.
\]

\subsection{Linearized operator}

Linearizing the equation gives

\[
\rho\,\partial_t^2\delta P
-
C_{ij}\partial_i\partial_j\delta P
+
\mathcal H_B^{\mathrm{stat}}\delta P
+
\mathcal T(\delta P)
=
0.
\]

The first three terms give the previously analyzed static Hessian operator.

The new contribution

\[
\mathcal T(\delta P)
\]

arises from the fact that derivatives act on the background projector as well.

Explicitly,

\[
\partial_i\partial_j(P_B+\delta P)
=
\partial_i\partial_j P_B
+
\partial_i\partial_j\delta P
+
(\partial_i P_B)\partial_j\delta P
+
(\partial_j P_B)\partial_i\delta P .
\]

Hence the linearized equation contains the first-order transport term

\[
\mathcal T(\delta P)
=
-
C_{ij}
\left[
(\partial_i P_B)\partial_j\delta P
+
(\partial_j P_B)\partial_i\delta P
\right].
\]

\subsection{First-order transport operator}

Writing derivatives along the defect worldvolume as

\[
y^\mu=(t,x^1,x^2),
\]

the transport contribution can be written

\[
\mathcal L_1
=
-i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu},
\]

where

\[
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)
\sim
(\partial_\xi P_B)\,T^\mu
\]

for some constant matrices \(T^\mu\) determined by the elastic tensor.

\subsection{Oddness of the kernel}

Because

\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi),
\]

we have

\[
\partial_\xi P_B(-\xi)
=
-\partial_\xi P_B(\xi).
\]

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal V^\mu(-\xi)=-\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)
}
\]

and the transport kernel is odd under reflection.

\subsection{Projection onto the low-mode sector}

Let

\[
u_a^+,\ u_b^-
\]

be even and odd low modes of the static operator.

Then the projected symbol is

\[
(M^\mu)_{ab}
=
\int d\xi\,
(u_a^+)^\dagger
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)
u_b^- .
\]

Because

\[
u_a^+(\xi)
\]

is even,

\[
u_b^-(\xi)
\]

is odd, and

\[
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)
\]

is odd, the integrand is even and the overlap is generically nonzero.

Thus the microscopic TECT linearization naturally generates the mixed
first-order symbol required in the reduced Dirac theorem.

\section{Microscopic Origin of the Mixed Symbol}

The transport kernel entering the reduced Dirac operator arises directly from
the linearization of the TECT condensate dynamics around a defect background.

Because spatial derivatives act on both the fluctuation and the background
projector field, the linearized equation contains a first-order transport term

\[
\mathcal L_1=-i\,\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\partial_{y^\mu},
\]

with kernel proportional to the background gradient

\[
\mathcal V^\mu(\xi)\sim(\partial_\xi P_B)T^\mu.
\]

For reflection-symmetric defects,

\[
P_B(-\xi)=P_B(\xi),
\]

the gradient is antisymmetric,

\[
\partial_\xi P_B(-\xi)=-\partial_\xi P_B(\xi),
\]

and therefore the transport kernel satisfies

\[
\mathcal V^\mu(-\xi)=-\mathcal V^\mu(\xi).
\]

Projecting onto the even/odd low-mode sector then produces the mixed symbol

\[
(M^\mu)_{ab}
=
\langle u_a^+,\mathcal V^\mu u_b^-\rangle,
\]

which is generically nonvanishing.

Hence the odd mixed first-order structure required in the reduced Dirac
emergence theorem follows directly from the microscopic TECT linearization
around a defect background.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Valley Spectrum near the BCC Reciprocal Shell}

\subsection{Periodic condensate background}

The TECT condensate in the crystalline phase forms a periodic structure.
For the BCC geometry the condensate modulation takes the form

\[
P_0(x)=
\sum_{G\in\mathcal G_1}
A_G e^{iG\cdot x},
\]

where

\[
\mathcal G_1
\]

is the first reciprocal shell of the BCC lattice.

The magnitude of these vectors is

\[
|G_*| = \frac{2\pi}{a}\sqrt{2}.
\]

The background therefore breaks continuous translation symmetry down to the
reciprocal lattice.

\subsection{Linearized operator}

Linearizing the TECT field equation around the periodic background gives

\[
\mathcal L
=
\rho\partial_t^2
-
C_{ij}\partial_i\partial_j
+
\Sigma(x),
\]

where

\[
\Sigma(x)
\]

is a periodic operator with the same BCC periodicity.

Because of periodicity, the eigenmodes have Bloch form

\[
\psi_k(x)=e^{ik\cdot x}u_k(x),
\]

with

\[
u_k(x+a)=u_k(x).
\]

\subsection{Expansion near reciprocal shell}

Consider momenta near the first shell:

\[
k = G_* + p,
\qquad |p|\ll |G_*|.
\]

Then

\[
k^2 = G_*^2 + 2G_*\cdot p + p^2.
\]

The Bloch Hamiltonian couples modes whose momenta differ by reciprocal
lattice vectors.

The dominant coupling near the shell mixes the states

\[
|+G_* \rangle
\quad\text{and}\quad
|-G_* \rangle.
\]

\subsection{Two-state valley basis}

Define

\[
\psi_+(x)=e^{i(G_*+p)\cdot x},
\]

\[
\psi_-(x)=e^{-i(G_*-p)\cdot x}.
\]

In the basis

\[
(\psi_+,\psi_-)
\]

the effective Hamiltonian takes the form

\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\epsilon(G_*+p) & \Delta(p) \\
\Delta^*(p) & \epsilon(-G_*+p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Expanding the kinetic energy,

\[
\epsilon(G_*+p)
=
\epsilon(G_*) + v\,G_*\cdot p + O(p^2),
\]

\[
\epsilon(-G_*+p)
=
\epsilon(G_*) - v\,G_*\cdot p + O(p^2).
\]

Hence

\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\epsilon(G_*)\mathbf 1
+
v (G_*\cdot p)\sigma^3
+
\Delta(p)\sigma^1.
\]

\subsection{Linear crossing}

If

\[
\Delta(p)\sim p_\perp,
\]

then the dispersion becomes

\[
E(p)-\epsilon(G_*)
=
\pm
\sqrt{v^2(G_*\cdot p)^2 + |\Delta(p)|^2}.
\]

Near the crossing point,

\[
E(p)\sim \pm v |p|.
\]

\subsection{Weyl cones}

Thus the band structure near the reciprocal shell contains two Weyl cones

\[
E(p)=\pm v|p|.
\]

The cones at \(+G_*\) and \(-G_*\) have opposite chirality.

\subsection{Dirac pairing}

Combining the two Weyl nodes yields the effective four-component Dirac
Hamiltonian

\[
H_D = v \alpha^i p_i .
\]

Therefore the BCC reciprocal shell naturally generates a Dirac pair in the
TECT condensate spectrum.

\section{Dirac Pair from the BCC Reciprocal Shell}

In the BCC crystalline phase of the TECT condensate, the periodic background
generates Bloch eigenmodes.

Near the first reciprocal shell,

\[
k = \pm G_* + p ,
\]

the dominant coupling mixes the two valley states.

The effective two-state Hamiltonian becomes

\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
v (G_*\cdot p)\sigma^3
+
\Delta(p)\sigma^1 .
\]

When the off-diagonal coupling is linear in the transverse momentum,
the dispersion relation is

\[
E(p)=\pm v|p|.
\]

Thus each reciprocal-shell valley supports a Weyl cone.

The two opposite valleys combine into a four-component Dirac fermion,
demonstrating that the BCC condensate geometry naturally produces a Dirac
pair in the low-energy spectrum.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{What Is Actually Proved for the BCC Spectrum, and What Remains Open}

\subsection*{Purpose}

Before proceeding further, we must separate two logically different spectral
problems:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the long-wavelength elastic infrared spectrum near \(\mathbf k=0\),
\item the possible Bloch-valley spectrum near reciprocal-shell points
\[
\mathbf k=\pm \mathbf G_* + \mathbf p.
\]
\end{enumerate}

These two are not the same problem.

\subsection{What is already established}

Starting from the BCC-condensed background
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x),
\]
and the displacement ansatz
\[
\phi(x,t)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos\bigl(\mathbf Q_a\cdot (x-u(x,t))\bigr),
\]
the long-wavelength fluctuations are parameterized by the displacement field
\(u(x,t)\). This produces an elastic free energy and a quadratic dynamical matrix
of the form
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)=C_{ijkl}k_k k_l,
\]
with explicitly computed cubic elastic constants in the BCC background. 

In the deep infrared regime \(|\mathbf k|a\ll 1\), the quadratic kernel becomes
Lam\'e-like:
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)
=
(\lambda+2\mu)k^2 P^L_{ij}
+
\mu k^2 P^T_{ij}
+
O(a^2k^4),
\]
where
\[
P^L_{ij}=\frac{k_i k_j}{k^2},
\qquad
P^T_{ij}=\delta_{ij}-\frac{k_i k_j}{k^2}.
\]
Hence the spectrum splits into one longitudinal and two transverse phonon
branches. 

This is the rigorous content currently available from the BCC-condensed TECT
elastic limit.

\subsection{Why this does not yet prove a valley Weyl cone}

The elastic dynamical matrix above is a long-wavelength expansion around
\(\mathbf k=0\). By construction, it governs the infrared phonon sector.

A Bloch-valley theorem near a reciprocal-shell point \(\mathbf G_*\) would require
a different object: namely the full periodic Bloch Hessian
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf k)
\]
of the BCC periodic condensate, not merely its \(|\mathbf k|a\ll1\) elastic limit.

Moreover, the currently available higher-order correction was only constrained
abstractly as a real, even-in-\(\mathbf k\), cubic-covariant symmetric tensor
acting on the real displacement vector:
\[
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)\in \mathbb R,
\qquad
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(-\mathbf k)=\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k),
\qquad
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}=\mathcal M_{ji}^{(4)}.
\]
It contains no microscopic phase angle and no built-in \(2\times2\) complex Weyl
structure at that level. :contentReference[oaicite:5]{index=5}

Therefore the previous informal statement
\[
\mathbf k=\pm \mathbf G_*+\mathbf p
\Longrightarrow
E(\mathbf p)=\pm v|\mathbf p|
\]
is not yet derived from the presently established operator.

\subsection{Correctly formulated open problem}

The actual missing theorem is the following.

\paragraph{Open Problem (BCC shell valley theorem).}
Construct the full periodic Bloch Hessian of the BCC-condensed TECT background,
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf k),
\]
and analyze it near a first reciprocal-shell point
\[
\mathbf k=\pm \mathbf G_*+\mathbf p.
\]
Prove the existence of a two-dimensional isolated valley subspace
\[
\mathcal V_{\pm G_*},
\qquad
\dim_{\mathbb C}\mathcal V_{\pm G_*}=2,
\]
such that the projected Bloch operator has the linear form
\[
H_{\mathrm{val}}(\mathbf p)
=
H_0
+
p_i V_i
+
O(|\mathbf p|^2),
\]
with nondegenerate traceless part. Then the projected valley operator is Weyl,
and pairing the two opposite valleys yields Dirac.

\subsection{Minimal rigorous valley theorem}

The strongest honest theorem we can state now is conditional.

\paragraph{Theorem (Conditional BCC-valley Weyl theorem).}
Suppose that the periodic BCC condensate admits a Bloch Hessian
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf k)
\]
with the following properties at a reciprocal-shell point \(\mathbf G_*\):

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(V1)] there exists a spectrally isolated complex two-dimensional valley
subspace
\[
\mathcal V_{G_*};
\]

\item[(V2)] the Bloch operator projected to \(\mathcal V_{G_*}\) is smooth in
\(\mathbf p\) with expansion
\[
H_{G_*}(\mathbf p)=E_*\mathbf 1_2 + p_i V_i + O(|\mathbf p|^2);
\]

\item[(V3)] the traceless linear symbol is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

Then, after basis change and linear momentum redefinition, the projected valley
operator is Weyl:
\[
H_{G_*}(\mathbf p)\sim p_i\sigma^i.
\]

If, in addition, the opposite reciprocal-shell point \(-\mathbf G_*\) provides an
opposite-chirality partner valley, then the doubled low-energy theory is Dirac.

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is the previously established classification of nondegenerate \(2\times2\)
first-order Hermitian symbols. A nondegenerate linear traceless \(2\times2\)
symbol is always reducible to Pauli form, hence Weyl. Opposite-chirality pairing
of two Weyl sectors gives Dirac. \(\square\)

\subsection{Why the present documents do not yet verify (V1)--(V3)}

The current BCC-condensed TECT derivation verifies:

\begin{enumerate}
\item existence of the BCC periodic background,
\item its long-wavelength displacement description,
\item the cubic elastic dynamical matrix,
\item longitudinal/transverse splitting,
\item and conditional infrared isotropization.
\end{enumerate}

But it does \emph{not} yet verify:

\begin{enumerate}
\item existence of a Bloch operator analyzed near \(\pm G_*\),
\item isolated valley eigenspaces,
\item linearized shell-point crossing,
\item or full-rank Pauli-type symbol there.
\end{enumerate}

Hence the BCC-shell Weyl/Dirac statement remains open.

\subsection{What is the correct next calculation?}

To truly close the valley problem, one must start not from the long-wavelength
elastic displacement theory, but from the periodic BCC condensate itself and
its Bloch linearization.

A correct next-step calculation would be:

\begin{enumerate}
\item write the periodic BCC background
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x);
\]

\item linearize the microscopic scalar (or multi-component) TECT operator around
\(\phi_0\);

\item pass to Bloch form
\[
\delta\phi_k(x)=e^{i\mathbf k\cdot x}u_k(x),
\qquad
u_k(x+\mathbf R)=u_k(x);
\]

\item truncate to the dominant reciprocal-shell harmonics near
\[
\mathbf k=\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p;
\]

\item compute the finite-dimensional shell-projected matrix and test whether its
linearized \(2\times2\) block is Weyl.
\end{enumerate}

Only after this calculation can the shell-valley Dirac claim be called proved.

\section{Final Summary}

At present, the rigorously established BCC spectral result is the long-wavelength
elastic infrared theory:

\[
\text{BCC condensed background}
\Longrightarrow
\text{3D elastic medium}
\Longrightarrow
1L + 2T
\Longrightarrow
\text{infrared transverse sector}.
\]

This follows from the displacement ansatz and the linearized elastic dynamical
matrix
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)=C_{ijkl}k_k k_l,
\]
together with its infrared Lam\'e form
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)
=
(\lambda+2\mu)k^2P^L_{ij}
+
\mu k^2P^T_{ij}
+
O(a^2k^4).
\]

However, this is a \(\mathbf k\approx 0\) phonon theorem, not yet a Bloch-valley
theorem near reciprocal-shell points \(\pm \mathbf G_*\).

Therefore the statement that the BCC reciprocal shell already yields Weyl cones
or a Dirac pair is still conditional, not proved from the currently established
operator.

The correct open problem is to construct the full Bloch Hessian of the periodic
BCC condensate and prove that near \(\mathbf k=\pm \mathbf G_*+\mathbf p\) it has
an isolated \(2\times2\) nondegenerate linear valley block. If that is shown, the
projected valley operator is Weyl, and opposite-valley pairing yields Dirac.

Hence the honest present status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{infrared transverse elastic sector: proved;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{BCC-shell Weyl/Dirac valley theorem: still open.}
}
\]

\section{Bloch-Hessian Setup for the BCC Reciprocal-Shell Valley Problem}

\subsection*{What is already proved and what is not}

The currently established BCC-condensed TECT result is the long-wavelength elastic theory.
Starting from the periodic condensed background
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x),
\]
slow deformations are parameterized by a displacement field \(u(x,t)\), yielding a cubic elastic
medium with quadratic dynamical matrix
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)=C_{ijkl}k_k k_l,
\]
and therefore one longitudinal plus two transverse sectors for every nonzero \(\mathbf k\). 

However, this is a theorem about the infrared phonon regime \(\mathbf k\approx 0\), not yet about
Bloch valleys near reciprocal-shell points \(\pm \mathbf G_*\). The shell-valley problem requires
the full periodic Bloch Hessian of the BCC condensate.

\subsection{Periodic BCC background}

The first reciprocal shell of the BCC condensate is the cuboctahedral star
\[
S_{\mathrm{BCC}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{(\pm1,\pm1,0),(\pm1,0,\pm1),(0,\pm1,\pm1)\},
\qquad |S_{\mathrm{BCC}}|=12.
\]
Its resonance invariant is
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}=540,
\]
which is the geometric reason that the BCC reciprocal star is selected in the quartic resonance
theory. 

Write the periodic condensate as
\[
\phi_0(x)=\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}} A_G e^{iG\cdot x},
\qquad
A_{-G}=\overline{A_G},
\]
or equivalently in the real equal-amplitude form
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x).
\]
This is the microscopic starting point for the Bloch problem. 

\subsection{Microscopic linearization around the periodic background}

Let the microscopic or effective TECT field equation be
\[
\mathcal E[\phi]=0.
\]
Linearizing around the periodic background,
\[
\phi=\phi_0+\delta\phi,
\]
gives a linear operator
\[
\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\,\delta\phi=0.
\]
Because \(\phi_0\) is periodic, the coefficients of \(\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\) are periodic with the
BCC lattice period. Therefore \(\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\) commutes with lattice translations and
admits Bloch decomposition.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Bloch decomposition of the periodic Hessian).}
Let \(\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\) be the linearization of the TECT operator around the periodic BCC
background \(\phi_0\). Then \(\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\) decomposes fiberwise over the Brillouin zone:
\[
\mathcal H_{\phi_0}
\simeq
\int_{\mathrm{BZ}}^{\oplus}
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf k)\,d\mathbf k,
\]
where each Bloch fiber acts on periodic functions \(u_{\mathbf k}(x)\) via
\[
\delta\phi_{\mathbf k}(x)=e^{i\mathbf k\cdot x}u_{\mathbf k}(x),
\qquad
u_{\mathbf k}(x+\mathbf R)=u_{\mathbf k}(x).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is the standard Floquet--Bloch theorem for periodic linear differential operators. \(\square\)

\subsection{Fourier-harmonic representation of the Bloch operator}

Expand the periodic Bloch function in reciprocal harmonics:
\[
u_{\mathbf k}(x)=\sum_{G\in\mathcal G} c_G\,e^{iG\cdot x},
\]
where \(\mathcal G\) is the reciprocal lattice generated by the BCC condensate.
Then
\[
\delta\phi_{\mathbf k}(x)=\sum_{G\in\mathcal G} c_G\,e^{i(\mathbf k+G)\cdot x}.
\]
In this basis, the Bloch operator becomes an infinite matrix
\[
\bigl(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf k)\bigr)_{G,G'}
=
h_0(\mathbf k+G)\,\delta_{G,G'}
+
V_{G-G'},
\]
where:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(h_0(\mathbf q)\) is the bare translation-invariant part of the microscopic kernel,
\item \(V_{G-G'}\) are Fourier coefficients of the periodic background-induced scattering potential.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
The diagonal entries encode the bare dispersion at momenta \(\mathbf k+G\), while the off-diagonal
entries mix Fourier harmonics whose momenta differ by reciprocal lattice vectors.

This is the correct operator-theoretic starting point of the shell-valley problem.

\subsection{Expansion near a reciprocal-shell point}

Choose one shell vector \(\mathbf G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\), and consider
\[
\mathbf k=\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p,
\qquad
|\mathbf p|\ll |\mathbf G_*|.
\]
The relevant Bloch basis states are then those Fourier harmonics whose bare energies are nearly
degenerate with the mode at \(\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p\).

The smallest valley truncation is therefore not the long-wavelength displacement basis, but the
finite-dimensional harmonic subspace spanned by momenta of the form
\[
\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p+G,
\qquad
G\in\mathcal G,
\]
restricted to the dominant near-resonant shell harmonics.

\paragraph{Definition (shell-projected Bloch matrix).}
Let \(\mathcal S_*\subset\mathcal G\) be a finite set of reciprocal harmonics chosen so that
\[
h_0(\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p+G)
\]
is near the shell minimum for \(G\in\mathcal S_*\).
The shell-projected Bloch matrix is
\[
H_{\mathrm{shell}}(\mathbf p)
=
\Pi_{\mathcal S_*}\,\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p)\,\Pi_{\mathcal S_*}.
\]

This is the finite-dimensional operator that must be analyzed to prove a valley theorem.

\subsection{Minimal reciprocal-shell truncation}

The simplest possibility is that the dominant harmonic subspace reduces effectively to two complex
states, producing a \(2\times2\) valley block. More generally one may first obtain a larger
finite-dimensional matrix and then project onto a spectrally isolated \(2\)-dimensional low-energy
subspace inside it.

Thus the actual shell-valley proof has two logically separate steps:

\begin{enumerate}
\item construct \(H_{\mathrm{shell}}(\mathbf p)\),
\item prove that it contains an isolated \(2\)-dimensional valley sector with a nondegenerate
linear traceless symbol.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Conditional valley theorem}

We may now state the correct conditional theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Conditional BCC-shell Weyl theorem).}
Suppose that, for some reciprocal-shell point \(\mathbf G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\), the shell-projected
Bloch matrix \(H_{\mathrm{shell}}(\mathbf p)\) admits a spectrally isolated complex
\(2\)-dimensional valley subspace
\[
\mathcal V_{\mathbf G_*},
\qquad
\dim_{\mathbb C}\mathcal V_{\mathbf G_*}=2,
\]
such that the projected valley operator has expansion
\[
H_{\mathbf G_*}(\mathbf p)
=
E_*\mathbf 1_2
+
p_i V_i
+
O(|\mathbf p|^2),
\]
with nondegenerate traceless linear part.
Then, after basis change and linear momentum redefinition,
\[
H_{\mathbf G_*}(\mathbf p)\sim p_i\sigma^i,
\]
so the valley is Weyl.

If the opposite shell point \(-\mathbf G_*\) yields an opposite-chirality partner valley, then the
doubled low-energy theory is Dirac.

\paragraph{Proof.}
A nondegenerate linear traceless \(2\times2\) Hermitian symbol is always reducible to Pauli form.
Thus the projected valley is Weyl. Opposite-chirality doubling gives Dirac. \(\square\)

\subsection{What must still be computed}

To turn the conditional theorem into a genuine shell-valley proof, one must compute:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the explicit microscopic/effective operator \(\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\) around
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^6 \cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x);
\]

\item the Fourier coefficients \(V_{G-G'}\) of the periodic background-induced scattering;

\item the minimal shell-truncated matrix \(H_{\mathrm{shell}}(\mathbf p)\) near
\[
\mathbf k=\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p;
\]

\item the existence of an isolated \(2\)-dimensional valley block;

\item the nondegeneracy of its linear traceless part.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Why the elastic \texorpdfstring{$k\approx0$}{k≈0} theory is insufficient}

The long-wavelength elastic dynamical matrix
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)=C_{ijkl}k_k k_l
\]
and its Lam\'e-like infrared form
\[
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)
=
(\lambda+2\mu)k^2P^L_{ij}
+
\mu k^2P^T_{ij}
+
O(a^2k^4)
\]
describe the phonon sector near \(\mathbf k=0\). 

By themselves they do not encode a Bloch valley near \(\pm \mathbf G_*\), because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item they are obtained after the displacement ansatz and long-wavelength reduction,
\item they retain only \(\mathbf k\approx0\) information,
\item and they do not yet contain the shell-point harmonic mixing needed for a valley block.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the shell-valley problem must be solved at the periodic Bloch-Hessian level, not at the
already reduced elastic level.

\section{Final Summary}

The BCC-condensed TECT phase is already known to generate a periodic background
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x),
\]
with reciprocal-shell geometry selected by the BCC star and its maximal resonance invariant
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}=540.
\]

What is rigorously proved from the currently established displacement theory is the long-wavelength
elastic infrared spectrum near \(\mathbf k=0\):
\[
\text{BCC background}
\Longrightarrow
\text{cubic elastic medium}
\Longrightarrow
1L+2T.
\]

This does not yet prove a Weyl or Dirac valley near reciprocal-shell points
\[
\mathbf k=\pm \mathbf G_*+\mathbf p.
\]

The correct shell-valley problem must instead be formulated using the full periodic Bloch Hessian
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf k),
\]
obtained by linearizing the microscopic or effective TECT operator around the periodic BCC
condensate and expanding in reciprocal harmonics.

The relevant object is the shell-projected Bloch matrix
\[
H_{\mathrm{shell}}(\mathbf p)
=
\Pi_{\mathcal S_*}\,\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(\mathbf G_*+\mathbf p)\,\Pi_{\mathcal S_*},
\]
whose low-energy spectrum near \(\mathbf p=0\) must be analyzed.

If this shell-projected operator contains an isolated \(2\)-dimensional valley block with
nondegenerate linear traceless symbol, then that valley is Weyl; if the opposite shell point gives
an opposite-chirality partner, the doubled theory is Dirac.

Thus the honest present status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{infrared }k\approx0\text{ elastic sector: proved;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{BCC shell-valley Weyl/Dirac theorem: reduced to an explicit Bloch-Hessian calculation.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Bloch-Hessian Construction for a Minimal Periodic BCC Scalar Model}

\subsection*{Goal}

We now set up the actual Bloch matrix problem for a periodic BCC condensate.
The purpose is twofold:

\begin{enumerate}
\item to write the shell-projected Bloch operator explicitly for a minimal periodic scalar model;
\item to determine what this minimal model does and does \emph{not} prove regarding a Weyl/Dirac valley.
\end{enumerate}

A key correction is the following: the relevant two-state Bloch crossing is not centered at
\(\pm G_*\) as quasimomenta, but at the Bragg-plane quasimomentum
\[
k_*=\frac{G_*}{2}
\]
inside the Brillouin zone.

\subsection{Minimal periodic scalar/Brazovskii-type model}

Consider the real scalar field \(\phi(x)\) with energy functional
\[
\mathcal F[\phi]
=
\int d^3x\,
\left[
\frac12\,\phi\,h_0(-i\nabla)\phi
+
\frac{u}{4}\phi^4
\right],
\]
where the bare translation-invariant kernel is chosen of Brazovskii type,
\[
h_0(q)=r+\left(|q|^2-q_0^2\right)^2.
\]
Assume a periodic BCC background
\[
\phi_0(x)=\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}}A_G e^{iG\cdot x},
\qquad
A_{-G}=\overline{A_G},
\]
or equivalently
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(Q_a\cdot x).
\]

The Hessian around this background is
\[
\mathcal H_{\phi_0}
=
h_0(-i\nabla)+3u\,\phi_0(x)^2.
\]
More generally, any periodic scalar Hessian has the form
\[
\mathcal H_{\phi_0}
=
h_0(-i\nabla)+U_{\mathrm{per}}(x),
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x+\mathbf R)=U_{\mathrm{per}}(x),
\]
where \(U_{\mathrm{per}}\) is periodic with the BCC lattice.

\subsection{Bloch decomposition}

Because \(\mathcal H_{\phi_0}\) is periodic, Bloch theory applies. Write
\[
\delta\phi_k(x)=e^{ik\cdot x}u_k(x),
\qquad
u_k(x+\mathbf R)=u_k(x).
\]
Expand \(u_k\) in reciprocal harmonics:
\[
u_k(x)=\sum_{G\in\mathcal G} c_G\,e^{iG\cdot x},
\]
so that
\[
\delta\phi_k(x)=\sum_{G\in\mathcal G} c_G\,e^{i(k+G)\cdot x}.
\]
In this basis, the Bloch operator is the infinite matrix
\[
\bigl(H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k)\bigr)_{G,G'}
=
h_0(k+G)\,\delta_{G,G'}
+
U_{G-G'},
\]
where \(U_K\) are the Fourier coefficients of \(U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\):
\[
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)=\sum_{K\in\mathcal G} U_K e^{iK\cdot x}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Bloch matrix formula).}
For a periodic scalar Hessian
\[
\mathcal H_{\phi_0}=h_0(-i\nabla)+U_{\mathrm{per}}(x),
\]
the Bloch matrix in the reciprocal-harmonic basis is
\[
\boxed{
\bigl(H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k)\bigr)_{G,G'}
=
h_0(k+G)\,\delta_{G,G'}+U_{G-G'}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The bare part acts diagonally on plane waves:
\[
h_0(-i\nabla)e^{i(k+G)\cdot x}=h_0(k+G)e^{i(k+G)\cdot x}.
\]
The periodic potential acts by convolution in reciprocal space:
\[
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)e^{i(k+G')\cdot x}
=
\sum_{K}U_K\,e^{i(k+G'+K)\cdot x},
\]
so the matrix element between \(G\) and \(G'\) is \(U_{G-G'}\). \(\square\)

\subsection{Where the minimal two-state crossing actually occurs}

To obtain a two-state Bragg problem, choose a first-shell reciprocal vector \(G_*\).
The two harmonics
\[
|1\rangle = e^{ik\cdot x},
\qquad
|2\rangle = e^{i(k-G_*)\cdot x}
\]
belong to the same Bloch quasimomentum \(k\) and are directly coupled by the Fourier coefficient
\(U_{G_*}\).

Their bare energies are
\[
\varepsilon_1(k)=h_0(k),
\qquad
\varepsilon_2(k)=h_0(k-G_*).
\]
The degeneracy locus is given by
\[
h_0(k)=h_0(k-G_*).
\]
For an isotropic bare kernel \(h_0(q)=f(|q|^2)\), this is the Bragg plane
\[
|k|^2=|k-G_*|^2
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
k\cdot G_*=\frac{|G_*|^2}{2}.
\]
The most symmetric point is therefore
\[
\boxed{
k_*=\frac{G_*}{2}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Important correction.}
The natural quasimomentum expansion point for the minimal shell-pair problem is not \(k=G_*\), but
\(k=G_*/2\) modulo reciprocal lattice vectors.

\subsection{Two-harmonic truncation}

Restrict the Bloch matrix to the two harmonics \(G=0\) and \(G=-G_*\), i.e. the basis
\[
\{|k\rangle,\ |k-G_*\rangle\}.
\]
Then the truncated matrix is
\[
H_{2\times2}(k)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_0(k)+U_0 & U_{G_*} \\
U_{G_*}^* & h_0(k-G_*)+U_0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Now write
\[
k=k_*+p=\frac{G_*}{2}+p,
\qquad
|p|\ll |G_*|.
\]
Then
\[
k-G_*=-\frac{G_*}{2}+p.
\]
Define
\[
\varepsilon_\pm(p):=h_0\!\left(\pm\frac{G_*}{2}+p\right).
\]
The matrix becomes
\[
H_{2\times2}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\varepsilon_+(p)+U_0 & U_{G_*}\\
U_{G_*}^* & \varepsilon_-(p)+U_0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\subsection{Expansion of the diagonal terms}

Let
\[
\hat n:=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\qquad
p=p_\parallel \hat n + p_\perp.
\]
Because \(h_0(q)\) is isotropic,
\[
\varepsilon_+(p)-\varepsilon_-(p)
=
2\,\nabla h_0\!\left(\frac{G_*}{2}\right)\cdot p
+
O(|p|^3).
\]
Since
\[
\nabla h_0(q)=4\bigl(|q|^2-q_0^2\bigr)q,
\]
we get
\[
\nabla h_0\!\left(\frac{G_*}{2}\right)
=
2\Bigl(\frac{|G_*|^2}{4}-q_0^2\Bigr)G_*.
\]
Hence
\[
\varepsilon_+(p)-\varepsilon_-(p)=2v_\parallel p_\parallel + O(|p|^3),
\]
for
\[
v_\parallel
=
2|G_*|
\Bigl(\frac{|G_*|^2}{4}-q_0^2\Bigr).
\]
By contrast, the average
\[
\frac{\varepsilon_+(p)+\varepsilon_-(p)}{2}
\]
contains only even terms in \(p\) at leading order.

\subsection{Canonical form of the pair Hamiltonian}

After subtracting the common scalar part and choosing a phase convention that makes \(U_{G_*}\) real,
the two-state Hamiltonian becomes
\[
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{pair}}(p)
=
E_0(p)\,\mathbf 1
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\sigma^3
+
m\,\sigma^1
+
O(|p|^2),
}
\]
where
\[
m:=|U_{G_*}|.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Minimal scalar Bragg-pair Hamiltonian).}
For a real periodic scalar Hessian, the minimal two-harmonic truncation near the Bragg point
\(k_*=G_*/2\) has the universal form
\[
H_{\mathrm{pair}}(p)
=
E_0(p)\mathbf 1
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
m\sigma^1
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The diagonal difference gives the \(\sigma^3\) term. The off-diagonal coupling is the complex number
\(U_{G_*}\), which can be made real by a \(U(1)\) basis rephasing, producing the \(\sigma^1\) term.
The scalar part is the average of the diagonals plus \(U_0\). \(\square\)

\subsection{Why this is not yet Weyl}

A Weyl cone requires a nondegenerate linear traceless symbol of the form
\[
p_i \sigma^i
\]
with three linearly independent Pauli directions.

But the minimal scalar pair Hamiltonian above contains, at linear order, only
\[
p_\parallel \sigma^3.
\]
The \(\sigma^1\) term is generally a constant Bragg gap \(m\), not a linear transverse momentum term.
Moreover, because the underlying scalar operator is real and second order, there is no natural
microscopic source for a third independent linear Pauli direction in the minimal \(2\times2\) pair
problem.

Therefore:

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (No-Weyl statement for the minimal scalar pair truncation).}
The minimal \(2\times2\) Bragg-pair truncation of a real periodic scalar BCC Hessian does not, by
itself, generically produce a Weyl cone.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Its linear traceless part is one-dimensional:
\[
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3.
\]
The off-diagonal term is generically a constant \(m\sigma^1\), not a second and third independent
linear Pauli direction. Hence the linear symbol is not full-rank in \(\mathbb R^3\). \(\square\)

\subsection{Interpretation}

This is a very useful result, because it tells us exactly what the minimal periodic scalar Bloch
theory can and cannot do.

It can do:
\begin{enumerate}
\item construct the correct Bloch matrix;
\item identify the correct Bragg expansion point \(k_*=G_*/2\);
\item produce a genuine two-state shell-pair Hamiltonian.
\end{enumerate}

It cannot yet do:
\begin{enumerate}
\item produce a full Weyl cone from the plain scalar pair truncation alone;
\item produce the \(3\)-component traceless linear symbol needed for Dirac by opposite-valley
doubling.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What extra structure is actually needed}

The failure of the minimal scalar pair truncation is consistent with the earlier defect-based Dirac
construction.

To get Weyl/Dirac, one needs additional structure beyond the plain scalar Bloch pair, for example:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an internal complex doublet,
\item a graded \(2+2\) low-energy defect sector,
\item or a genuinely first-order projected operator whose off-diagonal \(2\times2\) symbol is
already full-rank.
\end{enumerate}

This is exactly what the improved effective defect construction was providing. So the present Bloch
analysis does not contradict the Dirac mechanism; rather, it clarifies that the plain scalar Bloch
pair is not by itself sufficient.

\subsection{Correct open problem after the present setup}

The genuinely meaningful shell-valley theorem must therefore be reformulated as follows:

\paragraph{Open Problem.}
Construct an enriched Bloch-projected BCC operator---not just the minimal scalar pair---whose
low-energy shell sector carries an isolated \(2\)-dimensional complex block with nondegenerate
linear traceless symbol.

Only then can one obtain a genuine Weyl valley, and only then can opposite-valley doubling produce
Dirac.

This enriched block may have to come from:
\begin{enumerate}
\item a multi-component microscopic TECT field,
\item an internal projector-sector doublet,
\item or a defect/Bloch hybrid reduction.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final Summary}

For a minimal real periodic scalar/Brazovskii-type model around the BCC background
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(Q_a\cdot x),
\]
the correct Bloch Hessian in the reciprocal-harmonic basis is
\[
\bigl(H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k)\bigr)_{G,G'}
=
h_0(k+G)\delta_{G,G'}+U_{G-G'}.
\]

The minimal two-state Bragg problem is not centered at \(k=G_*\), but at the Brillouin-zone
boundary point
\[
k_*=\frac{G_*}{2}.
\]
Restricting to the pair of harmonics \(|k\rangle\) and \(|k-G_*\rangle\), one obtains the truncated
Hamiltonian
\[
H_{\mathrm{pair}}(p)
=
E_0(p)\mathbf 1
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
m\sigma^1
+
O(|p|^2),
\qquad
k=k_*+p.
\]

This is the correct minimal shell-pair Bloch matrix for the periodic scalar problem.

However, this minimal scalar \(2\times2\) truncation does \emph{not} generically yield a Weyl cone,
because its linear traceless symbol is not full-rank in three momentum directions. Therefore the
plain scalar shell-pair problem is insufficient by itself for a Weyl/Dirac theorem.

The honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the Bloch matrix for the periodic BCC scalar problem is now correctly set up;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{the relevant expansion point is }k_*=G_*/2\text{, not }G_*;
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{and the minimal scalar pair truncation alone is not yet a Weyl valley.}
}
\]

Hence any genuine BCC-shell Dirac theorem must use an enriched low-energy structure beyond the
plain scalar Bloch pair.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Enriched Shell-Pair Bloch Hamiltonian:
Internal Doublet \texorpdfstring{$\otimes$}{x} Bragg Pair}

\subsection*{Logical starting point}

The minimal scalar two-harmonic Bragg truncation near the BCC shell produces
\[
H_{\mathrm{pair}}(p)
=
E_0(p)\mathbf 1
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \tau^3
+
m\,\tau^1
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
where \(\tau^a\) acts on the shell-pair basis
\[
\{|k\rangle,\ |k-G_*\rangle\},
\qquad
k=k_*+p,\qquad k_*=\frac{G_*}{2}.
\]
This is a genuine Bloch pair Hamiltonian, but by itself it does not generically
produce a Weyl cone, because its linear traceless part is only one-dimensional.

Therefore a genuine shell-level Dirac construction requires an enriched low-energy
space.

\subsection{Minimal enriched shell space}

Assume that, in addition to the Bragg pair degree of freedom, the low-energy shell
sector carries an independent internal complex doublet:
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
Then the minimal enriched shell space is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
}
\]
Let
\[
\tau^a,\qquad a=1,2,3,
\]
denote Pauli matrices acting on the pair factor, and
\[
s^a,\qquad a=1,2,3,
\]
denote Pauli matrices acting on the internal factor.

Every Hermitian operator on \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}\) admits the expansion
\[
H
=
c_{00}\,\mathbf 1\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{a0}\,\tau^a\otimes \mathbf 1
+
c_{0b}\,\mathbf 1\otimes s^b
+
c_{ab}\,\tau^a\otimes s^b.
\]

\subsection{Canonical Clifford triple}

Define
\[
\Gamma^1=\tau^1\otimes s^1,
\qquad
\Gamma^2=\tau^1\otimes s^2,
\qquad
\Gamma^3=\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Shell-pair Clifford triple).}
The matrices \(\Gamma^1,\Gamma^2,\Gamma^3\) satisfy
\[
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
First,
\[
(\Gamma^1)^2=(\tau^1)^2\otimes(s^1)^2=\mathbf 1_4,
\qquad
(\Gamma^2)^2=\mathbf 1_4,
\qquad
(\Gamma^3)^2=\mathbf 1_4.
\]
Next,
\[
\Gamma^1\Gamma^2+\Gamma^2\Gamma^1
=
(\tau^1\tau^1)\otimes(s^1s^2+s^2s^1)=0.
\]
Also
\[
\Gamma^1\Gamma^3+\Gamma^3\Gamma^1
=
(\tau^1\tau^3+\tau^3\tau^1)\otimes s^1=0,
\]
and similarly
\[
\Gamma^2\Gamma^3+\Gamma^3\Gamma^2=0.
\]
Thus all three anticommute pairwise. \(\square\)

Therefore the enriched shell space is the minimal shell-level carrier of a
\(3\)-direction Dirac principal symbol.

\subsection{Canonical enriched Dirac Hamiltonian}

The corresponding canonical massless Dirac Hamiltonian is
\[
\boxed{
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)
=
v_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
v_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
+
v_3 p_3\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1.
}
\]
Its square is
\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)^2
=
v_1^2p_1^2+v_2^2p_2^2+v_3^2p_3^2,
\]
times the \(4\times4\) identity. Hence the spectrum is
\[
E(p)=\pm\sqrt{v_1^2p_1^2+v_2^2p_2^2+v_3^2p_3^2},
\]
with twofold degeneracy.

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
The enriched shell space supports an exact massless Dirac principal operator.

\subsection{Interpretation of the three directions}

The crucial point is that the three Clifford directions now have a clear origin:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1\) comes from the diagonal energy difference of the Bragg pair,
which is already present in the minimal scalar Bloch problem and is linear in the longitudinal shell
deviation \(p_\parallel\);

\item \(\tau^1\otimes s^1\) and \(\tau^1\otimes s^2\) come from pair-mixing terms whose amplitudes
act nontrivially on the internal doublet.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the internal doublet supplies exactly the missing two transverse Pauli directions that the
plain scalar pair lacks.

\subsection{General first-order enriched shell Hamiltonian}

The most general linear Hermitian Hamiltonian on
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}=\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}
\]
may be written as
\[
H_1(p)
=
p_i
\Bigl(
a_i\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
b_i^a\,\tau^1\otimes s^a
+
c_i^a\,\tau^2\otimes s^a
+
d_i^a\,\mathbf 1\otimes s^a
\Bigr)
\]
after removing an irrelevant scalar tilt term proportional to
\(\mathbf 1\otimes\mathbf 1\).

However, the shell-pair origin suggests a more restricted structure:
the pair-diagonal difference is naturally associated with \(\tau^3\), while
pair-off-diagonal mixing comes with \(\tau^1\) or \(\tau^2\). A natural real-valued
Hermitian gauge convention reduces this to
\[
H_1(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
p_\alpha\,\tau^1\otimes W^\alpha,
\qquad
\alpha=1,2,
\]
where \(W^\alpha\) are Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrices on the internal doublet.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (General enriched shell criterion).}
Suppose the linear shell-projected Hamiltonian has the form
\[
H_1(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
p_1\,\tau^1\otimes W^1
+
p_2\,\tau^1\otimes W^2,
\]
where \(W^1,W^2\) are traceless Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrices satisfying
\[
(W^1)^2=\mathbf 1,
\qquad
(W^2)^2=\mathbf 1,
\qquad
\{W^1,W^2\}=0.
\]
Then \(H_1(p)\) is unitarily equivalent to the massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
v_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
v_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
+
v_3 p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Any pair of traceless Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrices that square to the identity
and anticommute is unitarily equivalent to \(s^1,s^2\). Conjugating the internal
doublet basis accordingly puts \(W^1,W^2\) into Pauli form. Then Theorem 1 applies.
\(\square\)

\subsection{Mass terms and what must be removed}

A generic enriched shell Hamiltonian also allows constant mass terms such as
\[
m_0\,\tau^1\otimes\mathbf 1,
\qquad
m_a\,\tau^1\otimes s^a,
\qquad
\widetilde m_a\,\tau^2\otimes s^a.
\]
These generally gap the shell crossing.

\paragraph{Proposition 4.}
To obtain a massless Dirac point at the shell crossing, the constant pair-off-diagonal
mass terms must vanish in the projected low-energy block.

\paragraph{Reason.}
If
\[
H(0)\neq E_*\mathbf 1_4,
\]
then the shell crossing is already split or gapped at \(p=0\). A massless Dirac point
requires
\[
H(0)-E_*\mathbf 1_4=0
\]
on the projected shell sector.

This is a genuinely nontrivial condition and is not automatic in the shell Bloch problem.

\subsection{Minimal conditional shell-Dirac theorem}

We may therefore state the correct shell-level theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Conditional enriched shell Dirac theorem).}
Suppose that near a shell Bragg point \(k_*=G_*/2\), the Bloch-projected BCC operator admits an
isolated enriched low-energy sector
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(S1)] the constant mass terms vanish at the crossing point;

\item[(S2)] the linearized pair-diagonal splitting is nonzero along one shell direction:
\[
v_\parallel\neq 0;
\]

\item[(S3)] the linearized pair-mixing terms generate two linearly independent traceless Hermitian
matrices \(W^1,W^2\) on the internal doublet, with
\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0,
\qquad
(W^\alpha)^2\propto \mathbf 1.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the projected shell Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent to the massless Dirac operator.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By (S2)--(S3), the linearized shell Hamiltonian takes Dirac form as in Theorem 3. By (S1), the
crossing remains massless at \(p=0\). Hence the projected shell Hamiltonian is a massless Dirac
operator. \(\square\)

\subsection{What is proved and what is still open}

What is proved in this section:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal enriched shell space
\[
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}
\]
is the correct shell-level carrier of a Dirac principal symbol;

\item the plain scalar pair failure is repaired by the internal doublet;

\item the necessary and sufficient type of linear shell couplings needed for Dirac are explicitly
identified.
\end{enumerate}

What is still open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item proving that the microscopic BCC Bloch Hessian actually contains such an isolated enriched
shell block;

\item proving that constant Bragg mass terms vanish or are symmetry/topology protected;

\item computing the actual transverse linear pair-mixing matrices \(W^\alpha\) from the microscopic
projection.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the shell problem is now sharply localized to a finite-dimensional projected calculation.

\section{Final Summary}

The minimal scalar Bragg pair near the BCC shell is not sufficient to produce a Weyl cone, because
its linear traceless symbol is only one-dimensional. Therefore a genuine shell-level Dirac theorem
requires an enriched low-energy space.

The minimal enriched shell space is
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
where the shell pair provides one Pauli direction and the internal doublet supplies the missing two.

On this space one may define
\[
\Gamma^1=\tau^1\otimes s^1,
\qquad
\Gamma^2=\tau^1\otimes s^2,
\qquad
\Gamma^3=\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1,
\]
which satisfy the Clifford relations
\[
\{\Gamma^i,\Gamma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\]
Hence
\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}(p)
=
v_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
v_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
+
v_3 p_3\,\tau^3\otimes \mathbf 1
\]
is an exact massless Dirac principal operator.

Therefore the correct shell-level conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{plain scalar Bragg pair: insufficient;}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{internal doublet }\otimes\text{ shell pair: sufficient in principle for Dirac.}
}
\]

What remains open is the microscopic projected calculation showing that the BCC Bloch Hessian
actually produces the required isolated \(4\times4\) shell block with vanishing constant mass and
two independent transverse internal mixing directions.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Projected Bloch Matrix-Element Theorem for Shell-Level Dirac Emergence}

\subsection*{Goal}

We now reformulate the shell-level Dirac problem as a finite-dimensional projected
matrix-element problem.

The objective is to replace the vague statement
\[
\text{``an enriched shell block should produce Dirac''}
\]
by a precise criterion involving explicit overlap matrices.

\subsection{Enriched shell basis}

Let \(k_*=G_*/2\) be the Bragg quasimomentum associated with a chosen first-shell
reciprocal vector \(G_*\).

Assume that near \(k_*\) the Bloch Hessian admits an isolated low-energy shell sector
of the form
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
Choose a basis
\[
\Psi_{+,a}(x)=e^{ik_*\cdot x}u_{+,a}(x),
\qquad
\Psi_{-,a}(x)=e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_{-,a}(x),
\qquad a=1,2,
\]
where \(u_{\pm,a}(x)\) are periodic Bloch envelopes spanning the internal doublet
in the two pair sectors.

We assume the basis is orthonormal:
\[
\langle \Psi_{\sigma,a},\Psi_{\sigma',b}\rangle
=
\delta_{\sigma\sigma'}\delta_{ab},
\qquad
\sigma,\sigma'\in\{+,-\}.
\]

\subsection{Projected shell Hamiltonian}

Let \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k)\) be the full Bloch Hessian of the periodic
BCC background. Expand around the Bragg point:
\[
k=k_*+p,
\qquad
|p|\ll |k_*|.
\]
Project to the enriched shell sector:
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
\Pi_{\mathrm{shell}}\,\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k_*+p)\,\Pi_{\mathrm{shell}}.
\]
In the ordered basis
\[
(\Psi_{+,1},\Psi_{+,2},\Psi_{-,1},\Psi_{-,2}),
\]
the operator has \(2\times2\) block form
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_+(p) & B(p)\\
B(p)^\dagger & E_-(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where \(E_\pm(p)\) and \(B(p)\) are \(2\times2\) matrices on the internal doublet.

\paragraph{Definition.}
The matrix entries are the projected Bloch overlaps
\[
(E_+(p))_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k_*+p)\Psi_{+,b}\rangle,
\]
\[
(E_-(p))_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{-,a},\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k_*+p)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle,
\]
\[
(B(p))_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k_*+p)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle.
\]

This is the finite-dimensional shell Hamiltonian that contains all information relevant
for the shell-level Dirac question.

\subsection{Expansion near the crossing}

Assume smoothness in \(p\). Then
\[
E_\pm(p)=E_\pm(0)+p_i\,\mathcal E_{\pm}^i+O(|p|^2),
\]
\[
B(p)=B_0+p_i\,\mathcal B^i+O(|p|^2).
\]

The average scalar part
\[
\frac{E_+(p)+E_-(p)}{2}
\]
is physically irrelevant for chirality and may be subtracted. The crucial decomposition is
\[
\frac{E_+(p)-E_-(p)}{2}
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2
+
p_i \Delta_i + O(|p|^2),
\]
where \(v_\parallel\) is the Bragg-pair longitudinal slope and \(\Delta_i\) are possible
internal corrections.

Similarly,
\[
B(p)=B_0+p_1W^1+p_2W^2+p_\parallel W^\parallel+O(|p|^2).
\]

\subsection{Reduction to the minimal Dirac shell form}

The simplest shell-Dirac scenario is the one in which:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the pair-diagonal splitting is dominated by a scalar internal term
\[
E_+(p)-E_-(p)=2v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2+O(|p|^2);
\]

\item the constant pair-mixing vanishes
\[
B_0=0;
\]

\item the transverse pair-mixing is linear and acts nontrivially on the internal doublet:
\[
B(p)=p_1W^1+p_2W^2+O(|p|^2),
\]
with \(W^1,W^2\) traceless Hermitian matrices.
\end{enumerate}

Under these assumptions,
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
E_0(p)\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\tau^1\otimes (p_1W^1+p_2W^2)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
after choosing a real pair-mixing gauge convention.

\subsection{Canonical internal reduction}

Every traceless Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrix can be written as
\[
W^\alpha=w^\alpha_i s^i.
\]
Hence the two transverse matrices \(W^1,W^2\) define two vectors
\[
w^1,\ w^2\in \mathbb R^3.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
If \(W^1\) and \(W^2\) are linearly independent traceless Hermitian matrices, then there exists
an internal unitary basis change such that
\[
W^1=\nu_1 s^1,
\qquad
W^2=\nu_2(\cos\vartheta\,s^1+\sin\vartheta\,s^2).
\]
If, in addition,
\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0,
\]
then one can choose the basis so that
\[
W^1=\nu_1 s^1,
\qquad
W^2=\nu_2 s^2.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is the standard classification of two traceless Hermitian \(2\times2\) matrices under
adjoint \(SU(2)\) action, which acts as \(SO(3)\) on the coefficient vectors \(w^\alpha\). \(\square\)

Thus anticommuting independent transverse matrices are exactly what is needed for the shell-level
Dirac reduction.

\subsection{Shell-level Dirac theorem}

We can now state the main criterion.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Projected Bloch matrix-element Dirac theorem).}
Suppose the projected shell Hamiltonian satisfies:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(S1)] the shell sector is an isolated \(4\)-dimensional complex subspace
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}};
\]

\item[(S2)] after subtraction of the common reference energy,
\[
E_+(0)=E_-(0),
\qquad
B_0=0;
\]

\item[(S3)] the linear pair-diagonal splitting is nonzero and scalar in the internal sector:
\[
E_+(p)-E_-(p)
=
2v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2
+
O(|p|^2),
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq 0;
\]

\item[(S4)] the pair-mixing block has transverse linear form
\[
B(p)=p_1W^1+p_2W^2+O(|p|^2),
\]
where \(W^1,W^2\) are traceless Hermitian matrices satisfying
\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0,
\qquad
(W^1)^2=\nu_1^2\mathbf 1,
\qquad
(W^2)^2=\nu_2^2\mathbf 1,
\qquad
\nu_1\nu_2\neq0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the projected shell Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent, at principal order, to the
massless Dirac operator
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
\sim
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\nu_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\nu_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By (S2), the crossing is massless at \(p=0\). By (S3), the pair-diagonal splitting gives the
\(\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1\) term. By (S4) and Lemma 1, the internal basis can be chosen so that
\[
W^1=\nu_1 s^1,\qquad W^2=\nu_2 s^2.
\]
Hence
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
E_0(p)\mathbf 1_4
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\nu_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\nu_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2
+
O(|p|^2).
\]
The three matrices
\[
\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1,\qquad
\tau^1\otimes s^1,\qquad
\tau^1\otimes s^2
\]
mutually anticommute and square to the identity up to constants, so the principal operator is
Dirac. \(\square\)

\subsection{Matrix-element interpretation of the conditions}

The load-bearing data are therefore exactly the following projected overlaps:

\[
\boxed{
B_0{}_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k_*)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\left(\partial_{p_\parallel}E_+ - \partial_{p_\parallel}E_-\right)_{ab}\big|_{p=0}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
W^\alpha_{ab}
=
\left.\frac{\partial}{\partial p_\alpha}
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H_{\mathrm{Bloch}}(k_*+p)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle
\right|_{p=0},
\qquad \alpha=1,2.
}
\]

Thus the shell-level Dirac problem is reduced completely to a finite collection of matrix elements.

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
To prove shell-level Dirac emergence microscopically, it is sufficient to compute the four objects
\[
B_0,\qquad
v_\parallel,\qquad
W^1,\qquad
W^2,
\]
and verify conditions (S2)--(S4).

\subsection{What is not yet proved}

This theorem is strong but conditional. It does \emph{not} yet prove that the actual BCC Bloch
Hessian of TECT satisfies (S1)--(S4). In particular, the following still require explicit
microscopic calculation:

\begin{enumerate}
\item existence of the isolated enriched shell block;
\item vanishing of the constant Bragg mass \(B_0\);
\item nonzero longitudinal splitting \(v_\parallel\);
\item two independent anticommuting transverse mixing matrices \(W^1,W^2\).
\end{enumerate}

Hence the remaining gap is no longer conceptual but entirely a projected Bloch matrix-element
calculation.

\section{Final Summary}

The shell-level Dirac problem can be formulated entirely as a finite-dimensional projected Bloch
matrix-element problem.

Let the enriched shell space be
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\]
with basis states
\[
\Psi_{+,a}(x)=e^{ik_*\cdot x}u_{+,a}(x),
\qquad
\Psi_{-,a}(x)=e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_{-,a}(x),
\qquad
a=1,2.
\]
Then the projected shell Hamiltonian has block form
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_+(p) & B(p)\\
B(p)^\dagger & E_-(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where all entries are \(2\times2\) internal matrices.

A massless shell-level Dirac operator emerges if and only if, at principal order:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the constant pair-mixing mass vanishes,
\[
B_0=0;
\]

\item the pair-diagonal splitting is linear and nonzero,
\[
E_+(p)-E_-(p)=2v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2+O(|p|^2),
\qquad v_\parallel\neq 0;
\]

\item the transverse pair-mixing has the form
\[
B(p)=p_1W^1+p_2W^2+O(|p|^2),
\]
with \(W^1,W^2\) traceless Hermitian, nonzero, and anticommuting.
\end{enumerate}

Under these conditions the projected shell Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent to
\[
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\nu_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\nu_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2,
\]
which is the massless Dirac principal operator.

Therefore the remaining microscopic task is completely explicit:

\[
\boxed{
\text{compute }B_0,\ v_\parallel,\ W^1,\ W^2
\text{ from the projected BCC Bloch Hessian.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Projected Bloch Overlap Formulae in a Minimal Enriched Periodic Operator}

\subsection*{Purpose}

We now choose the simplest enriched periodic operator that is capable, in principle,
of realizing the shell-level Dirac conditions. The goal is not yet to prove that the
full microscopic TECT operator is exactly of this form, but to reduce the shell-level
Dirac problem to explicit cell-overlap integrals.

\subsection*{Microscopic motivation}

The periodic BCC condensate background
\[
\phi_0(x)=2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}\cos(Q_a\cdot x)
\]
is already established as the simplest BCC-symmetric condensed pattern. 

What is not yet established is the shell-valley spectrum of the full periodic Bloch Hessian.
To expose the shell-level Dirac conditions, we therefore consider a minimal enriched operator
acting on an internal complex doublet
\[
\Phi(x)\in \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]

\subsection{Minimal enriched periodic operator}

Let
\[
\mathcal H
=
h_0(-i\nabla)\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{\alpha=1}^{2}
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,(-i\partial_\alpha)\otimes s^\alpha,
\]
where:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(h_0(q)\) is a scalar bare kernel, e.g. Brazovskii-type,
\[
h_0(q)=r+(|q|^2-q_0^2)^2;
\]

\item \(U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\) is a real periodic scalar potential with BCC periodicity,
\[
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x+\mathbf R)=U_{\mathrm{per}}(x);
\]

\item \(\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\) are real periodic coefficients, also with BCC periodicity,
which encode the leading gradient-induced internal mixing;

\item \(s^\alpha\) are Pauli matrices acting on the internal doublet.
\end{enumerate}

The third term is the minimal enriched coupling that can produce internal traceless
mixing at first order in momentum.

\subsection{Shell basis at the Bragg point}

Let
\[
k_*=\frac{G_*}{2}
\]
be the Bragg quasimomentum associated with a chosen reciprocal-shell vector \(G_*\).

Assume that there is an internal periodic doublet basis
\[
u_a(x),\qquad a=1,2,
\]
orthonormal on the primitive cell:
\[
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\,u_a^\dagger(x)u_b(x)=\delta_{ab}.
\]

Define the shell-pair basis states
\[
\Psi_{+,a}(x)=e^{ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),
\qquad
\Psi_{-,a}(x)=e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),
\qquad a=1,2.
\]
These span the enriched shell space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{shell}}
=
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{pair}}\otimes\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]

\subsection{Projected shell Hamiltonian}

Define the projected Bloch Hamiltonian
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
\Pi_{\mathrm{shell}}\,
\mathcal H(k_*+p)\,
\Pi_{\mathrm{shell}},
\qquad
|p|\ll |k_*|.
\]
In the ordered basis
\[
(\Psi_{+,1},\Psi_{+,2},\Psi_{-,1},\Psi_{-,2}),
\]
it has the \(2\times2\) block form
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_+(p) & B(p)\\
B(p)^\dagger & E_-(p)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with \(E_\pm(p)\) and \(B(p)\) acting on the internal doublet.

\subsection{Constant pair-mixing mass \(B_0\)}

The constant pair-mixing block at \(p=0\) is
\[
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H(k_*)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle.
\]
For the operator above, the bare translation-invariant kernel does not contribute to
pair mixing, so the leading scalar contribution comes from \(U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\):
\[
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,
e^{-2ik_*\cdot x}\,
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\,
u_b(x)
+
(B_0^{\mathrm{grad}})_{ab}.
\]
If the gradient couplings \(\mathcal G_\alpha\) are odd under a shell-exchange symmetry
or vanish at \(p=0\), then
\[
B_0^{\mathrm{grad}}=0
\]
and the dominant constant Bragg mass is exactly the Fourier coefficient of
\(U_{\mathrm{per}}\) at wavevector \(2k_*=G_*\), projected onto the internal doublet.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Projected Bragg-mass formula).}
For the minimal enriched operator,
\[
\boxed{
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}\,
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\,
u_b(x)
}
\]
up to possible additional gradient-sector contributions that vanish if the first-order
mixing is odd and momentum-linear.

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
The shell crossing is massless if and only if this projected \(G_*\)-Fourier matrix vanishes
on the shell doublet:
\[
B_0=0.
\]

\subsection{Longitudinal pair splitting \(v_\parallel\)}

The pair-diagonal blocks are
\[
(E_+(p))_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H(k_*+p)\Psi_{+,b}\rangle,
\]
\[
(E_-(p))_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{-,a},\mathcal H(k_*+p)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle.
\]
The leading pair difference comes from the bare dispersion:
\[
h_0(k_*+p)-h_0(-k_*+p)
=
2\,\nabla h_0(k_*)\cdot p+O(|p|^3).
\]
Assuming the internal envelopes are the same in the two pair sectors, the pair-diagonal
difference is scalar in the internal space at leading order:
\[
E_+(p)-E_-(p)
=
2v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2 + O(|p|^2),
\]
with
\[
\boxed{
v_\parallel
=
\hat n\cdot \nabla h_0(k_*),
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
}
\]

\paragraph{For the Brazovskii kernel}
\[
h_0(q)=r+(|q|^2-q_0^2)^2,
\]
one gets
\[
\nabla h_0(q)=4(|q|^2-q_0^2)q,
\]
hence
\[
v_\parallel
=
4\left(\frac{|G_*|^2}{4}-q_0^2\right)\frac{|G_*|}{2}.
\]
Thus
\[
v_\parallel\neq0
\]
unless the Bragg point lies exactly on the bare shell \( |G_*|=2q_0 \).

\subsection{Transverse internal mixing matrices \(W^\alpha\)}

Now consider the first-order internal-mixing term
\[
\sum_{\alpha=1}^{2}
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,(-i\partial_\alpha)\otimes s^\alpha.
\]
Its projected pair-mixing derivative at \(p=0\) defines
\[
(W^\alpha)_{ab}
:=
\left.
\frac{\partial}{\partial p_\alpha}
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H(k_*+p)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle
\right|_{p=0}.
\]
Because
\[
(-i\partial_\alpha)e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_b(x)
=
(-k_{*,\alpha}-i\partial_\alpha) e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_b(x),
\]
one obtains
\[
\boxed{
(W^\alpha)_{ab}
=
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}\,
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,
u_b(x)\, s^\alpha
}
\]
up to basis-convention terms coming from derivatives of the periodic envelopes.
If the envelopes are chosen \(p\)-independently at leading order, this is the principal formula.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Projected transverse-mixing formula).}
The shell-level transverse internal mixing matrices are given, at principal order, by the
cell overlaps
\[
\boxed{
(W^\alpha)_{ab}
=
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}\,
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,
u_b(x)\, s^\alpha,
\qquad \alpha=1,2.
}
\]

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
The shell-level Dirac problem is therefore reduced to the question whether the projected
\(G_*\)-harmonics of the odd gradient couplings \(\mathcal G_1,\mathcal G_2\) generate two
independent traceless Hermitian matrices on the internal doublet.

\subsection{Projected shell Dirac criterion in overlap form}

We now collect the previous formulas.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Overlap-form shell Dirac criterion).}
Suppose:

\begin{enumerate}
\item[(i)] the shell basis
\[
\Psi_{\pm,a}(x)=e^{\pm ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),\qquad a=1,2,
\]
defines an isolated \(4\)-dimensional enriched shell block;

\item[(ii)] the projected Bragg mass vanishes,
\[
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}\,
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\,
u_b(x)=0
\qquad
\forall a,b;
\]

\item[(iii)] the longitudinal slope is nonzero,
\[
v_\parallel=\hat n\cdot \nabla h_0(k_*)\neq 0;
\]

\item[(iv)] the projected transverse matrices
\[
W^1,\qquad W^2
\]
defined by the cell overlaps above are traceless Hermitian, nonzero, and satisfy
\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0,
\qquad
(W^1)^2=\nu_1^2\mathbf 1,\qquad
(W^2)^2=\nu_2^2\mathbf 1,
\qquad
\nu_1\nu_2\neq0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the shell-projected Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent at principal order to
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
\sim
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\nu_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\nu_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2,
\]
hence is the massless Dirac principal operator.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Condition (ii) gives \(B_0=0\). Condition (iii) produces the pair-diagonal
\(\tau^3\otimes\mathbf1\) term. Condition (iv) allows internal unitary reduction
of \(W^1,W^2\) to Pauli form. The three resulting matrices mutually anticommute,
so the principal operator is Dirac. \(\square\)

\subsection{What this does and does not prove}

This theorem is strong because it converts the shell-level Dirac problem into explicit
cell integrals over a primitive BCC unit cell.

However, it still does not prove that the actual microscopic TECT Bloch Hessian satisfies
these conditions. The remaining microscopic tasks are now exactly:

\begin{enumerate}
\item construct the actual internal shell envelopes \(u_a(x)\);
\item compute the projected \(G_*\)-Fourier matrix of \(U_{\mathrm{per}}\) and test \(B_0=0\);
\item compute the projected gradient harmonics \(\mathcal G_\alpha\) and extract \(W^1,W^2\);
\item verify that the shell block is spectrally isolated.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the remaining gap is no longer conceptual: it is a finite set of explicit BCC cell-overlap
calculations.

\section{Final Summary}

For a minimal enriched periodic operator acting on an internal doublet,
the shell-level Dirac problem can be reduced entirely to projected cell-overlap integrals.

Using the shell basis
\[
\Psi_{+,a}(x)=e^{ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),
\qquad
\Psi_{-,a}(x)=e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),
\qquad
a=1,2,
\]
the projected shell Hamiltonian has block form
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_+(p) & B(p)\\
B(p)^\dagger & E_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The load-bearing quantities are:

\[
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,e^{-iG_*\cdot x}\,U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\,u_b(x),
\]

\[
v_\parallel=\hat n\cdot \nabla h_0(k_*),
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|},
\]

\[
(W^\alpha)_{ab}
=
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
u_a^\dagger(x)\,e^{-iG_*\cdot x}\,\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,u_b(x)\,s^\alpha,
\qquad \alpha=1,2.
\]

If

\[
B_0=0,\qquad v_\parallel\neq0,
\]
and the projected transverse matrices \(W^1,W^2\) are nonzero traceless Hermitian matrices
satisfying
\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0,
\]
then the shell-projected Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent at principal order to
\[
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\nu_1 p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\nu_2 p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2,
\]
which is the massless Dirac operator.

Therefore the microscopic BCC shell-Dirac problem is now reduced to a finite set of explicit
primitive-cell overlap computations.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{A Simplest Internal-Envelope Ansatz and Fourier Selection Rules for Shell-Level Dirac Emergence}

\subsection*{Goal}

We now evaluate the shell-level overlap quantities
\[
B_0,\qquad W^1,\qquad W^2
\]
in the simplest nontrivial enriched periodic ansatz.

The purpose is to determine whether the shell-level Dirac conditions can already
be reduced to explicit Fourier selection rules on the primitive BCC cell.

\subsection*{Setup}

We work with the minimal enriched periodic operator
\[
\mathcal H
=
h_0(-i\nabla)\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\otimes \mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{\alpha=1}^{2}
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,(-i\partial_\alpha)\otimes s^\alpha,
\]
acting on an internal complex doublet \(\Phi(x)\in\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}\).

The periodic BCC condensate background is
\[
\phi_0(x)=\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}} A_G e^{iG\cdot x},
\qquad
A_{-G}=\overline{A_G},
\]
with first reciprocal shell \(S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\).

Fix a shell vector \(G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\), and let
\[
k_*=\frac{G_*}{2}
\]
be the corresponding Bragg quasimomentum.

\subsection{Simplest internal periodic-envelope ansatz}

Choose a constant orthonormal internal basis
\[
\xi_1,\xi_2\in\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}},
\qquad
\xi_a^\dagger \xi_b=\delta_{ab},
\]
and define periodic envelopes
\[
u_1(x)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}}\,\xi_1,
\qquad
u_2(x)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}}\,\xi_2,
\]
where \(V_{\mathrm{cell}}\) is the primitive cell volume.

Then the shell basis states are
\[
\Psi_{+,a}(x)=e^{ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),
\qquad
\Psi_{-,a}(x)=e^{-ik_*\cdot x}u_a(x),
\qquad a=1,2.
\]

\paragraph{Remark.}
This is the simplest enriched shell ansatz. It ignores nontrivial periodic structure
of the internal Bloch envelopes, but has the advantage that the projected overlaps reduce
directly to primitive-cell Fourier coefficients.

\subsection{Projected Bragg mass \(B_0\)}

By definition,
\[
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H(k_*)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle.
\]
For the operator above, the translation-invariant bare kernel does not contribute to
pair mixing. Thus the leading scalar contribution is
\[
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\frac{1}{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
\xi_a^\dagger
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)
\xi_b.
\]
Since \(U_{\mathrm{per}}\) acts trivially on the internal doublet, this becomes
\[
(B_0)_{ab}
=
\widehat U(G_*)\,\delta_{ab},
\]
where
\[
\widehat U(G)
:=
\frac{1}{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
e^{-iG\cdot x}U_{\mathrm{per}}(x).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Constant-envelope Bragg-mass formula).}
For the constant internal-envelope ansatz,
\[
\boxed{
B_0=\widehat U(G_*)\,\mathbf 1_2.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from orthonormality of the constant internal basis and the fact that
\(U_{\mathrm{per}}\) is scalar in the internal sector. \(\square\)

\subsection{First-shell selection rule for a quadratic scalar potential}

Now suppose the periodic scalar potential is generated by the quadratic harmonic content
of the first-shell condensate, for example in a Brazovskii/\(\phi^4\) Hessian:
\[
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)=3u\,\phi_0(x)^2.
\]
Expand
\[
\phi_0(x)^2
=
\sum_{G,G'\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}} A_G A_{G'} e^{i(G+G')\cdot x}.
\]
Hence the Fourier support of \(U_{\mathrm{per}}\) lies in the set
\[
\{G+G' : G,G'\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\}.
\]
This contains:
\[
0,\qquad \pm 2Q_a,\qquad \pm(Q_a\pm Q_b),
\]
but generically does not contain a single first-shell vector \(G_*\) itself.

Therefore
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (First-shell vanishing of the scalar Bragg mass).}
If the periodic scalar potential is generated by a quadratic function of the
first-shell BCC condensate,
\[
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\propto \phi_0(x)^2,
\]
then its first-shell Fourier coefficient vanishes:
\[
\boxed{
\widehat U(G_*)=0.
}
\]
Consequently,
\[
\boxed{
B_0=0.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The Fourier support of \(\phi_0^2\) is given by pairwise sums of first-shell vectors.
A single first-shell vector \(G_*\) is not produced as such a sum in the generic
first-shell BCC harmonic set. Hence the \(G_*\)-Fourier coefficient vanishes. \(\square\)

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
This is a strong selection rule: in the simplest scalar periodic Hessian generated from
a first-shell condensate, the constant Bragg mass is removed automatically at the shell
crossing.

\subsection{Longitudinal pair splitting \(v_\parallel\)}

For the constant-envelope ansatz, the pair-diagonal slopes are inherited from the bare kernel:
\[
E_+(p)-E_-(p)=2v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2+O(|p|^2),
\]
with
\[
v_\parallel=\hat n\cdot \nabla h_0(k_*),
\qquad
\hat n=\frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]
For the Brazovskii kernel
\[
h_0(q)=r+(|q|^2-q_0^2)^2,
\]
this gives
\[
v_\parallel
=
4\left(\frac{|G_*|^2}{4}-q_0^2\right)\frac{|G_*|}{2}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Constant-envelope longitudinal slope).}
Under the constant-envelope ansatz,
\[
\boxed{
E_+(p)-E_-(p)=2v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\mathbf 1_2+O(|p|^2),
}
\]
with \(v_\parallel\) determined solely by the bare dispersion at the Bragg point.

\subsection{Projected transverse mixing matrices \(W^\alpha\)}

Now consider the enriched first-order couplings
\[
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\,(-i\partial_\alpha)\otimes s^\alpha,
\qquad
\alpha=1,2.
\]
Their projected pair-mixing derivatives define
\[
(W^\alpha)_{ab}
=
\left.
\frac{\partial}{\partial p_\alpha}
\langle \Psi_{+,a},\mathcal H(k_*+p)\Psi_{-,b}\rangle
\right|_{p=0}.
\]
For constant envelopes,
\[
(W^\alpha)_{ab}
=
\frac{1}{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
\xi_a^\dagger
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)
\xi_b\, s^\alpha.
\]
Thus
\[
W^\alpha=\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\,s^\alpha,
\]
where
\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G)
:=
\frac{1}{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}
\int_{\mathrm{cell}} d^3x\;
e^{-iG\cdot x}\mathcal G_\alpha(x).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Constant-envelope transverse-mixing formula).}
For the constant-envelope ansatz,
\[
\boxed{
W^\alpha=\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\,s^\alpha,
\qquad \alpha=1,2.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Again this follows from the constant internal envelopes and orthonormality of the
internal basis. \(\square\)

\subsection{When do \(W^1\) and \(W^2\) give Dirac?}

Because
\[
W^1=\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)\,s^1,
\qquad
W^2=\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)\,s^2,
\]
we automatically have
\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0
\]
whenever both coefficients are nonzero. Also
\[
(W^1)^2=\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)^2\,\mathbf 1,
\qquad
(W^2)^2=\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)^2\,\mathbf 1.
\]

Therefore the shell-level Dirac condition reduces simply to
\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)\neq 0,
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)\neq 0,
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
\widehat U(G_*)=0.
\]

\paragraph{Corollary 5 (Fourier-coefficient shell Dirac criterion).}
Under the constant-envelope ansatz, the shell-level massless Dirac operator emerges if
\[
\boxed{
\widehat U(G_*)=0,
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq0,
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)\neq0,
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)\neq0.
}
\]
In that case,
\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
\sim
v_\parallel p_\parallel\,\tau^3\otimes\mathbf 1
+
\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)\,p_1\,\tau^1\otimes s^1
+
\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)\,p_2\,\tau^1\otimes s^2.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is an immediate application of Theorems 1--4 and the projected shell Dirac theorem.
\(\square\)

\subsection{Microscopic interpretation of the gradient coefficients}

The coefficients \(\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\) should be thought of as the periodic part of the
linearized background-gradient coupling. In the simplest enriched model, they are generated by
first-shell harmonics or first derivatives of the BCC condensate, for example
\[
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\propto \partial_\alpha \phi_0(x)
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\propto \phi_0^{(1)}(x),
\]
where \(\phi_0^{(1)}\) denotes first-shell harmonic content.

Since \(\phi_0(x)\) itself contains first-shell harmonics,
\[
\phi_0(x)=\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}} A_G e^{iG\cdot x},
\]
a coefficient built linearly from \(\phi_0\) or its first derivatives generically has
\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\neq0.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 6 (Generic first-shell nonvanishing of linear gradient couplings).}
If \(\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\) is built linearly from the first-shell condensate harmonics or their
first derivatives, then generically
\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\neq0
\]
for \(G_*\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}\), unless forbidden by an additional symmetry or accidental
coefficient cancellation.

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
Thus, in the simplest enriched periodic ansatz, the dangerous constant Bragg mass vanishes by a
quadratic-harmonic selection rule, while the desired transverse Dirac couplings survive because
they arise linearly from first-shell gradient harmonics.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is proved here:

\begin{enumerate}
\item in the simplest constant-envelope enriched shell ansatz,
\[
B_0,\quad W^1,\quad W^2
\]
reduce to primitive-cell Fourier coefficients at \(G_*\);

\item a quadratic scalar potential generated from first-shell condensate harmonics satisfies
\[
B_0=0;
\]

\item linear gradient-like enriched couplings generically produce
\[
W^1\propto s^1,\qquad W^2\propto s^2,
\]
hence automatically satisfy the anticommuting internal condition.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item whether the actual microscopic TECT enriched Bloch Hessian can be reduced to this same
constant-envelope shell block;

\item whether the enriched couplings \(\mathcal G_\alpha(x)\) derived from the true microscopic
linearization have nonzero \(G_*\)-Fourier coefficients in the required directions;

\item whether the enriched shell block is spectrally isolated from the rest of the Bloch spectrum.
\end{enumerate}

Hence the shell-level Dirac problem is now reduced to a very explicit Fourier selection problem.

\section{Final Summary}

Using the simplest constant-envelope internal doublet ansatz
\[
u_1(x)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}}\xi_1,
\qquad
u_2(x)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{V_{\mathrm{cell}}}}\xi_2,
\]
the shell-level projected quantities reduce to primitive-cell Fourier coefficients:

\[
B_0=\widehat U(G_*)\,\mathbf 1_2,
\]
\[
W^\alpha=\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\,s^\alpha,
\qquad \alpha=1,2.
\]

If the scalar periodic potential is generated quadratically from the first-shell BCC condensate,
for example
\[
U_{\mathrm{per}}(x)\propto \phi_0(x)^2,
\]
then its \(G_*\)-Fourier coefficient vanishes:
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0,
\]
hence
\[
B_0=0.
\]

If, on the other hand, the enriched gradient couplings \(\mathcal G_1,\mathcal G_2\) are built
linearly from first-shell condensate harmonics or their gradients, then generically
\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)\neq0,
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)\neq0,
\]
and therefore
\[
W^1\propto s^1,\qquad W^2\propto s^2,
\]
which are automatically traceless, Hermitian, nonzero, and anticommuting.

Thus the shell-level massless Dirac criterion reduces simply to the Fourier-coefficient conditions
\[
\widehat U(G_*)=0,
\qquad
v_\parallel\neq0,
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_1}(G_*)\neq0,
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_2}(G_*)\neq0.
\]

Therefore the remaining microscopic BCC shell-Dirac problem is now sharply localized to an explicit
primitive-cell Fourier-overlap calculation.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Microscopic Origin of the Gradient Couplings}

\subsection{Goal}

The remaining condition in the shell-level Dirac criterion is that the transverse
mixing matrices satisfy

\[
W^\alpha \propto \widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\neq 0.
\]

We now show that such coefficients arise naturally from the gradient structure
of the BCC condensate.

\subsection{BCC condensate}

The first-shell BCC condensate is

\[
\phi_0(x)
=
2A\sum_{i=1}^{6}\cos(Q_i\cdot x)
\]

where

\[
Q_i\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}.
\]

In exponential form

\[
\phi_0(x)
=
A\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}}
e^{iG\cdot x}.
\]

\subsection{Gradient structure}

The gradient of the condensate is

\[
\nabla \phi_0(x)
=
-2A\sum_i Q_i\sin(Q_i\cdot x).
\]

Using

\[
\sin(Q\cdot x)
=
\frac{1}{2i}
(e^{iQ\cdot x}-e^{-iQ\cdot x}),
\]

we obtain

\[
\nabla\phi_0(x)
=
\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}}
c_G\,G\,e^{iG\cdot x},
\]

with coefficients

\[
c_G=\frac{A}{i}.
\]

Therefore

\[
\nabla\phi_0(x)
\]

contains **exactly the same first-shell Fourier harmonics**.

\subsection{Minimal enriched gradient coupling}

Consider the minimal internal coupling

\[
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)
=
\lambda\,\partial_\alpha\phi_0(x).
\]

Then

\[
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)
=
\lambda\sum_{G\in S_{\mathrm{BCC}}}
c_G\,G_\alpha\,e^{iG\cdot x}.
\]

The Fourier coefficient at \(G_*\) is therefore

\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)
=
\lambda\,c_{G_*}\,G_{*,\alpha}.
\]

Hence

\[
\boxed{
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)
=
\frac{\lambda A}{i}\,G_{*,\alpha}
}
\]

and this is generically nonzero.

\subsection{Transverse mixing matrices}

From the overlap formula derived previously,

\[
W^\alpha
=
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\,s^\alpha
\]

so

\[
W^\alpha
=
\frac{\lambda A}{i}\,G_{*,\alpha}\,s^\alpha.
\]

Therefore

\[
W^1\neq0,
\qquad
W^2\neq0
\]

whenever the shell vector has nonzero components in those directions.

\subsection{Anticommutation}

Since

\[
W^1\propto s^1,
\qquad
W^2\propto s^2,
\]

we have

\[
\{W^1,W^2\}=0.
\]

Thus the Dirac anticommutation relations follow automatically.

\subsection{Result}

The microscopic gradient structure of the BCC condensate automatically produces
nonzero transverse Dirac couplings at the Bragg shell.

\section{Result: Microscopic Origin of Dirac Transverse Couplings}

If the enriched internal coupling is generated from the condensate gradient,

\[
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)=\lambda\,\partial_\alpha\phi_0(x),
\]

then the Fourier coefficient at the shell vector is

\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)
=
\frac{\lambda A}{i}G_{*,\alpha}.
\]

Hence

\[
W^\alpha
=
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\,s^\alpha
\]

is generically nonzero.

Together with the previously established selection rule

\[
B_0=0
\]

and the generic longitudinal slope

\[
v_\parallel\neq0,
\]

the shell-level projected Hamiltonian reduces to

\[
H_{\mathrm{proj}}(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel\tau^3
+
\nu_1 p_1\tau^1 s^1
+
\nu_2 p_2\tau^1 s^2
\]

which is the massless Dirac operator.

Therefore the microscopic gradient structure of the BCC condensate naturally
produces the transverse couplings required for Dirac emergence.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Corrected Fourier-Support Theorem for the Enriched Gradient Couplings}

\subsection*{Why a correction is needed}

In the previous heuristic argument, one might be tempted to write
\[
\mathcal G_\alpha(x)=\lambda\,\partial_\alpha \phi_0(x)
\]
and then conclude immediately that
\[
\widehat{\mathcal G_\alpha}(G_*)\neq 0.
\]
This is too strong as stated.

The reason is that, in the shell-level Dirac reduction, the labels
\(\alpha=1,2\) naturally denote the two momentum directions transverse to the shell vector \(G_*\).
If one literally identifies \(\partial_\alpha\) with differentiation along those transverse
directions, then
\[
\widehat{\partial_\alpha \phi_0}(G_*)
\propto G_*\cdot e_\alpha = 0,
\]
because \(e_\alpha\perp G_*\).

Therefore the correct statement is not that the needed transverse couplings arise automatically
from \(\partial_\alpha\phi_0\) alone, but rather that any enriched coupling which is \emph{linear}
